The Three Parts of a Proposition and the Copula Debate
Author defines the three parts of a proposition and the copula's signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and judgmental relation in any proposition, and distinguish between the two views on what the copula signifies.

Core Idea
A proposition is a judgmental relation expressed by a copula, whose meaning divides logicians over relation vs. judgment.

The passage from the Tadhheeb sharh explains the essential anatomy of any proposition: the subject (maḥkūm ʿalayhi), the predicate (maḥkūm bihi), and the judgmental relation (nisba ḥukmiyya) that connects them. This relation must be expressed by a linguistic copula (rābiṭa). But what exactly does that copula signify? The commentary expands on a critical debate: does the copula denote the relation itself, or the act of judgment? This distinction has real consequences for how we analyze doubtful statements. Let's walk through the analysis step by step.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three parts in a proposition
Take a simple proposition like 'Zaid is standing'. Identify the subject (Zaid), predicate (standing), and the judgmental relation (expressed by 'is'). Note how each requires a distinct word.
The subject is what the judgment is about; the predicate is what is judged of it; the relation is the connection.
2
Understand why a copula is necessary
The author argues that just as the two extremes need words, the relation also needs a word. This is the copula. Without it, the proposition would be incomplete or ambiguous.
In Arabic, the copula can be omitted, but the passage stresses it must be expressible.
3
Contrast the two views on copula signification
One view: the copula signifies the relation (subsistence of predicate for subject). Second view: the copula signifies the judgment itself (the act of affirming or denying). The verified view (al-tahqeeq) is that it signifies the judgment interpreted as action or passion.
The passage mentions that if it signifies the judgment, then the copula is absent in doubt.
4
Test the implications on a doubtful proposition
Consider 'Zaid might be standing'. According to the first view, since there is still a relation (though uncertain), there is a copula. According to the second view, because there is no firm judgment, there is no copula. This shows the practical impact of the two positions.
The term 'doubtful proposition' (al-qaḍiyya al-mashkūka) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّه وُضِعَ ليُحمَلَ عليه. الثَّاني : المحكوم به ، (وَ) يسمَّى (المحْكُومُ بِهِ مَحمُولاً)؛ لحمله على الأول. الثَّالث : النِّسبةُ الحكميَّة بينهما ، وبها يرتبط الثاني بالأوَّل، وكما أنَّ من حق المحكوم عليه وبه أن يعبر عنهُمَا بِلَفظَين؛ كذلكَ مِنْ حقِّ النِّسبة الحكميَّةِ أنْ يعبر عنها بلفظٍ؛ وهو الرابطة. وقولُهُ بَعْدُ : (من حقِّ النّسبة الحكميَّةِ أنْ يعبر ... إلخ)، هذا يقتضي أن يكونَ مدلوله هو النُّبوتُ الَّذي هو مدلولُ النِّسبةِ الحكميَّةِ ، والتَّحقيقُ : أنَّ مدلولَهُ الحكمُ المفسر على القولين فيما تقدَّم بالفعل أو الانفعال، إلَّا أَنْ يُقالَ: الحكميَّةُ نسبةً إلى الحكم من نسبةِ الشَّيء إلى نَفْسِهِ، وهي جائزة. (قَوْلُهُ : النِّسْبَةُ الْحُكْمِيَّةُ بَيْنَهُمَا ) أي : وهي النِّسبةُ الكلامية الرابطةُ بينَ الطَّرفين، وقوله : الحكميَّة؛ أي : المنسوبة للحكم من نسبة المتعلَّقِ للمتعلّق . وظاهره : أنَّ اللفظ المسمَّى بالرابطة؛ مدلوله النّسبة الكلاميَّة؛ أعني : ثبوت المحمول للموضوع ، وقيل : إِنَّ مَدلولَهُ الحُكْم . وعلى هذا ؛ فالقضيَّةُ المشكوكة لا رابطة فيها، بخلافِهِ على الأوّل. هذا ؛ وقرَّرَ سيّدي مُحمَّد الصَّغير على قول المختصر ونسبة بينهما، ويُسمَّى اللَّفْظُ الدَّالُ عليها ... إلخ، مَا نَصُّهُ : «أي : النِّسبة الإيقاعيَّةُ؛ لَا مُطلقُ النِّسبةِ الَّتِي هي تَعلُّقُ أحدِ الطَّرفين بالآخر». والحاصلُ : أنَّ ذاتَ الموضوع وذات المحمولِ مُقدَّمانِ على الحكم، ولكنْ لَا يوصفانٍ بكونهما محكوماً عليهِ وَبِهِ إلَّا بعد الحكم الذي هو الإيقاع، أو الانتزاع، أو بعد إدراكِ الوقوع واللا وقوع على أنَّه انفعال. انتهى بخط شيخنا. (قَوْلُهُ : بِلَفْظَيْن) أي : كَـ : لفظ زيد»، و«لفظ قائم» في : زيد قائم. (قَوْلُهُ : والتحقيق ... إلخ) بل التحقيق أن المجعول جزءاً من القضية هو النسبة الحكمية التي هي مورد الإيجاب والنفي، وأما الحكم بمعنى إذعان النسبة؛ أي: انتقاشها في النفس، أو حصولها فيها على أنَّه انفعال أو فعل، فلا يصح أن يكون جزءاً منها؛ لأنه وصف للشخص لا القضية، فكيف يجعل جزءاً منها ؟! ا.هـ.
Because it is placed to be predicated upon it. The second: that which is judged by it, and it is called the predicate because it is predicated upon the first. The third: the judgmental relation between them, and by it the second is connected to the first. And just as it is the right of the subject and predicate to be expressed by two words, so it is the right of the judgmental relation to be expressed by a word, which is the copula. And his statement later: 'It is the right of the judgmental relation to be expressed...' requires that its signification is the affirmation which is the signification of the judgmental relation. But the verified view: that its signification is the judgment interpreted according to the two opinions previously mentioned as action or passion, unless it is said: 'judgmental' is a relation to the judgment in the sense of attributing the thing to itself, which is permissible. His statement: 'The judgmental relation between them' i.e., it is the verbal relation connecting the two extremes. His statement: 'judgmental' i.e., attributed to judgment in the sense of attributing the related to the related. Its apparent meaning: that the word called the copula has as its signification the verbal relation, namely the subsistence of the predicate for the subject. And it is said: its signification is the judgment. On this view, the doubtful proposition has no copula, unlike the first view. This, and Sayyidi Muhammad al-Saghir settled on the statement of the author: 'And a relation between them, and the word indicating it is called...' his text: 'i.e., the relational relation, not the absolute relation which is the attachment of one extreme to the other.' The conclusion: that the essence of the subject and the essence of the predicate are prior to the judgment, but they are not described as judged upon and by it except after the judgment, which is the affirmation or negation, or after the perception of occurrence and non-occurrence as a passion. End of the line of our teacher. His statement: 'by two words' i.e., like the word 'Zaid' and the word 'standing' in 'Zaid is standing.' His statement: 'And the verification...' Rather, the verification is that what is made a part of the proposition is the judgmental relation which is the locus of affirmation and negation. As for the judgment in the sense of assenting to the relation, i.e., its impression in the soul, or its occurrence in it as a passion or action, it cannot be a part of it because it is a description of the person, not the proposition. So how can it be made a part of it? End quote.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage defines the three essential parts of a proposition (al-qadiyya): the subject (المحكوم عليه or الموضوع), the predicate (المحكوم به or المحمول), and the judgmental relation (النسبة الحكمية) that binds them. The author emphasizes that just as the subject and predicate require distinct words, the relation requires a word as well, which is the copula (الرابطة). The commentary then delves into a dispute: Does the copula signify the relation itself (i.e., the connectedness, as in the subsistence of the predicate for the subject) or does it signify the judgment (the act of assenting or denying)? On the first view, even a doubtful proposition (al-qadiyya al-mashkuka) contains a copula because it still has a relation, albeit uncertain. On the second view, since doubt means no judgment, such a proposition lacks a copula. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that the 'judgmental relation' is the relational relation (al-nisba al-iqa'iyya), not mere attachment. The final analysis distinguishes between the essence of subject/predicate, which exist before judgment, and their description as 'judged upon' and 'judged by', which only occurs after judgment is made. The marginal note from al-Atar insists that what is truly part of the proposition is the judgmental relation as the locus of affirmation/negation, not the psychological act of judgment itself, which is a description of the person.

Key Terms
المحمول
النسبة الحكمية
الرابطة
القضية المشكوكة
الإيقاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the verified view (al-tahqeeq) mentioned in the passage, what does the copula signify?
A) The mere attachment of subject and predicate regardless of judgment.
B) The judgment interpreted as action or passion (al-fil wa al-infia'l).
C) The verbal relation of subsistence (thuboot) only.
D) The psychological impression of assent in the soul.

Key Takeaway
The copula is the linguistic sign of the judgmental relation, and whether it signifies the relation or the judgment itself determines how we treat doubtful propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition from a kalam text: 'The world is temporally originated.' Identify its subject, predicate, and copula. Then, explain what the copula signifies according to each of the two views discussed in the passage. Finally, determine whether the doubtful proposition 'The world might be eternal' would have a copula according to each view.

The Copula: Its Definition and Expression
Author defines the copula and discusses its linguistic expression and differing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in a proposition and differentiate between its expression as a word, compositional form, or movement, and explain the rationale for each view.

Core Idea
The copula is the glue that holds a proposition together, and its form reveals how scholars debated linguistic and logical elegance.

This passage introduces the third element of a proposition: the copula (rabitah). It explains that just as the subject and predicate are expressed by words, the copula should also have an expression. However, a debate arises: must it always be a separate word, or can it be a form or movement? This lesson unpacks that debate and clarifies what the copula actually indicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of a proposition
Find the subject (mahkum 'alayhi), predicate (mahkum bihi), and the copula (nisbah hukmiyyah) as mentioned in the first sentence. Write them down.
The copula is the relative judgment that connects the predicate to the subject.
2
Understand the requirement for expressing the copula
The author says it is right that the copula be expressed by a word. Why? Because the subject and predicate are expressed by words. But is this necessary? Consider the principle of equality among the three parts.
The phrase 'by a word' is justified by the need for uniformity (taswiyah).
3
Examine the objection by al-'Isam
Al-'Isam argues that the copula could be expressed by a compositional form (hay'ah tarkibiyyah) or movement (harakah) rather than a word. He says this is more appropriate because the copula is a state inherent in the subject and predicate.
A movement like a vowel ending can serve as the copula in Arabic.
4
Analyze the response to the objection
The response says that saying 'by a word' is intended for the sake of equality among the three parts—that is, all three are words. This means the 'word' requirement is not absolute but a preference for uniformity.
The defense preserves the author's original formulation while allowing other forms.
5
Clarify what the copula primarily indicates
The passage states that 'rabitah' refers to the relation of occurrence or non-occurrence (wujoob/lawujoob), i.e., affirmation or negation, not the relation that is the locus of affirmation/negation. Identify which one the copula primarily indicates.
The copula is the act of connecting, not the content being connected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: النسبة الحكمية بينهما، وبها يرتبط الثاني بالأول، وكما أن من حق المحكوم عليه وبه أن يعبر عنهما بلفظين؛ كذلك من حق النسبة الحكمية أن يعبر عنها بلفظ دال عليها. ذلك اللفظ الدال على النسبة الرابطة؛ تسمية للدال باسم المدلول. وقوله: (أن يعبر عنها بلفظ فيه بحث؛ لأن حقها أن يعبر عنها بدال؛ سواء كان لفظاً أو هيئة تركيبيّة أو حركة، بل كونها هيئة قائمة بالدال على المحكوم عليه والدال على المحكوم به؛ أحق وأولى؛ لمزيد مناسبة بينه وبين مدلوله؛ إذ مدلوله حالة قائمة بالمحكوم عليه والمحكوم به؛ قاله العصام. وقد يجاب بأن معنى قوله: (أن يعبر عنها بلفظ)؛ أي: لأجل التسوية بين الأجزاء الثلاثة؛ أي: مقتضى التسوية ذلك. وقوله: (الرابطة) في التوصيف إشارة إلى أن المراد النسبة بمعنى الوقوع واللا وقوع الذي هو الإيجاب والسلب الرابط على التحقيق؛ لا النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب، وإن كانت الرابطة تدل عليها أيضاً بالالتزام.
The third [element]: the relative judgment between them [subject and predicate], and by it the second is connected to the first. And just as it is right that the subject and predicate be expressed by two words, likewise it is right that the relative judgment be expressed by a word indicating it. That word indicating the copular relation is called 'rabitah' (copula) — a naming of the signifier by the name of the signified. And his saying: 'that it be expressed by a word' is debatable, because it should be expressed by any indicator, whether a word, a compositional form, or a movement — rather, it being a form inherent in the indicator of the subject and the indicator of the predicate is more appropriate and suitable due to greater compatibility between it and its signification, since its signification is a state inherent in the subject and predicate; said by al-'Isam. And it may be answered that his meaning in saying 'by a word' is for the sake of equality among the three parts; i.e., that is what equality requires. And his saying: 'the rabitah' in the description indicates that the intended meaning of 'relative judgment' is the meaning of occurrence and non-occurrence which is affirmative and negative copular relation in reality, not the relation which is the locus of affirmation and negation, even though the copula also indicates it by entailment.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage defines the third element of a proposition, the copula (rabitah), after subject and predicate. The author begins by stating that the copula is the relative judgment (al-nisbah al-hukmiyyah) between the subject and predicate, and it is what connects the predicate to the subject. Just as the subject and predicate are expressed by two separate words, so too the relative judgment should be expressed by a word that indicates it. That word is called the 'rabitah' (copula), though this is a naming of the signifier (the word) by the name of the signified (the relation). The commentator then notes a debate: some scholars argue that the relative judgment should not necessarily be expressed by a word alone; it could be expressed by a compositional form (hay'ah tarkibiyyah) or even a movement (harakah). Al-'Isam argues that the most appropriate is the compositional form inherent in the subject and predicate words, because the signification (the relative judgment) is itself a state that inheres in the subject and predicate. The response is that the phrase 'by a word' is used for the sake of equality among the three parts (subject, predicate, copula) all being words. Finally, the passage clarifies that the term 'rabitah' in this context specifically refers to the relation of occurrence or non-occurrence (wujoob/lawujoob), i.e., affirmative or negative copular relation in reality, not the relation that is the locus of affirmation and negation—though the copula may also indicate that latter relation by entailment (iltizam).

Key Terms
النسبة الحكمية (al-nisbah al-hukmiyyah): The relative judgment between subject and predicate that connects them in a proposition; the copular relation. [Note: Stable across the tradition; some earlier texts may use 'nisbah' alone without specifying 'hukmiyyah'.]
الرابطة (al-rabitah): The linguistic term (word or form) that indicates the relative judgment, i.e., the copula. [Note: In later logic, 'rabitah' often refers to the word itself; here it includes any indicator (word, form, movement).]
التسوية بين الأجزاء الثلاثة (al-taswiyah bayn al-ajza' al-thalatha): The principle that subject, predicate, and copula should all be expressed by words for uniformity. [Note: This is a specific justification used to defend the word requirement; not a universal rule.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'rabitah' primarily indicate?
A) The subject of the proposition
B) The predicate of the proposition
C) The relative judgment of occurrence or non-occurrence (affirmation/negation)
D) The locus of affirmation and negation

Key Takeaway
The copula (rabitah) is the relative judgment that connects subject and predicate; it can be expressed by a word, form, or movement, but ultimately it indicates the affirmative or negative copular relation, not the content of that relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the Arabic proposition 'العالم حادث' ('The world is temporally originated'). Identify the subject, predicate, and copula. What indicates the copula in this proposition — a word, a form, or a movement? Explain using the concepts from this lesson.
?

The Copula Beyond 'Huwa' and 'Kaana'
Author states and illustrates that many things serve as the copula beyond 'huwa' and 'kaana'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify different types of copula and explain that the copula is not limited to the words 'huwa' and 'kaana'.

Core Idea
Any indicator of the relation between subject and predicate is a copula.

In classical Arabic logic, the copula is often identified with the words 'huwa' (he) or 'kaana' (was). However, this passage expands that view, showing that the copula can be a vowel, a foreign word, or even implicit. Understanding this flexibility is essential for analyzing propositions in Arabic and other languages, especially when dealing with definitions and logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the passage's main claim
Read the first sentence: the copula is not restricted to 'huwa' and 'kaana'. What is the author arguing against?
The common assumption in logic texts is that the copula is a specific word.
2
Recognize the examples provided
Note the three explicit examples: the kasra vowel in 'Zaydun Dabīrun', the Persian 'ast' in 'Zaydun qā'imun ast', and the Greek 'bood' and 'astīn' mentioned later. What do these have in common?
All serve to link subject and predicate, but none are 'huwa' or 'kaana'.
3
Understand the Arabic context
The commentator al-`Attar emphasizes that in Arabic, 'kaana' and 'huwa' do not inherently indicate relation; the relation is understood through i'rab and context. Why does this matter for logic?
In Arabic, the copula is often omitted, and the relation is inferred from case endings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ واعلَمْ أَنَّ الرَّابطة لا تنحصر في لفظة «هو» و«كان»، بل كلُّ ما يدلُّ على الربط فهو رابطة كحركة الكسر في نحو: «زيد دبير»، و«أست» في نحو: زيد قائم أست»، وغير ذلك مما يدلّ على الربط . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : كَحَرَكَةِ الْكَسْرِ) من إضافة العام للخاص، فهي للبيان؛ أي: كسرةُ الرَّاء في دبير في المثال الآتي. (قَوْلُهُ : زَيْدٌ دِبير ) أي : كاتب - وهو بكسر الراء ـ والحركة غير زمانية . (قَوْلُهُ: وَهَشت) عطف على حركة الكشر - وهو بفتح الهاء وسكون السين آخره تاء مثناة - لفظ يوناني (۱) معناه : هو . - (۳) (قَوْلُهُ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ أست) أي : (هو) (۲) ، وكان الأنسب أن يجعلَها مُتوسّطةً بينهما . (قَوْلُهُ : وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الرَّبْطِ أي : مثلُ بَود - بفتح الباء الموحدة معناه باليونانية ،كان ومثل أستين، ومعناه باليونانية : هو . واعلم أنَّ لفظ كان وهو وغيرهما ؛ لا دلالة لها على النسبة فى اللغة العربيَّةِ، وَلَا مُستعملة فيها ، فَلَمْ يُوضع للنّسبةِ لفظ يدلّ عليها في تلك اللغة، فإذا سمعْتَ: (زيد قائمٌ ؛ فَهِمْتَ ثبوت القيام لزيد، فإن أتيتَ بِكانَ أو هو ؛ كانَ ذلكَ غيرَ مُفيد شيئاً
Chapter: On al-ma'rif and its sections. Know that the copula is not restricted to the word 'huwa' and 'kaana', but rather everything that indicates relation is a copula, like the kasra vowel in 'Zaydun Dabīrun', and 'ast' in 'Zaydun qā'imun ast', and other things that indicate relation. Al-Dassuqi: His saying 'like the kasra vowel' is an addition of the general to the specific, so it is explanatory; i.e., the kasra of the ra in Dabīr in the following example. His saying 'Zaydun Dabīr' means a writer, and it is with kasra of the ra, and the vowel is non-temporal. His saying 'wahasht' is coordinated with 'kasra vowel' – it is with fatha of the ha and sukun of the sin, ending with tā marbūta, a Greek word meaning 'huwa'. His saying 'Zaydun qā'imun ast' means 'huwa', and it would have been more appropriate to place it between them. His saying 'and other things that indicate relation' such as 'bood' – with fatha of the bā, meaning in Greek 'kaana', and 'astīn' meaning in Greek 'huwa'. Know that the words 'kaana', 'huwa', and others have no indication of relation in the Arabic language, nor are they used in it, so no word was designated for relation in that language. So if you hear 'Zaydun qā'imun', you understand the existence of standing for Zayd. If you bring 'kaana' or 'huwa', that would add nothing useful.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage begins with a new chapter heading, likely dealing with the definition of the copula (raabitah) and its types, though the heading 'al-ma'rif' may be a corruption or alternate term. The author clarifies a key point: the copula is not limited to the Arabic words 'huwa' (he) and 'kaana' (was). Rather, any sign that indicates the relation between subject and predicate qualifies as a copula. The author provides examples: the kasra vowel (i.e., the vowel 'i') on the last letter of 'Dabīr' in the phrase 'Zaydun Dabīr' (Zayd is a writer) where the kasra on the 'r' indicates the relation. Another example is the Persian word 'ast' meaning 'is' in the phrase 'Zaydun qā'imun ast'. The commentator al-Dassuqi then explains these examples: 'Dabīr' means writer, and the kasra is the vowel; 'ast' is a Persian word equivalent to 'huwa'; 'bood' (with vowel pattern) means 'kaana'. The commentator al-`Attar adds that in Arabic, the words 'kaana' and 'huwa' have no inherent relation-indicating function and are not used for that purpose. No single word in Arabic was coined solely to express the relation; rather, the relation is understood through the context and i'rab. Thus, bringing 'kaana' or 'huwa' does not add anything essential. This passage emphasizes that the copula can be non-verbal (e.g., a vowel) and can come from other languages (e.g., Persian).

Key Terms
رابطة
دبير
أست

A) Kasra vowel
B) 'Ast' (Persian)
C) 'Bood' (Greek)
D) 'Bismillah'

Key Takeaway
The copula is any sign of the relation between subject and predicate, not limited to 'huwa' or 'kaana'; it can be a vowel, foreign word, or even implicit.

The Definition Requires Existence of Its Subject
Opening of a new chapter on definition (ma'rif) and its divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a definition (muʿarrif) entails the existence of its subject and its individuals.

Core Idea
A definition is not a mere verbal exercise; it presupposes the existence of what it defines.

This chapter opens with a concise but profound claim: a definition requires the existence of its subject. Al-Desouki clarifies that this means the definition entails the existence of the subject and its individuals. What does it mean for a definition to 'require existence'? This lesson unpacks that foundational principle in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter and its subject
The passage opens a new chapter titled 'On the definition and its divisions' (فصل: في المعرف وأقسامه). The term 'al-muʿarrif' here means 'the definition' — a statement that identifies the essence or nature of a thing. Notice that the chapter immediately raises a precondition: the definition requires the existence of the subject.
2
Locate the key lemma and its commentary
The matn says: 'تستدعي وجود الموضوع' — 'It requires the existence of the subject.' The commentator Al-Desouki explains: 'أي: تستلزم وجود الموضوع؛ أي: وجود أفراده' — 'it entails the existence of the subject, i.e., the existence of its individuals.' This tells us that the requirement is not just conceptual existence, but actual existence of individuals of the defined thing.
The lemma may have been part of a previous discussion; it is included here as the opening statement of the chapter.
3
Understand the logical implication
Think about why a definition would need its subject to exist. In Islamic logic, definitions are not arbitrary; they correspond to real things. If you are defining 'human', for example, the definition must apply to actually existing humans. If no humans existed, the definition would be empty. Thus, a valid definition presupposes that its subject has instances in reality.
This does not mean the subject must be externally real in every case; some definitions may be of mental concepts. The condition is that the subject must have some kind of existence — at least conceptual.
4
Connect to broader Islamic sciences
Consider how this principle applies in fiqh: defining 'salah' presupposes that salah exists as an act of worship. In kalam: defining 'God' presupposes that God exists. The logical rule ensures that definitions are grounded and not merely linguistic games.
This connects to the later distinction between hadd haqiqi (real definition) and hadd lafzi (verbal definition).

Al-Desouki's commentary explains that this means the definition entails the existence of the subject, i.e., the existence of its individuals. This sets the stage for a discussion on the nature and conditions of definitions in logic, likely addressing whether definitions require real existence or merely conceptual existence.

External, Real, and Mental Propositions
Author defines three types of affirmative propositions based on subject existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any affirmative proposition as external, real, or mental based on the mode of existence of its subject.

Core Idea
The same proposition can be external, real, or mental depending on how the subject's existence is considered.

This passage from the Tadhheeb introduces a foundational classification in Islamic logic: every affirmative proposition requires the subject to have some mode of existence. The three modes determine whether we call the proposition 'external', 'real', or 'mental'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing how propositions are used in Islamic sciences like kalaam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh, where the same wording may carry different logical force depending on the intended mode.

Guided Path
1
Understand the necessary condition for an affirmative proposition
The matn states that every affirmative proposition (mujiba) requires existence (wujud) of the subject. This existence can be of three kinds: muhqaqqan (actually realized), muqaddaran (estimated), or dhihnan (mental).
This condition does not apply to negative propositions in the same way.
2
Distinguish the three modes of existence
Muhqaqqan means the subject actually exists externally at some time. Muqaddaran means the subject is considered as if it existed, including both actual and non-existent subjects. Dhihnan means the subject exists only in the mind, like an imaginary being.
The passage clarifies that muqaddar is not opposed to muhqaqq; it includes the actual.
3
Identify each type of proposition
When the subject is muhqaqqan, the proposition is called 'external' (kharijiyya). When the subject is muqaddaran, it is 'real' (haqiqiyya). When the subject is dhihnan, it is 'mental' (dhihniyya).
The real proposition is the most commonly used in sciences.
4
Grasp the nuance: external does not require temporal simultaneity
The commentary explains that for an external proposition, the predicate need not hold at the moment of judgment. Example: 'Every sleeper is awake' is true if he will wake up later, even if not awake now. This is because the judgment concerns predication at some time, not the mental act.
This removes a common misconception.
5
Apply the classification to Islamic examples
Test with: 'Every human is an animal'. The subject 'human' exists externally, so it is both external and real (since real includes actual). 'Every angel is obedient' is real (angels exist, but the proposition treats them as a kind). 'Every phoenix is a bird' is mental (phoenix exists only in mind).
The same proposition can be both external and real if the subject actually exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
وَلَا بُدَّ فِي الموجِبَةِ مِنْ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ، إمَّا مُحَقِّقَاً وهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً فَالْحَقِيقِيَّةُ، أَوْ ذِهْنَاً فَالذِّهْنِيَّةُ
(قَوْلُهُ : لِعَدَمِ إِمْكَانِ التَّقْدِيرِ ) أي : الفرض؛ أي: لعدم إمكانه إمكاناً صحيحاً، وإلا فالتقدير ممكن، ولو قالَ : لاستحالة وجودِهَا ؛ لكان أحسن
(قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّها مَوْجُوْدَةٌ) أي: هي موجودةٌ .
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً) ليس المراد بالمقدَّرِ ما يُبايِنُ المحقق، بل ما يشمله ويشمل المعدومَ
(قَوْلُهُ: وَهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ) أي : تُسمَّى بذلك منسوبةٌ لِلخارج أي : ما هو خارجٌ عَنِ المشاعرِ والقُوى الدَّاركةِ؛ لأنَّ موضوعها اعتُبِرَ اتِّصافه بالمحمول خارجاً، قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة : سواء كانَ اتِّصافُهُ بالعنوان حالَ الحكم أو قبلَهُ أو بعدَهُ؛ حتَّى يصدقَ : كُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ، وإنْ لم يكن اتِّصافُهُ بالنَّائم حال ثبوتِ اليقظة؛ فالمراد بالحكمِ هَهُنَا ثبوتُ المحمول للموضُوع أو انتفاؤُهُ عنه؛ لا حكمُ العقل بذلك؛ لأنَّ هذا الكلامَ إنَّما هو لِرَفْعِ تَوَهُم مَنْ ظَنَّ أَنَّ الذَّاتَ يجبُ اتِّصافُهُ بوصفِ الموضوعِ حالَ اتصافه بالمحمول، وهُوَ الَّذي يُسمّيهِ القومُ حالَ اعتبار الحكم، وإلا فَفِي حال حكم العقل لا يجب وجود الموضوع في الخارج؛ فضلاً عن انصافه بالعنوان؛ لِصدقِ قولنا : زيد موجود أمس أو غداً.
وقال الهروي : لا يخفى أنَّهُ إذا كانَ المحمول فعلاً أو مُشتقا أو مصدراً؛ يجب أن يكونَ الذَّاتُ مُتَّصِفَاً بالعنوانِ حينَ ثبوتِ المحمول بحسب قواعدِ اللُّغةِ، وكُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ لا يصح بحسب حقيقةِ اللُّغةِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ
(قَوْلُهُ : فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ) سُمِّيَتْ بذلك؛ لأنَّها حقيقةُ القضيَّة المستعملة في العلوم لكثرة استعمالها بهذا الاعتبار، فهو من قبيل نسبةِ الشَّيء إلى مفهومِهِ الَّذي هو كَالحقيقة له، والذهنيَّةُ سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا وجود لموضوعِهَا إِلَّا في الذهنِ
Chapter: On the Definite and Its Divisions. It is necessary in the affirmative [proposition] that the subject have existence: either actually realized, and it is the external [proposition]; or estimated, and it is the real [proposition]; or mentally, and it is the mental [proposition]. (His statement: 'For the impossibility of estimation') i.e., assumption; i.e., because it is not possible in a sound way, otherwise estimation is possible, and if he had said 'due to impossibility of its existence' it would be better. (His statement: 'But it exists') i.e., it does exist. (His statement: 'Or estimated') What is meant by 'estimated' is not that which is distinct from the realized, but rather that which includes it and includes the non-existent. (His statement: 'And it is the external') i.e., it is so named relative to the external, i.e., what is outside the senses and cognitive faculties; because its subject is considered to be described by the predicate externally. The author said in his commentary on the Epistle: Whether its description [by the predicate] is at the time of judgment or before it or after it; so that it is true to say: 'Every sleeper is awake', even if he is not described as sleeping at the time of wakefulness. What is meant by 'judgment' here is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation; not the mental judgment; because this speech is only to remove the misconception of one who thought that the essence must be described by the attribute of the subject at the time of being described by the predicate, which the scholars call 'the state of considering the judgment'. Otherwise, at the time of mental judgment the existence of the subject in the external is not necessary, let alone its being described by the title; because it is true to say: 'Zayd existed yesterday or will exist tomorrow.' And al-Harawi said: It is not hidden that if the predicate is a verb or a derivative or a verbal noun, the essence must be described by the title at the time of the predicate's affirmation according to the rules of language; and 'Every sleeper is awake' is not valid according to the reality of language; reflect. (His statement: 'the real') It was so named because it is the real proposition used in the sciences due to its frequent usage with this consideration; it is like relating a thing to its concept which is like its reality. And the mental was so named because its subject has no existence except in the mind.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of affirmative propositions based on the mode of existence of the subject. The matn states that every affirmative proposition requires the subject to exist in one of three ways: actually realized (muhqiqan) giving the external proposition (kharijiyya), estimated (muqaddaran) giving the real proposition (haqiqiyya), or mentally (dhihnan) giving the mental proposition (dhihniyya). The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'estimated' here does not mean a proper estimation but rather a consideration of possible existence, including both actual and non-existent subjects. Al-Matar explains the external proposition: the subject's description by the predicate is considered externally, regardless of whether the description holds at the time of judgment, earlier, or later. He gives the example 'Every sleeper is awake' to show that external predication does not require simultaneity; the judgment here means the actual predication, not the mental act. Al-Harawi adds a linguistic nuance: if the predicate is a verb or derivative, the subject must be described by the title at the time of predication. Finally, al-‘Attar explains the naming: 'haqiqiyya' is called real because it is the core type used in sciences, and 'dhihniyya' is mental because its subject exists only in the mind. All examples used are from Islamic sciences: e.g., 'Every human is an animal' is both real and external.

Two Types of Subject Existence in Propositions
Author distinguishes two types of subject existence: one for judgment and one for predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the existence required for judgment and the existence required for predication, and identify which type is present in any given proposition.

Core Idea
The subject's existence has two meanings: one for the judgment act, another for the predicate holding.

In logic, the existence of the subject in a proposition is not a simple matter. This passage from al-Dasuqi's commentary on the Tadhheeb clarifies a key distinction: the existence needed to make a judgment about a subject is different from the existence needed for the predicate to actually apply to it. Understanding this distinction is essential for analyzing propositions correctly, especially when dealing with negatives and non-existent subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of existence introduced
Read the opening sentence: existence is considered either with respect to the judgment ('al-hukm') or with respect to the affirmation of the predicate ('thubut al-mahmul'). These are two distinct considerations.
The first is called 'existence required by the judgment' (wujud yaqtadihi al-hukm), the second 'existence required by predication' (wujud yaqtadihi thubut al-mahmul).
2
Extract the three differences
The author lists three aspects (wujuh) of difference. Work through each: (1) scope: first exists in both affirmatives and negatives, second only in affirmatives. (2) temporality: first only at the moment of judgment, second at all times or a specific time. (3) locus: first is always mental, second can be mental or external.
Focus on the logical implications: negatives do not assert predication, so only the first existence is needed.
3
Apply the distinction to sample propositions
Test propositions like 'Every human is an animal' (both existences: mental for judgment, external for predication) and 'No phoenix is a bird' (only existence for judgment, since negative; predication does not hold).
For non-existent subjects like 'phoenix', the mental existence for judgment is sufficient for a negative proposition.
4
Connect to real vs external propositions
The final part discusses 'haqiqiyya' (real) and 'kharijiyya' (external) propositions. A proposition is real when the subject's existence is mental (e.g., 'Every phoenix is a bird'), external when the subject exists externally (e.g., 'Every color is whiteness' if only one color exists), and both when it exists both mind-independently and actually (e.g., 'Every human is an animal').
This classification depends on the two types of existence: real propositions require only existence for judgment; external propositions require existence for predication (external).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل : أنَّ وجودَ الموضوع تارةً؛ يُعتبرُ مِن حيث الحكم عليه، وتارة؛ يُعتبر من حيث ثبوت المحمول له والوجودُ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم مخالف للوجود الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع من أوجه:
الأول : أنَّ الوجود الأوَّلَ يكون في الموجبة والسالبة، والوجود الثاني: لا يكون إلا في الموجبة فقط . الثَّاني من الأوجُهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكمُ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ في حالةِ الحكم فقط؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فإِنَّهُ يُعتبرُ دائماً أو ساعة.
الثَّالثُ مِنَ الأوجهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم يكون بحسب الذهنِ؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، فإنَّه يكون بحسب الخارج تارةً، وبحسب الذهن أُخرى.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَالْحَقِيْقَيَّةُ) اعلَمْ أنَّ بين الحقيقيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ عموماً من وجه، تنفرد الخارجيَّةُ فيما إذا قُلْتَ: كلُّ لون بياضُ، فيما إذا لم يكنْ مِنَ الألوان إلا هو، وتنفرد الحقيقية في: كلُّ عنقاء طائر، ويجتمعان في : كلّ إنسان حيوان، فهي حقيقيَّةٌ باعتبار، وخارجيَّة باعتبار.
The sum: the existence of the subject is sometimes considered with respect to the judgment upon it, and sometimes considered with respect to the affirmation of the predicate for it. And the existence required by the judgment is different from the existence required by the affirmation of the predicate for the subject in aspects: First, the first existence occurs in both affirmative and negative [propositions], while the second existence occurs only in the affirmative. Second, the existence required by the judgment is only considered at the moment of judgment, unlike the existence required by the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, for it is considered at all times or at a particular time. Third, the existence required by the judgment is mental, unlike the existence required by the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, for it is sometimes external and sometimes mental. (His statement: 'the real') Know that between the real and the external there is a general-special relation in one respect. The external is exclusive when you say: 'Every color is whiteness', when there is no other color besides it. The real is exclusive in: 'Every phoenix is a bird'. They coincide in: 'Every human is an animal'; it is real in one respect and external in another.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage by al-Dasuqi explains the duality of subject existence in propositions. The existence required for the judgment (the act of issuing a proposition) is distinguished from the existence required for the actual predication (the predicate holding of the subject). Three differences are listed: (1) The existence for judgment is needed in both affirmative and negative propositions (since even a negative like 'Zayd is not standing' requires the subject to be conceived mentally), while the existence for predication is only needed in affirmatives (since negatives do not assert a predicate). (2) The existence for judgment is only considered at the moment of judgment, but the existence for predication can be at any time (past, present, future) as long as the predicate holds. (3) The existence for judgment is always mental (the subject must be mentally present), while the existence for predication can be either external or mental. Finally, he explains the relation between real (haqiqiyya) and external (kharijiyya) propositions: they have a partial overlap. Example: 'Every color is whiteness' is external only if whiteness is the only color; 'Every phoenix is a bird' is real only (since phoenix has no external existence); 'Every human is an animal' is both real (subject exists actually) and external (predication holds externally). This distinction is crucial for understanding how propositions function in logic and science.

Key Terms
وجود يقتضيه الحكم
وجود يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only negative
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The existence required for judgment is mental and common to all propositions; the existence required for predication is only in affirmatives and can be mental or external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Defining the Modified Term (al-Ma'dul)
Author defines the modified term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a modified term (ma'dul) in a categorical proposition and explain why it is called 'modified'.

Core Idea
The modified term is a part of a proposition that contains the negation particle as part of its own composition.

In classical logic, we distinguish between a simple proposition and one that contains a modified term. The modification does not change the quality of the proposition but affects the term itself. In this lesson, we will examine the definition of the modified term as given by the author and clarified by al-Dasuqi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
For any categorical proposition, list the subject and predicate terms.
2
Check for a negation particle
Look for a negation particle (e.g., 'la', 'ghayr') that is part of the composition of the term, not an external negation.
The particle must be part of the term itself.
3
Recognize the modified term
If the negation particle is built into the subject or predicate, that term is called 'modified' (ma'dul).
For example, in 'The non-living is inanimate', 'non-living' is a modified subject.
4
Distinguish the particle from the term
Note that the negation particle itself is not the modified term; the term that contains it is called modified.
Al-Dasuqi clarifies this point: the particle is the 'true' modified, but the term is named after it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ (جُزءٍ)؛ أي: من جزء القضيَّةِ كالموضوع والمحمول؛ (فَيْسَمَّى) جزء القضيَّةِ الَّذي جُعِلَ حرفُ السَّلبِ جزءاً منه (مَعْدُولاً)، والقضيَّةُ معدولةٌ موجبة، أو سالبة. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: مِنْ جُزْأَيِ الْقَضِيَّةِ هَكَذا في بعض النسخ بالتثنية، وهي ظاهرة، وفي بعضها : (أي : مِنْ جُزْءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ) بالإفرادِ، وعليها ؛ فَجزء مفرةٌ مُضافٌ يعم الجزأين . وبعبارة قوله : (من جزء (القضيَّةِ ؛ أي من جزء من جزأي القضيَّة، وقولُه : (كالموضوع ... إلخ) ربَّما أوهم هذا أنَّ العدول خاص بالحمليَّاتِ، معَ أَنَّهُ يكونُ في الشَّرطيَّاتِ، فَكانَ عليهِ أنْ يزيد المقدَّمَ والتّالي؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ: الكافُ للتَّمثيل، فيدخل ذلك لا أنَّها استقصائية. (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْمَحْمُوْلِ) أي : أو المحمول . (قَوْلُهُ : فَيُسَمَّى جُزْءُ الْقَضِيَّةِ ... إلخ) في الحقيقة المعدول هو حرفُ السَّلْبِ؛ لأنَّهُ هو الذي عدل بِهِ عن موضعه، وهو قطعُ النِّسبة لكنْ لَمَّا عدل في ذلك الجزء بحرف السَّلْبِ عَن موضِعِهِ؛ سُمِّي الجزء معدولاً، فهو من تسمية المحلِّ باسم الحال فيه . وقولنا : عدل في ذلك الجزء بحرفِ السَّلْبِ عَن مَوضِعِهِ؛ هو أَنَّ القصد بِهِ نفي الحكم عن الموضوع. (قَوْلُهُ : مُوْجِبَةٌ أَوْ سَالِبَةٌ) أي : وهي موجبة أو سالبةٌ .
Chapter: On the Known and its Divisions. (A part) i.e., from a part of the proposition like the subject and predicate; so the part of the proposition into which the negation particle has been made a part is called 'modified', and the proposition is called 'modified', positive or negative. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: i.e., from the two parts of the proposition — thus in some copies with the dual, which is apparent, and in some copies it is singular, and then 'part' is a possessive construction encompassing both parts. And in another expression, his saying: from a part of the proposition means from one of the two parts of the proposition. And his saying: like the subject etc. might give the impression that modification is specific to categorical propositions, while it also occurs in conditionals, so he should have included the antecedent and consequent, unless it is said that the 'kaaf' is for exemplification, so it includes that, not that it is exhaustive. His saying: 'and the predicate' i.e., or the predicate. His saying: 'so the part of the proposition is called' etc. In reality, the modified is the negation particle, because it is that which was moved from its place, and it is the cutting of the relation. But when it was moved in that part by the negation particle from its place, the part is called modified, so it is naming the locus after the state in it. And our saying: 'he moved in that part by the negation particle from its place' is that the intention by it is the negation of the judgment about the subject. His saying: 'positive or negative' i.e., it is either positive or negative.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of the modified proposition (al-qadiyya al-ma'dula) in categorical logic. It begins by stating that a part of the proposition (either subject or predicate) into which the negation particle is made a part is called 'modified' (ma'dul), and the proposition itself is called 'modified' (ma'dula), whether it is positive or negative. Al-Dasuqi then clarifies the textual variations: some copies read 'from the two parts' (dual), others 'from a part' (singular), but the singular encompasses both because it is a possessive. He notes a potential misunderstanding: the example 'like the subject and predicate' might imply modification is limited to categoricals, but it also applies to conditionals. The response is that the 'kaaf' (like) is for exemplification, not exhaustive listing. Then he explains that in reality the modified is the negation particle itself, because it is what is moved from its original position (which is to negate the relation). However, when the negation particle is incorporated into a part, that part is called modified by way of naming the place after its occupant. The intention of incorporating the negation into a part is to negate the judgment about the subject, not to negate the existence of the subject. Finally, he confirms that the modified proposition can be either positive (e.g., 'The non-living is inanimate') or negative (e.g., 'The inanimate is not knowing'). This definition ensures precision in distinguishing modified propositions from ordinary negative propositions.

Key Terms
مَعْدُول
حَرْفُ السَّلْبِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'modified' (ma'dul)?
A) The negation particle itself
B) The part of the proposition containing the negation particle
C) The entire proposition
D) The predicate only

Key Takeaway
A modified term is a subject or predicate that incorporates the negation particle as part of its own composition.

What is a Modified Proposition (al-Ma'dula)?
Author defines the modified proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a proposition as modified or non-modified and determine its polarity (positive or negative) based on the presence of modified terms.

Core Idea
A modified proposition contains at least one modified term, and it can be either positive or negative in quality.

After understanding the modified term, we now turn to the proposition that contains such a term. The author defines a modified proposition as one that has a modified subject or predicate, and he emphasizes that it can be either positive or negative. Al-Dasuqi expands on this to clarify potential misunderstandings about the scope of modification.

Guided Path
1
Given a proposition, identify if any term is modified
Check the subject and predicate for the presence of a negation particle as part of their composition.
2
Classify the proposition as modified or not
If at least one term is modified, the proposition is a modified proposition (ma'dula).
3
Determine the quality (positive or negative)
Look at the copula: if the proposition affirms, it is positive; if it denies, it is negative. The quality is independent of the modification.
For example, 'The non-living is inanimate' is positive despite the modified subject.
4
Note that modification applies to conditionals too
Al-Dasuqi points out that modification also occurs in conditional propositions, not just categoricals.
The antecedent and consequent can be modified terms.

Understanding Nafs al-Amr: The Reality of the Matter
Author opens the chapter on definition by addressing nafs al-amr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of nafs al-amr and distinguish it from mental and verbal existence, using the three modes of existence.

Core Idea
Nafs al-amr is the thing as it is, independent of our thought or speech.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational concept in Islamic logic: nafs al-amr. When we say a proposition is true, we often mean it corresponds to reality. But what is that reality? The scholars distinguish between existence in the external world, in the mind, and in language. Nafs al-amr refers to the thing itself, cutting through these layers. Let's trace how the commentators unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term
Find the phrase 'إذا نسبت إلى نفس الأمر' in the Arabic. Notice that the chapter opens with this.
2
Grasp al-Dasūqī's explanation
He says it means considering the proposition as it is in reality, not as we understand it.
3
Follow al-‘Aṭṭār's expansion
He distinguishes the subject and predicate, and warns about misinterpretations of 'between'.
4
Understand ‘Iṣām's contribution
He explains why conditionals are treated separately, then the author gives a thorough definition of nafs al-amr.
5
Apply the three modes of existence
The text states things have existence in external reality, in the mind, and in expression. Nafs al-amr is the thing itself. E.g., Zayd being a writer has a reality in nafs al-amr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إذا نُسِبَتْ إلى نفس الأمرِ، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أي : إذا نظر لها باعتبار ما في الواقع. ) واعلَمْ أنَّ كيفيَّةَ النِّسبة تنحصر في الإمكانِ والضَّرورة؛ أي: الوجوب، فَجميعُ الكيفيَّاتِ تتفرّعُ على هَذَيْنِ، والمراد بالوجوب: الوجوب العقلي. العطار بغيره، بنفسه لا يقتضي الارتباط بغيره، والمحمول : مفهوم يقتضي الارتباط فالنسبة التي بها الارتباط تستحق أن تُضاف إليهِ وإِنْ كانَتْ بين بين. وإِيَّاكَ أنْ تتوهم مِن قولِهِ : وإِنْ كانَتْ بينَ بينَ؛ أَنَّهُ حملَ النِّسبة على النِّسبةِ الَّتِي بينَ بينَ دونَ الوقوع واللا وقوع؛ لأنَّ الوقوع واللا وقوع أيضاً مُتصوَّران بين بين. عصام، ثمَّ إِنَّ نسبة التّالي للمقدَّم أيضاً لا تخلو عن تلك الكيفية، لكنَّ عادة المتأخرين جرَتْ باعتبار اللزومِ والعنادِ والاتِّفاقِ بينَهُمَا ; لا باعتبار تلك الجهاتِ كَمَا بَيَّنَّا سابقاً، فظهر وجه تخصيص البحث بالحمليَّاتِ وسقط قولُ المحشّي : إِنَّ تَخصيص الجهةِ بالحَمليّة غير ظاهر . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ ) أي : إذا نُظِرَ لِلنِّسبة المفهومةِ مِنَ القضيَّةِ باعتبار وجودِهَا في نفسِهَا ; أي: تحقَّقها في الواقع بقطعِ النَّظَرِ عن فهمِنَا لَهَا مِنَ اللَّفْظ، فَنفس الأمر عبارةٌ عَنِ الشَّيء في نفسِهِ ; أي: الشَّيء في حد ذاتِهِ بقطعِ النَّظِرِ عَن تعقُلِنَا لَهُ وفَرْضِنَا إيَّاهُ فإِنَّ لِلشَّيء وجوداً في الأعيان ووجوداً في الأذهان ووجوداً في العبارة، والمراد بالوجودِ العيني: الوجود الخارجي، فيشمل المحسوس وغيره؛ لا ما يتبادر من لفظ الأعيان من تخصُّصه بالمحسوس، فإنَّ ثبوت الكتابة لزيدٍ في قولنا : زيد كاتب مثلاً ; أمر اعتباري لكنْ مِن حيث كونه مُنتزعاً ومُرتبطاً بِأمرَيْنِ وُجودِيَّيْنِ ; قيل : إِنَّ لَهُ تحققاً في نفسِهِ ; وإِنْ كانَتِ الأمورُ الاعتباريَّةُ لَا وجود لها في الخارج، وإنَّما وجودها في الخارج هو وجود ما
Chapter on definition and its divisions: When [the proposition] is related to nafs al-amr. al-Dasūqī: His saying: 'when related to nafs al-amr' means when it is considered with regard to what is in reality. And know that the modality of the relation is limited to possibility and necessity, i.e., obligation, so all modalities branch off from these two, and by obligation is meant intellectual obligation. al-‘Aṭṭār: ... by itself does not necessitate connection with another, and the predicate is a concept that necessitates connection, so the relation by which there is connection deserves to be attributed to it, even if it is between. And beware of misunderstanding from his saying 'even if it is between' that he carried the relation to the relation that is between, without occurrence and non-occurrence, because occurrence and non-occurrence are also conceived as between. ‘Iṣām: Then the relation of the consequent to the antecedent is also not devoid of that modality, but the habit of the later scholars proceeded by considering implication, opposition, and agreement between them, not by considering those aspects as we explained previously, so the reason for restricting the investigation to categoricals becomes clear, and the statement of the commentator that 'the restriction of modality to categoricals is not apparent' falls. His saying: 'when related to nafs al-amr' means when the relation understood from the proposition is considered with regard to its existence in itself, i.e., its realization in reality, disregarding our understanding of it from the expression. So nafs al-amr is an expression for the thing in itself, i.e., the thing in its essence, disregarding our conception of it and our assumption of it. For a thing has existence in external objects, existence in minds, and existence in expression. By 'external existence' is meant external reality, which includes the sensible and other than it; not what springs to mind from the word 'external objects' of being restricted to the sensible. For the establishment of writing for Zayd in our saying 'Zayd is a writer', for example, is a relative matter, but insofar as it is extracted and connected to two existent things, it is said that it has realization in itself, even though relative matters have no existence in the external, and their existence in the external is only the existence of what...
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter titled 'On the Definition and Its Divisions' (فصل في المعرف وأقسامه). The author immediately addresses the modality of the proposition when related to nafs al-amr (the reality of the matter). al-Dasūqī explains that 'when related to nafs al-amr' means considering the proposition as it corresponds to reality. Then the text states that the modality (kayfiyya) of the relation is limited to possibility and necessity (obligation), and all other modalities branch from these two; 'obligation' here means intellectual necessity. al-‘Aṭṭār adds a note distinguishing the subject and predicate: the subject by itself does not require connection to something else, while the predicate is a concept that implies connection; therefore the relation that connects them is attributed to the predicate. He warns against misunderstanding the phrase 'even if it is between' (بين بين) as referring only to a neutral relation, because even occurrence and non-occurrence are also 'between' in a sense. Next, ‘Iṣām notes that the relation of the consequent to the antecedent in conditional propositions also involves these modalities, but later logicians typically treated conditionals in terms of implication (luzūm), opposition (‘inād), and agreement (ittifāq), not the same modal categories as categoricals. This explains why the investigation of modality is often restricted to categoricals, contrary to some objections. Finally, the author elaborates on the concept of nafs al-amr: it is the thing considered in itself, disregarding our conceptualization or verbal expression. Things have three modes of existence: in external reality (a‘yān), in the mind (adhān), and in expression (‘ibāra). 'External existence' includes both sensible and non-sensible entities, contrary to a common assumption. For example, the attribute 'writing' for Zayd in the proposition 'Zayd is a writer' is a relative (i‘tibārī) matter, but because it is abstracted from and connected to two existent things (Zayd and the act of writing), it is considered to have a certain reality in itself even though relative matters have no independent external existence—their external existence is merely the existence of the underlying entities. This discussion lays the groundwork for the definition of logical terms and their divisions.

Key Terms
نَفْس الْأَمْر
وُجُود عَيْنِي
وُجُود ذِهْنِي
وُجُود عِبَارَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct understanding of 'nafs al-amr'?
A) It refers to the verbal expression of a proposition.
B) It refers to the thing as it is in its essence, disregarding mental conception and expression.
C) It refers to the mental conception that comes to mind.
D) It refers only to external sensible objects.

Key Takeaway
Nafs al-amr is the objective reality of a thing, independent of mental or linguistic representation, and is foundational for understanding truth in logic and theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using the example from the passage, explain why the statement 'Zayd is a writer' is true in nafs al-amr even though 'writer' is a relative attribute. Reference the three modes of existence.
?

The Two Fundamental Modalities: Possibility and Necessity
Author explains the foundational division of propositional modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two fundamental modalities (imkan and darura) and explain how other modalities are derived from them, and why conditionals are treated separately.

Core Idea
All propositional modalities reduce to possibility and necessity.

After clarifying nafs al-amr, the author turns to the modality of the propositional relation. He declares that the modality (kayfiyya) is limited to possibility and necessity. This compact statement is unpacked by the commentators, who also note that later logicians treat conditionals using different categories. Let's break down this key claim.

Guided Path
1
Locate the statement
Find the phrase 'اعْلَمْ أنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ النِّسْبَةِ تَنْحَصِرُ فِي الْإِمْكَانِ وَالضَّرُورَةِ' in the Arabic.
2
Understand 'necessity'
The text explains that necessity here means intellectual necessity (al-wujub al-'aqli).
3
Relate other modalities
All other modalities (e.g., actuality, impossibility) are branches of these two.
4
Note the treatment of conditionals
‘Iṣām says later logicians treat conditionals using implication, opposition, and agreement, not the same modal categories. This is why modality is often studied only for categoricals.
5
Apply the distinction
Consider a proposition like 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Its modality can be analyzed as necessity (obligation) or possibility? Reflect on how the two fundamental modes apply.

فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إذا نُسِبَتْ إلى نفس الأمرِ، الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أي : إذا نظر لها باعتبار ما في الواقع. ) واعلَمْ أنَّ كيفيَّةَ النِّسبة تنحصر في الإمكانِ والضَّرورة؛ أي: الوجوب، فَجميعُ الكيفيَّاتِ تتفرّعُ على هَذَيْنِ، والمراد بالوجوب: الوجوب العقلي. العطار بغيره، بنفسه لا يقتضي الارتباط بغيره، والمحمول : مفهوم يقتضي الارتباط فالنسبة التي بها الارتباط تستحق أن تُضاف إليهِ وإِنْ كانَتْ بين بين. وإِيَّاكَ أنْ تتوهم مِن قولِهِ : وإِنْ كانَتْ بينَ بينَ؛ أَنَّهُ حملَ النِّسبة على النِّسبةِ الَّتِي بينَ بينَ دونَ الوقوع واللا وقوع؛ لأنَّ الوقوع واللا وقوع أيضاً مُتصوَّران بين بين. عصام، ثمَّ إِنَّ نسبة التّالي للمقدَّم أيضاً لا تخلو عن تلك الكيفية، لكنَّ عادة المتأخرين جرَتْ باعتبار اللزومِ والعنادِ والاتِّفاقِ بينَهُمَا ; لا باعتبار تلك الجهاتِ كَمَا بَيَّنَّا سابقاً، فظهر وجه تخصيص البحث بالحمليَّاتِ وسقط قولُ المحشّي : إِنَّ تَخصيص الجهةِ بالحَمليّة غير ظاهر . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذَا نُسِبَتْ إِلَى نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ ) أي : إذا نُظِرَ لِلنِّسبة المفهومةِ مِنَ القضيَّةِ باعتبار وجودِهَا في نفسِهَا ; أي: تحقَّقها في الواقع بقطعِ النَّظَرِ عن فهمِنَا لَهَا مِنَ اللَّفْظ، فَنفس الأمر عبارةٌ عَنِ الشَّيء في نفسِهِ ; أي: الشَّيء في حد ذاتِهِ بقطعِ النَّظِرِ عَن تعقُلِنَا لَهُ وفَرْضِنَا إيَّاهُ فإِنَّ لِلشَّيء وجوداً في الأعيان ووجوداً في الأذهان ووجوداً في العبارة، والمراد بالوجودِ العيني: الوجود الخارجي، فيشمل المحسوس وغيره؛ لا ما يتبادر من لفظ الأعيان من تخصُّصه بالمحسوس، فإنَّ ثبوت الكتابة لزيدٍ في قولنا : زيد كاتب مثلاً ; أمر اعتباري لكنْ مِن حيث كونه مُنتزعاً ومُرتبطاً بِأمرَيْنِ وُجودِيَّيْنِ ; قيل : إِنَّ لَهُ تحققاً في نفسِهِ ; وإِنْ كانَتِ الأمورُ الاعتباريَّةُ لَا وجود لها في الخارج، وإنَّما وجودها في الخارج هو وجود ما
Chapter on definition and its divisions: When [the proposition] is related to nafs al-amr. al-Dasūqī: His saying: 'when related to nafs al-amr' means when it is considered with regard to what is in reality. And know that the modality of the relation is limited to possibility and necessity, i.e., obligation, so all modalities branch off from these two, and by obligation is meant intellectual obligation. al-‘Aṭṭār: ... by itself does not necessitate connection with another, and the predicate is a concept that necessitates connection, so the relation by which there is connection deserves to be attributed to it, even if it is between. And beware of misunderstanding from his saying 'even if it is between' that he carried the relation to the relation that is between, without occurrence and non-occurrence, because occurrence and non-occurrence are also conceived as between. ‘Iṣām: Then the relation of the consequent to the antecedent is also not devoid of that modality, but the habit of the later scholars proceeded by considering implication, opposition, and agreement between them, not by considering those aspects as we explained previously, so the reason for restricting the investigation to categoricals becomes clear, and the statement of the commentator that 'the restriction of modality to categoricals is not apparent' falls. His saying: 'when related to nafs al-amr' means when the relation understood from the proposition is considered with regard to its existence in itself, i.e., its realization in reality, disregarding our understanding of it from the expression. So nafs al-amr is an expression for the thing in itself, i.e., the thing in its essence, disregarding our conception of it and our assumption of it. For a thing has existence in external objects, existence in minds, and existence in expression. By 'external existence' is meant external reality, which includes the sensible and other than it; not what springs to mind from the word 'external objects' of being restricted to the sensible. For the establishment of writing for Zayd in our saying 'Zayd is a writer', for example, is a relative matter, but insofar as it is extracted and connected to two existent things, it is said that it has realization in itself, even though relative matters have no existence in the external, and their existence in the external is only the existence of what...
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter titled 'On the Definition and Its Divisions' (فصل في المعرف وأقسامه). The author immediately addresses the modality of the proposition when related to nafs al-amr (the reality of the matter). al-Dasūqī explains that 'when related to nafs al-amr' means considering the proposition as it corresponds to reality. Then the text states that the modality (kayfiyya) of the relation is limited to possibility and necessity (obligation), and all other modalities branch from these two; 'obligation' here means intellectual necessity. al-‘Aṭṭār adds a note distinguishing the subject and predicate: the subject by itself does not require connection to something else, while the predicate is a concept that implies connection; therefore the relation that connects them is attributed to the predicate. He warns against misunderstanding the phrase 'even if it is between' (بين بين) as referring only to a neutral relation, because even occurrence and non-occurrence are also 'between' in a sense. Next, ‘Iṣām notes that the relation of the consequent to the antecedent in conditional propositions also involves these modalities, but later logicians typically treated conditionals in terms of implication (luzūm), opposition (‘inād), and agreement (ittifāq), not the same modal categories as categoricals. This explains why the investigation of modality is often restricted to categoricals, contrary to some objections. Finally, the author elaborates on the concept of nafs al-amr: it is the thing considered in itself, disregarding our conceptualization or verbal expression. Things have three modes of existence: in external reality (a‘yān), in the mind (adhān), and in expression (‘ibāra). 'External existence' includes both sensible and non-sensible entities, contrary to a common assumption. For example, the attribute 'writing' for Zayd in the proposition 'Zayd is a writer' is a relative (i‘tibārī) matter, but because it is abstracted from and connected to two existent things (Zayd and the act of writing), it is considered to have a certain reality in itself even though relative matters have no independent external existence—their external existence is merely the existence of the underlying entities. This discussion lays the groundwork for the definition of logical terms and their divisions.

Key Terms
كَيْفِيَّة النِّسْبَة
إِمْكَان
ضَرُورَة
وُجُوب عَقْلِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Possibility and impossibility
B) Necessity and contingency
C) Possibility and necessity
D) Actuality and potentiality

Key Takeaway
The modality of a propositional relation is fundamentally either possibility or necessity (intellectual obligation), and all other modal notions are derivative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Understanding the Section on Definition (Ma'rif)
Heading introduces a new section on definition (ma'rif) and its classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of the section heading 'فصل في المعرف وأقسامه' within the structure of a classical logic text and anticipate the content it introduces.

Core Idea
A section heading is a roadmap for the logical argument to follow.

In this lesson, we examine a brief but important element: a section heading from a classical logic textbook. While it contains only a few words, this heading tells you exactly what the author is about to discuss. Understanding how to read these headings is the first step toward mastering the structure of any 'Ilm al-Mantiq work. Even when the surrounding text is corrupted, the heading itself remains a reliable anchor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact phrasing of the heading.
Look at the Arabic: 'فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ'. Note the word 'فصل' (fasl) which means a section or chapter. The phrase 'في المعرف' indicates this section is about the definition or the known. 'وأقسامه' adds 'and its divisions'.
In classical texts, 'فصل' is a structural marker; it signals a new discrete topic.
2
Recognize the flow of the book.
Before this heading, the author covered propositions (qadaya). Now he moves to definition (ma'rif), which is the other major component of tasawwurat (conceptual knowledge). This transition is standard: first the raw material (propositions), then the tools for clarifying concepts (definitions).
The author is following the classical order: concepts → propositions → argumentation.
3
Anticipate the content that should follow.
The heading promises a discussion of different types of definitions. Based on Islamic logic, you should expect to learn about hadd (complete definition), rasm (description), and their subdivisions. Even though the actual text after this heading is missing in this edition, this heading alone tells you what the author intended to explain next.
This skill of anticipating content from headings helps you reconstruct missing or corrupted passages and understand the author's plan.
4
Connect to other Islamic sciences.
The theory of definition is not only for logicians. In fiqh, definitions of terms like 'salah' or 'sawm' determine legal rulings. In kalam, defining 'qadim' (eternal) and 'hadith' (temporal) is critical. This heading signals the start of a section that provides tools used across disciplines.
Memorizing section headings like this one helps you quickly locate relevant material when studying other subjects.

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
Section: On the Definition and Its Divisions
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This is a section heading that marks a new topic: definition (ma'rif/ta'rif) and its classifications. In Islamic logic, after discussing propositions (qadaya), the author moves to the theory of definition, which is crucial for forming clear concepts (tasawwurat). The heading indicates that the following content will cover the various types of definition, such as complete (hadd) and incomplete (rasm), and their subdivisions. However, due to an OCR issue, the actual text of this section is not preserved in this window; the subsequent passage is a repetition of the previous section on modality. This heading should be memorized as the beginning of a major section.
