Distinguishing Absolute and Eternal Necessity
Author defines absolute necessity and distinguishes it from eternal necessity with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute necessity (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) and eternal necessity (al-daruriyya al-azaliyya) and identify the correct applicability of each in logical propositions.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity is wider than eternal necessity; a predicate can be necessary as long as the subject exists without being eternal.

In Islamic logic, distinguishing types of necessity is crucial for correctly analyzing modal propositions. This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies the definitions of absolute and eternal necessity, showing that eternal necessity is a subset of absolute necessity, and that essential predicates like 'animal' for 'human' are absolute necessities without being eternal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity defined
The passage defines absolute necessity (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) and eternal necessity (al-daruriyya al-azaliyya). Note that absolute necessity is also called essential necessity (al-dhatiyya).
2
Extract the definition of absolute necessity
Absolute necessity is defined as 'as long as the subject's essence exists.' This means the predicate is necessarily true of the subject only during the time the subject exists.
This is conditional on existence, not unconditional.
3
Extract the definition of eternal necessity
Eternal necessity is defined as 'from eternity to eternity' unconditionally. Example: 'God is living necessarily.' It applies only to subjects whose existence is necessary.
This is more specific than absolute necessity.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: If absolute necessity is 'as long as the subject exists,' then 'Every human exists with possibility' would be necessary. The response: That necessity is conditional on existence, not absolute. The difference will be clarified later.
The key is that absolute necessity does not require eternal existence.
5
Understand the asymmetry in affirmatives and negatives
In affirmative propositions, eternal necessity implies absolute necessity but not vice versa. In negatives, they are equivalent because if a predicate is negated as long as the subject exists, it is negated eternally (since it cannot be affirmed in non-existence).
This is a subtle logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوْعِ مَوْجُوْدَةٌ قالَ في شرح الأصل: فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الضَّروريّة المطلقة : هي الذاتية على ما في الذَّاتِيَّةُ عَلَى مَا فِي الشَّفاءِ؛ لَا الأَزليَّةُ على ما في الإشاراتِ، فإِنْ قُلْتَ : الضَّروريَّةُ بهذا التَّفسيرِ لا تُنافي الممكنة الخاصَّةً إذا كانَ مَحمولُها الوجودَ كَقولِنَا : كُلُّ إنسانٍ مَوجود بالإمكان الخاص ؛ لأنَّ المحمول ضَروريُّ الثُبوتِ للموضوع ما دامَتْ ذاتُ الموضوع موجودةً؛ قُلْتُ : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ المحمول ضَروري القُبوتِ لِلموضوعِ في جميع أوقاتِ وجودِ الموضوع؛ بل بشرط وجودِ الذَّاتِ، وَسَتعرفُ الفرق بينَهُما .ا.هـ. وهذا الفرقُ سيأتي في الكلام على المشروطة العامة، والضَّرورة الأزليَّةُ ما حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع أزلاً وأبداً ؛ كَمَا في قولنا : اللهُ حَيٌّ بالضَّرورةِ، والضَّرورةُ الأزليَّةُ أخصُّ مِنَ الضَّرورةِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ المطلقة؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة متى تحققت أزلاً وأبداً ؛ تتحقق ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجودةٌ من غيرِ عكس، وإنَّما يصحُ هذا في الإيجاب، وأمَّا في السَّلْبِ؛ فَهُمَا مُتساويانِ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِذا سُلِبَ المحمولُ عَنِ الموضوع ما دامَتْ ذاتُهُ مَوجودة؛ يكون مسلوباً عنه أزلاً وأبداً؛ لامتناع ثبوتِه له حال العدم، والجلال بعد أن نقل هذا الكلامَ؛ نظرَ فِيهِ بأَنَّهُ لو كانَ معنى الضَّرورةِ المطلقة ما ذكر ؛ لزم أن لا تصدق إلا في مادَّةِ الضَّرورةِ الأزليَّةِ، فَلَا تكون أعم منها ؛ لأنَّ وجودَ الموضوع إذا لم يكن ضروريا في وقت وجودِهِ؛ لم يكن ثبوت المحمول لهُ ضَروريا في ذلك الوقت، وهذا ظاهر ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : إنَّ معنى ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً : أن يكون أوقات وجودِهِ ظرفاً للضَّرورة؛ لَا شرطاً ، فلا يرد المثال المذكورُ ؛ لأنَّ الضَّرورة فيهِ بشرط الوجودِ؛ لا في زمانِ الوجودِ، وأمَّا مَا أوردَ عليهِ أنَّهُ يلزمُ حينئذٍ حصرُ الضَّرورة الذاتية في الأزليَّة لأنَّه لا يصدقُ إلا في الموضوع الواجب أو الممتنع لأنَّهُ ما لم يجب وجوده؛ لم يجب له شيءٌ في أوقاتِ وجودِهِ؛ فَمدفوع بأنَّ ثبوت الذَّاتِيَّاتِ لِلذَّاتِ ضَروري في زمانِ وجودِهِ لا بشرط الوجودِ؛ نحو: كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ، فَإِنَّ الذَّاتِيَّ مُتقدِّم على الذَّاتِ وُجوداً وعدماً .
His saying: 'as long as the subject's essence exists.' He said in the commentary on the original: In it is an indication that the absolute necessary is the essential necessary according to what is in al-Shifa, not the eternal necessary according to what is in al-Isharat. If you say: The necessary with this interpretation does not contradict the particular possible when its predicate is existence, like our saying: 'Every human exists with particular possibility' because the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject as long as the subject's essence exists; I say: We do not accept that the predicate is necessarily affirmed of the subject in all times of the subject's existence; rather, conditionally on the existence of the essence, and you will know the difference between them. End. And this difference will come in the discussion on the general conditional. And the eternal necessary is that in which the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject is judged eternally and forever, as in our saying: 'God is living necessarily.' And the eternal necessary is more specific than the essential absolute necessary, because whenever necessity is realized eternally and forever, it is realized as long as the subject's essence exists, without the reverse. And this is only correct in affirmative [propositions]; as for negative, they are equal, because if the predicate is negated from the subject as long as its essence exists, it is negated from it eternally and forever, due to the impossibility of its affirmation to it in the state of non-existence. And al-Jalal, after quoting this speech, criticized it: If the meaning of absolute necessity were as mentioned, then it would not be true except in the matter of eternal necessity, so it would not be more general, because if the existence of the subject is not necessary at the time of its existence, then the affirmation of the predicate to it would not be necessary at that time, and this is apparent. End. And Abd al-Hakim said: The meaning of 'as long as the subject's essence exists' is that the times of its existence are a vessel for the necessity, not a condition, so the mentioned example does not apply, because the necessity in it is conditional on existence, not in the time of existence. As for the objection that then the essential necessity would be confined to the eternal, because it is only true of the necessary or impossible subject, since unless its existence is necessary, nothing is necessary for it in the times of its existence; it is refuted by the fact that the affirmation of the essentials to the essence is necessary in the time of its existence without the condition of existence, like: 'Every human is an animal necessarily', for the essential precedes the essence in existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion that clarifies the meaning of 'absolute necessity' (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) as used in logic, distinguishing it from 'eternal necessity' (al-daruriyya al-azaliyya). It begins by citing the phrase 'as long as the subject's essence exists' from the matn, and the commentator explains that this indicates absolute necessity is the same as 'essential necessity' (al-daruriyya al-dhatiyya) as per Avicenna's al-Shifa, not the 'eternal necessity' of al-Isharat. The author then raises a potential objection: if absolute necessity is defined this way, it would not contradict 'particular possibility' (al-mumkina al-khassa), as in 'Every human exists with particular possibility'—because the predicate 'existence' is necessarily affirmed of the subject as long as it exists. The response is that the necessity in such a proposition is conditional on the existence of the subject, not absolute; the difference will be clarified later. The passage then defines eternal necessity as a judgment of necessity for all eternity, e.g., 'God is living necessarily.' Eternal necessity is more specific than absolute necessity because if a necessity holds eternally, it also holds as long as the subject exists, but not vice versa. This asymmetry only holds for affirmative propositions; in negatives, they are equivalent because if a predicate is negated as long as the subject exists, it is negated eternally (since it cannot be affirmed in non-existence). The commentator al-Jalal raises a critique: If absolute necessity were as defined, it would only be true in cases of eternal necessity, thus not more general, because if the subject's existence is not necessary at the time, the predicate's necessity is not guaranteed. Abd al-Hakim responds by interpreting 'as long as the subject's essence exists' as a vessel for the necessity, not a condition. The objection that this restricts essential necessity to eternal subjects is refuted by noting that essential predicates (like 'animal' for 'human') are necessary in the time of existence without condition, because the essential precedes the essence in existence and non-existence.

Key Terms
الضَّرورة المُطلقة
الضَّرورة الأزليَّة
الضَّرورة الذاتيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of eternal necessity (al-daruriyya al-azaliyya) according to the passage?
A) Every human is an animal necessarily.
B) God is living necessarily.
C) The sun rises necessarily.
D) Every writer is human necessarily.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between absolute and eternal necessity hinges on whether the necessity persists only as long as the subject exists (absolute) or from eternity to eternity (eternal); this distinction is crucial for accurately analyzing modal propositions about contingent and necessary beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each proposition as either absolute or eternal necessity: (a) 'God is powerful necessarily.' (b) 'Every angel is a substance necessarily.' (c) 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) is human necessarily.'
?
Using the distinction in the passage, explain why 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is an example of absolute necessity, not eternal necessity, even though it holds for all humans.

The Absolute Necessary Proposition: Temporal Context of Necessity
Commentary defines absolute necessary proposition and clarifies temporal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly interpret the phrase 'as long as the subject exists' in the definition of absolute necessary proposition as a temporal context rather than a restrictive condition.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity is in the time of existence, not conditional upon existence.

In Islamic logic, the absolute necessary proposition is a key type of necessary proposition. However, its definition—'the judgment is necessary as long as the subject exists'—can be misinterpreted as a condition that restricts necessity to the subject's existence. This lesson unpacks the commentary by al-Dasuqi, al-Attar, al-Jalal, and Abd al-Hakim to clarify that the phrase indicates the time frame in which necessity holds, not a conditional restriction. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the concept correctly in theological and logical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'absolute necessary proposition' (ضرورية مطلقة). Note that it is a compound term: 'necessary' (ضرورية) qualified by 'absolute' (مطلقة).
2
Analyze al-Dasuqi's initial presentation
Al-Dasuqi explains why this type is presented first: it is the most specific among necessary propositions, and because divine perfect attributes are all established by necessity.
This hints at its importance for Kalam.
3
Examine al-Jalal's objection
Al-Jalal objects: if 'as long as the subject exists' means necessity is conditional upon existence, then absolute necessity would only apply to eternal subjects (e.g., God), and would not be more general than eternal necessity.
This reveals a potential misinterpretation.
4
Understand Abd al-Hakim's clarification
Abd al-Hakim responds: 'as long as the subject's essence exists' is a context (zarf) for necessity, not a condition (shart). The necessity holds during the time of existence, not conditional upon that existence.
He gives the example: 'Every human is an animal is necessary' — this is true in the time of the human's existence, even though the human's existence is not necessary.
5
Apply the distinction to avoid confusion
Recognize that the phrase does not restrict necessity to subjects whose existence is necessary (like God). Instead, it specifies that whenever the subject exists, the predicate is necessary. This applies to both necessary and contingent subjects.
The example of essential attributes (ذاتيات) shows that necessity precedes the subject's existence in conceptual order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فَضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ). الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) إِنَّما قَدَّمَهَا ؛ لأنَّها أخصُّ مِن كُلِّ مَا بعدها، وَلِأَنَّ ثبوت الصفات الكماليةِ للهِ كُلُّها تكيف بِالضَّرورة . العطار عكس، وإنَّما يصحُ هذا في الإيجاب، وأمَّا في السَّلْبِ؛ فَهُمَا مُتساويانِ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِذا سُلِبَ المحمولُ عَنِ الموضوع ما دامَتْ ذاتُهُ مَوجودة؛ يكون مسلوباً عنه أزلاً وأبداً؛ لامتناع ثبوتِه له حال العدم، والجلال بعد أن نقل هذا الكلامَ؛ نظرَ فِيهِ بأَنَّهُ لو كانَ معنى الضَّرورةِ المطلقة ما ذكر ؛ لزم أن لا تصدق إلا في مادَّةِ الضَّرورةِ الأزليَّةِ، فَلَا تكون أعم منها ; لأنَّ وجودَ الموضوع إذا لم يكن ضروريا في وقت وجودِهِ؛ لم يكن ثبوت المحمول لهُ ضَروريا في ذلك الوقت، وهذا ظاهر ا.هـ. وقال عبد الحكيم : إنَّ معنى ما دامَ ذاتُ الموضوع موجوداً : أن يكون أوقات وجودِهِ ظرفاً للضَّرورة؛ لَا شرطاً ، فلا يرد المثال المذكورُ ; لأنَّ الضَّرورة فيهِ بشرط الوجودِ؛ لا في زمانِ الوجودِ، وأمَّا مَا أوردَ عليهِ أنَّهُ يلزمُ حينئذٍ حصرُ الضَّرورة الذاتية في الأزليَّة لأنَّه لا يصدقُ إلا في الموضوع الواجب أو الممتنع لأنَّهُ ما لم يجب وجوده؛ لم يجب له شيءٌ في أوقاتِ وجودِهِ؛ فَمدفوع بأنَّ ثبوت الذَّاتِيَّاتِ لِلذَّاتِ ضَروري في زمانِ وجودِهِ لا بشرط الوجودِ؛ نحو: كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ، فَإِنَّ الذَّاتِيَّ مُتقدِّم على الذَّاتِ وُجوداً وعدماً ، وَمَا قِبْلَ (١) في الجواب : إِنَّ زيداً موجود؛ قضيَّةٌ ذهنيَّةٌ، والكلام في القضايا الحقيقيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ فَلَا يحسم مادَّةَ الإشكالِ ; لأنَّ كُلَّ قضيَّةٍ خارجيَّة أو ذهنية يكون محمولها الوجود ترد إشكالاً ، فإنَّ المحمول ضَروريُّ الثُّبوتِ ما دامَ الموضوع موجوداً . (قَوْلُهُ : فَضَرُورِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) أي : تُسمَّى بِمجموع هَذين اللفظين؛ لا ما يوهمه كلام الشَّارح مِن تسميتها بكلٍّ منهما، حيث قال : (وإنَّما سُمِّيَتْ... إلخ)، وإلا ; فَاللائقُ أنْ يقول : وإِنَّما سُمِّيَتْ ضَروريَّةٌ مُطلقةٌ لِكَذا وكذا (١) (قولُهُ: وَمَا قيلَ... إلخ) قائله العصام .ا.هـ.
(Absolute necessary).  Al-Dasuqi (His saying: 'absolute necessary') He only presented it because it is more specific than everything after it, and because the establishment of God's perfect attributes are all conditioned by necessity.  Al-Attar: Conversely, this is only valid in affirmative, but in negative; they are equal; because if the predicate is negated from the subject as long as its essence exists; it is negated from it eternally and forever; due to the impossibility of its affirmation for it in the state of non-existence. And al-Jalal after quoting this speech; examined it: if the meaning of absolute necessity is what was mentioned; then it would necessarily not be true except in the matter of eternal necessity, so it would not be more general than it; because if the existence of the subject is not necessary at the time of its existence; then the affirmation of the predicate to it would not be necessary at that time, and this is apparent. End. And Abd al-Hakim said: The meaning of 'as long as the essence of the subject exists' is that the times of its existence are a context for necessity, not a condition; so the mentioned example does not apply; because the necessity in it is conditional upon existence, not in the time of existence. As for the objection raised that then it would necessitate restricting essential necessity to the eternal because it is only true for the necessary subject or the impossible because as long as its existence is not necessary; nothing is necessary for it in any time of its existence; it is repelled by the fact that the affirmation of essential attributes to the essence is necessary in the time of its existence not conditional upon existence; like: Every human is an animal necessarily, for the essential is prior to the essence in existence and non-existence. And what was before (1) in the answer: 'Zayd exists' is a mental proposition, and the discussion is about real and external propositions, so it does not resolve the material of the objection; because every external or mental proposition whose predicate is existence gives rise to an objection, for the predicate is necessarily affirmed as long as the subject exists.  (His saying: 'absolute necessary') i.e., it is called by the combination of these two words; not as the Sharh's statement implies that it is called by each of them separately, where he said: 'and it is only called... etc.', otherwise; it would be appropriate for him to say: 'and it is only called absolute necessary for such and such'  (1) (His saying: 'and what was said... etc.') Its speaker is al-Isam. End.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the term 'absolute necessary proposition' (ضرورية مطلقة). It begins with al-Dasuqi explaining why the author (the matn) presented this type first: because it is the most specific among the types of necessary propositions, and because divine perfect attributes are all established by necessity. Then al-Attar adds that the equality of affirmative and negative forms holds only in affirmative propositions; in negative propositions, the negation of a predicate from a subject as long as the subject's essence exists means the predicate is negated eternally, since it cannot be affirmed when the subject does not exist. Al-Jalal then critiques that if absolute necessity means necessity as long as the subject exists, then it would only apply to eternal necessity (like God), and thus would not be more general. Abd al-Hakim responds by clarifying that the phrase 'as long as the subject's essence exists' indicates the time frame for necessity, not a conditional restriction; necessity is in the time of existence, not conditional upon existence. He uses the example of essential attributes (like 'Every human is an animal') where the predicate is necessarily true in the time of the subject's existence without requiring the subject's existence as a condition. The discussion then addresses an objection about mental propositions like 'Zayd exists', which are also necessary as long as Zayd exists, and argues that such propositions are mental, not real, so they do not refute the point. Finally, al-Dasuqi corrects a misunderstanding: the term 'absolute necessary' refers to the combination of both words, not to each word separately as the Sharh might imply.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة
ما دام ذات الموضوع موجوداً
ضرورة أزلية

C) It applies only to eternal subjects like God.
D) It applies only to mental propositions.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'as long as the subject exists' in absolute necessary propositions specifies the time frame of necessity, not a restrictive condition; necessity is in the time of existence, not conditional upon existence.

Defining the General Conditional Proposition
Definition of the general conditional and contrast with absolute necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general conditional proposition from the absolute necessary proposition and identify its defining condition.

Core Idea
The general conditional ties necessity to a description, not to the essence.

This passage introduces the second type of necessary proposition: the general conditional. Unlike the absolute necessary, which holds regardless of any attribute, the general conditional requires that the subject's description be present for the necessity to apply. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions in Islamic logic and for building sound arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessary propositions
The passage first describes the absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة) where the judgment is not restricted by description or time. Then it introduces the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) where necessity depends on a description.
Look for the contrast between 'غير مقيد بوصف أو وقت' and 'بشرط وصف الموضوع'.
2
Extract the condition for the general conditional
The definition states: the judgment is necessary 'as long as the description of the subject exists' (ما دام وصف الموضوع). This is the key differentia: necessity is conditioned by the persistence of a specific attribute.
The phrase 'بشرط وصف الموضوع' is used interchangeably with 'ما دام وصفه'.
3
Contrast with the absolute necessary
In the absolute necessary, necessity holds purely based on the subject's essence (e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' as long as the human essence exists). The general conditional adds a conditional layer: the description must be present.
For example, 'Every writer is moving necessarily' only holds while the subject is writing.
4
Apply the definition to an Islamic example
Consider the fiqh ruling: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden necessarily as long as it intoxicates.' Here, the necessity of forbiddance is tied to the description 'intoxicating'. This is a clear case of general conditional.
If the description is absent, the necessity no longer applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إنَّما سميت ضروريَّةٌ ; لاشتمالها على الضَّرورة، وإنَّما سمّيت مطلقةً ; لأنَّ الحكم فيها غيرُ مقيَّد بوصف أو وقتٍ كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنَّ ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان وسلب الحجريَّة عنه ضروري ما دام ذاتُ الإنسان موجودةً . ثانيًا: [المشروطة العامة ]: أَوْ مَا دَامَ وَصْفُهُ) عطفٌ على قوله : ما دام ذاتُ الموضوع»؛ أي : إن كانَ الحكم بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع موجوداً؛ أي: بشرط وصفِ الموضوع ; (فَمَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ) . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الضَّرُورَةِ) أي: لفظاً في القضيَّةِ اللفظية، وحكم العقل بالضرورة في القضيَّةِ العقلية. (قَوْلُهُ : بِالضَّرُوْرَةِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَا فرق بين تقديم لفظ بالضَّرورة أو تأخيرِهِ (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: بِشَرْطِ ... إِلَخ) يقتضي أنَّ المراد بالمشروطة العامَّةِ أحد المعنيين الآتيين، وهو المعنى الأوَّلُ الآتي، وقوله في آخِرِ السَّوادة: واعلَمْ أَنَّ مَا ذكره المصنِّفُ في تعريف المشروطة؛ مُحتمل لكِلا المعنيين إلخ، يُنافي ذلك العطار (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الحُكْمَ فِيْهَا غَيْرُ مُقَيَّدٍ ... إِلَخ) وأمَّا ذِكْرُ مَا دَامَ الذَّاتُ ; فَلِإبقاءِ الضَّرورةِ على عُمومِهَا ; لا لِلتَّقييدِ ، قالَهُ العِصامُ، وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّ هذا التوجيه مبني على عدم الفرق بين اعتبار القيدِ في المفهوم وفيما صدق عليه المفهوم ولم يفهم أنَّهُ في التَّعريفِ لِلإخراج، فكيف لا يكونُ تَقييداً؟ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِ الْمَوْضُوع سيأتي لهُ أنَّ المشروطة قد تُقَالُ على الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورة النّسبة في جميع أوقاتِ ثبوتِ الوصف للموضوع، وحينئذ ; فَالمناسب أن يقول هنا :أي بشرط وصف الموضوع، أو في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع، إلَّا أنَّهُ راعى ظاهر كلام المصنفِ ; حيثُ اقتصر على المعنى الأوَّلِ، وإن قال أَخِراً : إنّ كلامه يحتملُ كِلَا المعنيين.
Chapter on the Definition and its Divisions.   It is only called 'necessary' because it includes necessity, and it is only called 'absolute' because the judgment in it is not restricted by a description or time, like our saying: Every human is an animal necessarily, and Nothing of human is a stone necessarily, for the affirmation of animality for human and the negation of stoneness from it is necessary as long as the human's essence exists.  Second: [The General Conditional]: 'Or as long as its description' is a conjunction to his saying 'as long as the subject's essence'; i.e., if the judgment is with necessity of the relation, as long as the description of the subject exists; i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject; (then it is general conditional).  Al-Dasuqi (His saying: 'because it includes necessity') i.e., in wording in the verbal proposition, and the mind's judgment of necessity in the mental proposition.  (His saying: 'necessarily every human is an animal' no difference between advancing the word 'necessarily' or delaying it.  (His saying: 'i.e., conditioned by... etc.') implies that what is meant by the general conditional is one of the two meanings to follow, which is the first meaning to follow, and his saying at the end of the margin: 'Know that what the author mentioned in the definition of the conditional is probable for both meanings etc.' contradicts that.  Al-Attar (His saying: 'because the judgment in it is not restricted... etc.') And as for mentioning 'as long as the essence'; it is to preserve the generality of necessity; not for restriction, said by al-Isam, and Abd al-Hakim refuted it by saying that this orientation is based on not distinguishing between considering the restriction in the concept and in what the concept applies to, and he did not understand that it is in the definition for exclusion, so how is it not a restriction?  (His saying: 'i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject' it will come for him that the conditional may be said about the one in which the judgment is with necessity of the relation in all times of the existence of the description for the subject, and then it is appropriate for him to say here: i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject, or in all times of the description of the subject, except that he observed the apparent meaning of the author's statement; where he restricted to the first meaning, although he said later: 'His statement is probable for both meanings.'
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a new section (fasl) on the definition and its divisions, focusing on types of necessary propositions. It first explains why the absolute necessary proposition is so named: it is called 'necessary' because it includes the modality of necessity, and 'absolute' because the judgment is not restricted by any description or time. Example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' where the necessity holds as long as the human's essence exists. Then it defines the second type: the general conditional (المشروطة العامة). This is where the judgment is necessary as long as a particular description of the subject exists, i.e., conditioned by the subject's description. For example, if we say 'Every writing thing is moving necessarily' as long as it is writing. The commentary then clarifies details: al-Dasuqi explains that 'including necessity' refers to both the verbal expression of necessity and the mental judgment. Then a note that the order of words like 'necessarily' does not matter. Al-Attar points out that the phrase 'as long as the essence' in the absolute necessary is meant to preserve generality, not to restrict, but Abd al-Hakim refutes this, arguing that in definition it serves to exclude, thus it is a restriction. Finally, there is discussion that the general conditional can have two meanings: one where necessity holds only at the time the description exists, and another where it holds in all times the description exists. The author is careful to align with the first meaning but later acknowledges both possibilities.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
بشرط وصف الموضوع
ما دام وصفه

Key Takeaway
A general conditional proposition is a necessary proposition where the necessity is conditioned by a description of the subject, not by its mere essence.

Two Interpretations of the General Conditional
Commentary debate on the two possible meanings of the general conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of the general conditional and explain the dispute among commentators regarding its definition.

Core Idea
One general conditional can mean necessity only at the moment, another means necessity throughout the description's duration.

The commentary on this passage reveals that the general conditional can be understood in two ways: either the necessity holds only at the time the description exists, or it holds in all times the description exists. This ambiguity led to a debate among logicians about the correct interpretation. Understanding this nuance is essential for precise logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's definition of the general conditional
The matn defines the general conditional as a necessity 'as long as the description exists' (ما دام وصفه). This concise statement gives rise to two possible readings.
The definition is: 'إذا كان الحكم بضرورة النسبة ما دام وصف الموضوع موجوداً'.
2
Identify the first interpretation
The first interpretation (al-ma'na al-awwal) takes the condition strictly: necessity holds exactly at the time when the description is present. It does not extend to other times when the description might be absent.
Example: 'Every writer is moving as long as he writes' means only during the act of writing.
3
Identify the second interpretation
The second interpretation expands the condition: necessity holds in all times that the description exists, meaning that if the description persists over time, necessity persists throughout that duration. This is a more inclusive reading.
Example: 'Every traveler is permitted to shorten prayer as long as he is traveling' applies continuously during the travel period.
4
Trace the commentator debate
Al-Dasuqi notes that the author's wording is ambiguous. Al-Attar points out a potential contradiction: early in the commentary, the author seems to restrict to one meaning, but later acknowledges both. Abd al-Hakim and al-Isam debate whether the phrase 'as long as the essence' in the absolute necessary is for generality or restriction.
This debate shows the importance of precise definition in logical terminology.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ إنَّما سميت ضروريَّةٌ ; لاشتمالها على الضَّرورة، وإنَّما سمّيت مطلقةً ; لأنَّ الحكم فيها غيرُ مقيَّد بوصف أو وقتٍ كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة، فإنَّ ثبوت الحيوانية للإنسان وسلب الحجريَّة عنه ضروري ما دام ذاتُ الإنسان موجودةً . ثانيًا: [المشروطة العامة ]: أَوْ مَا دَامَ وَصْفُهُ) عطفٌ على قوله : ما دام ذاتُ الموضوع»؛ أي : إن كانَ الحكم بضرورةِ النِّسبةِ، ما دامَ وصفُ الموضوع موجوداً؛ أي: بشرط وصفِ الموضوع ; (فَمَشْرُوطَةٌ عَامَّةٌ) . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لاشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الضَّرُورَةِ) أي: لفظاً في القضيَّةِ اللفظية، وحكم العقل بالضرورة في القضيَّةِ العقلية. (قَوْلُهُ : بِالضَّرُوْرَةِ كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ لَا فرق بين تقديم لفظ بالضَّرورة أو تأخيرِهِ (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: بِشَرْطِ ... إِلَخ) يقتضي أنَّ المراد بالمشروطة العامَّةِ أحد المعنيين الآتيين، وهو المعنى الأوَّلُ الآتي، وقوله في آخِرِ السَّوادة: واعلَمْ أَنَّ مَا ذكره المصنِّفُ في تعريف المشروطة؛ مُحتمل لكِلا المعنيين إلخ، يُنافي ذلك العطار (قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّ الحُكْمَ فِيْهَا غَيْرُ مُقَيَّدٍ ... إِلَخ) وأمَّا ذِكْرُ مَا دَامَ الذَّاتُ ; فَلِإبقاءِ الضَّرورةِ على عُمومِهَا ; لا لِلتَّقييدِ ، قالَهُ العِصامُ، وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّ هذا التوجيه مبني على عدم الفرق بين اعتبار القيدِ في المفهوم وفيما صدق عليه المفهوم ولم يفهم أنَّهُ في التَّعريفِ لِلإخراج، فكيف لا يكونُ تَقييداً؟ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ بِشَرْطِ وَصْفِ الْمَوْضُوع سيأتي لهُ أنَّ المشروطة قد تُقَالُ على الَّتي حكم فيها بضرورة النّسبة في جميع أوقاتِ ثبوتِ الوصف للموضوع، وحينئذ ; فَالمناسب أن يقول هنا :أي بشرط وصف الموضوع، أو في جميع أوقات وصف الموضوع، إلَّا أنَّهُ راعى ظاهر كلام المصنفِ ; حيثُ اقتصر على المعنى الأوَّلِ، وإن قال أَخِراً : إنّ كلامه يحتملُ كِلَا المعنيين.
Chapter on the Definition and its Divisions.   It is only called 'necessary' because it includes necessity, and it is only called 'absolute' because the judgment in it is not restricted by a description or time, like our saying: Every human is an animal necessarily, and Nothing of human is a stone necessarily, for the affirmation of animality for human and the negation of stoneness from it is necessary as long as the human's essence exists.  Second: [The General Conditional]: 'Or as long as its description' is a conjunction to his saying 'as long as the subject's essence'; i.e., if the judgment is with necessity of the relation, as long as the description of the subject exists; i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject; (then it is general conditional).  Al-Dasuqi (His saying: 'because it includes necessity') i.e., in wording in the verbal proposition, and the mind's judgment of necessity in the mental proposition.  (His saying: 'necessarily every human is an animal' no difference between advancing the word 'necessarily' or delaying it.  (His saying: 'i.e., conditioned by... etc.') implies that what is meant by the general conditional is one of the two meanings to follow, which is the first meaning to follow, and his saying at the end of the margin: 'Know that what the author mentioned in the definition of the conditional is probable for both meanings etc.' contradicts that.  Al-Attar (His saying: 'because the judgment in it is not restricted... etc.') And as for mentioning 'as long as the essence'; it is to preserve the generality of necessity; not for restriction, said by al-Isam, and Abd al-Hakim refuted it by saying that this orientation is based on not distinguishing between considering the restriction in the concept and in what the concept applies to, and he did not understand that it is in the definition for exclusion, so how is it not a restriction?  (His saying: 'i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject' it will come for him that the conditional may be said about the one in which the judgment is with necessity of the relation in all times of the existence of the description for the subject, and then it is appropriate for him to say here: i.e., conditioned by the description of the subject, or in all times of the description of the subject, except that he observed the apparent meaning of the author's statement; where he restricted to the first meaning, although he said later: 'His statement is probable for both meanings.'
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage is a new section (fasl) on the definition and its divisions, focusing on types of necessary propositions. It first explains why the absolute necessary proposition is so named: it is called 'necessary' because it includes the modality of necessity, and 'absolute' because the judgment is not restricted by any description or time. Example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' where the necessity holds as long as the human's essence exists. Then it defines the second type: the general conditional (المشروطة العامة). This is where the judgment is necessary as long as a particular description of the subject exists, i.e., conditioned by the subject's description. For example, if we say 'Every writing thing is moving necessarily' as long as it is writing. The commentary then clarifies details: al-Dasuqi explains that 'including necessity' refers to both the verbal expression of necessity and the mental judgment. Then a note that the order of words like 'necessarily' does not matter. Al-Attar points out that the phrase 'as long as the essence' in the absolute necessary is meant to preserve generality, not to restrict, but Abd al-Hakim refutes this, arguing that in definition it serves to exclude, thus it is a restriction. Finally, there is discussion that the general conditional can have two meanings: one where necessity holds only at the time the description exists, and another where it holds in all times the description exists. The author is careful to align with the first meaning but later acknowledges both possibilities.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
بشرط وصف الموضوع
ما دام وصفه

A) Necessity holds only at the moment the description appears.
B) Necessity holds throughout all times the description exists.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional can be interpreted narrowly (necessity at the time of description) or broadly (necessity throughout the description's duration), and commentators debated which interpretation the original definition intended.

The Four Divisions of Subject and Predicate
Author explains the four rational divisions of subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct combination of subject and predicate intended in a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
In predication, we always intend the individuals of the subject and the concept of the predicate.

This passage explains why only one out of four possible combinations of subject and predicate is valid for scientific propositions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing correct definitions and arguments in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four possible divisions
List the four combinations: individuals of subject, concept of subject, individuals of predicate, concept of predicate. The possible pairings are: (subject individuals, predicate individuals), (subject individuals, predicate concept), (subject concept, predicate individuals), (subject concept, predicate concept).
2
Examine the invalid combinations
If we intend the concept of the subject, the proposition becomes natural (طبيعية) and indefinite, which is unsuitable for sciences. If we intend individuals of both, then if the predicate is broader, it entails predicating something different on itself; if equivalent, it is self-predication, which is uninformative.
For example, 'Every human is an animal' with both individuals would predicate 'animal' (which includes horses) on 'human' individuals, which is not a proper predication.
3
Determine the valid combination
The only valid combination is individuals of the subject and concept of the predicate. This avoids natural predication and self-predication, and yields a universal proposition that is informative.
Thus in 'Every human is an animal', 'human' refers to its individuals (e.g., Zayd) and 'animal' refers to its concept (the essence of animal).
4
Apply to an example from fiqh
Consider 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. The subject 'intoxicant' must refer to its individuals (wine, beer, etc.) and the predicate 'forbidden' to its concept (the legal ruling). This ensures the proposition is a proper universal.
If we mistakenly took the concept of 'intoxicant', the proposition would be natural and not universal.

واعلمْ أنَّ ما صَدَقَ عليه الموضوع (قَوْلُهُ : وَاعْلَمْ . . . إِلَخ غرضُهُ الفرقُ بينَ الذَّاتِ والوصف الواقعين في المتن؛ أي : في قولِهِ : مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع أو ما دامَ وَصفه، فهو مُتعلّق بالمتن (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ مَا صَدَقَ ... إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ المعتبر عندَهُمْ مِنَ الموضوع ؛ الأفرادُ، وَمِنَ المحمول ؛ المفهوم أي الماهيَّة، وإنْ كانَتِ القسمة العقلية أربعةً ؛
(قَوْلُهُ : مَا صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ الْمَوْضُوْعُ) أي : اتَّصفَ بِهِ، وقد اختلفَ الشَّيخانِ في اتِّصافِ الذَّاتِ بالعنوانِ؛ فقال الفارابي : إِنَّهُ بالإمكان المقابل للامتناع ؛ لا بمعنى القوَّةِ المقابل للفعلِ لَا مُجرَّد الفرضِ ؛ حتّى لا يدخل الحجرُ في : كلَّ إنسان حيوان مثلاً ، وبالفعل عندَ الشَّيخِ الرَّئيس لا بحسب الخارج ؛ بل بأنْ يفرضَهُ العقل بالفعل، فإذا
(1) (قَوْلُ الشَّارح : واعلم ... إلخ) عبارة الشارح فيها خفاء أوجب ارتباك الحواشي في فهمها بتقدير مضافات أو اعتبار مجازات ونحن نكشف عنها النقاب بتقديم مقدمة فنقول : القضية لها طرفان . الموضوع ،والمحمول، ولكلِّ منهما أفراد ووصف عنواني ينطبق عليها انطباق الكلي على جزئياته، ومدلول هذا العنوان هو المفهوم لكل منهما، فالأقسام أربعة : أفراد الموضوع ومفهومه وأفراد المحمول ومفهومه مثلاً : كلَّ إنسان حيوان؛ الموضوع فيها وهو (إنسان) له أفراد كزيد وبكر ... إلخ، وهذه الأفراد معنونة باللفظ الدال عليها وهو إنسان ومفهومه حيوان ناطق والمحمول فيها وهو (حيوان) له أفراد كإنسان وفرس... إلخ، وهذه الأفراد معنونة باللفظ الدال عليها وهو حيوان ومفهومه جسم نامي ... إلخ، ولا يصح وقت الحمل إرادة المفهوم من الموضوع ؛ سواء أريد مفهوم المحمول أيضاً أو أريد أفراده؛ لأنَّ القضية تكون طبيعية وهي مهملة في العلوم كما لا يصح أن يراد به وبالمحمول أفرادهما لما يلزم عليه من حمل الشيء ومباينه على نفسه إن كان المحمول أعم كالمثال المذكور أو حمله على نفسه إن كان مساوياً نحو كل إنسان متكلم فتعين القسم الرابع وهو أن يراد بالموضوع أفراده وبالمحمول مفهومه. إذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الوصف العنواني للموضوع ينقسم باعتبار مفهومه ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: أن يكون نوعاً إن كان تمام ماهية الأفراد نحو كل إنسان حيوان الثَّاني : أن يكون جزءاً من ماهية أفراده على أنَّه جنس إن كانت مختلفة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ حيوان حساس، أو فصل إن كانت منفقة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ناطق إنسان. الثَّالث: أن يكون خارجاً عن ماهية الأفراد على أنَّه خاصة إن كانت أفراده متفقة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ضاحك متعجب، أو عرض عام إن كانت مختلفة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ماش. حيوان. والداعي لهذا كله قول المتن: ما دام ذات الموضوع، وقوله: ما دام وصفه، فإنه في الأولى ليس للوصف العنواني دخل في ضرورة النسبة ، وفي الثاني له دخل . ا.هـ.
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions. Know that what the subject applies to... (His saying: 'Know...' etc., his purpose is to distinguish between the essence (dhat) and the description (wasf) occurring in the text; i.e., in his saying: 'as long as the essence of the subject' or 'as long as its description'; so it relates to the text. His saying: 'that what applies to the subject... etc.' indicates that what is considered by them from the subject is the individuals, and from the predicate is the concept (essence), even though the rational division is fourfold. His saying: 'what the subject applies to' i.e., that which is described by it, and the two masters (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) differed regarding the description of the essence by the title. Al-Farabi said it is by possibility opposed to impossibility, not in the sense of potentiality opposed to actuality, nor mere supposition; so that stone is not included in 'every human is an animal' for example. And according to the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) it is by actuality, not according to external existence, but by the mind's postulating it as actual. Then... (1) (The commentator's saying: 'Know... etc.') The commentator's expression is obscure, causing confusion among the glosses in understanding it, by assuming additional elements or considering metaphors. We will unveil it by providing a preface: The proposition has two sides: subject and predicate, and each has individuals and a descriptive term that applies to them as the universal applies to its particulars, and the denotation of this term is the concept for each. So the divisions are four: individuals of the subject and its concept, individuals of the predicate and its concept. For example: 'Every human is an animal'; the subject (human) has individuals like Zayd, Bakr, etc., and these individuals are named by the word indicating them which is 'human', and its concept is 'rational animal'. The predicate (animal) has individuals like human, horse, etc., and these individuals are named by the word indicating them which is 'animal', and its concept is 'growing body'. During predication, it is not valid to intend the concept from the subject, whether the concept of the predicate is also intended or its individuals; because the proposition would be natural and it is indefinite in the sciences. Nor is it valid to intend from both (subject and predicate) their individuals, because that would entail predication of the thing and its different on itself if the predicate is broader, as in the example, or its self-predication if it is equivalent, like 'every human is rational'. So the fourth division is determined: that the individuals of the subject and the concept of the predicate are intended. If you know that, then know that the descriptive term of the subject, in terms of its concept, divides into three sections: First: that it is a species if it is the complete essence of the individuals, like 'every human is an animal'. Second: that it is a part of the essence of its individuals, as a genus if they are of different realities, like 'every animal is sentient', or as a differentia if they are of agreed reality, like 'every rational is human'. Third: that it is outside the essence of the individuals, as a property if its individuals are of agreed reality, like 'every laughing being is wondering', or as a general accident if they are of different realities, like 'every walking being is an animal'. The reason for all this is the text's saying: 'as long as the essence of the subject' and his saying: 'as long as its description'. For in the first, the descriptive term has no role in the necessity of the relation; in the second it has a role. End.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on definition (المعرف) and its divisions, though it primarily discusses the structure of propositions and the distinction between essence and description. The author begins by setting the purpose: to differentiate between the subject's essence (ذات) and its description (وصف) as used in the earlier text. He emphasizes that in logical predication, what matters from the subject is its individuals (أفراد), while from the predicate it is its concept (مفهوم) or essence (ماهية). This is despite the fact that conceptually there are four possible combinations: subject individuals, subject concept, predicate individuals, predicate concept. The passage then explains why all but one combination are invalid. If we intend the concept of the subject, the proposition becomes 'natural' (طبيعية) and indefinite, which is unsuitable for sciences. If we intend individuals from both subject and predicate, then if the predicate is broader (like 'animal' vs 'human'), we would be predicated something different on itself; if equivalent (like 'human' and 'rational'), we would be predicating the same on itself, which is uninformative. Thus only the fourth combination remains: individuals of subject and concept of predicate. Next, the passage classifies the descriptive term of the subject into three types based on its relation to the essence of the individuals: (1) it may be the complete essence (species), e.g., 'human' in 'every human is an animal'; (2) a part of the essence (genus or differentia), e.g., 'animal' as genus or 'rational' as differentia; (3) outside the essence (property or general accident), e.g., 'laughing' or 'walking'. This classification explains the two phrases 'as long as the essence of the subject' and 'as long as its description': in the first, the descriptive term does not affect the necessity of the relation (since it is the essence itself), while in the second it does (since it is an external description). The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies the ambiguity of the original sharh and provides this detailed breakdown. This passage is foundational for understanding how definitions (حدود) are constructed and how propositions are correctly interpreted in logic.

الوصف العنواني

The Three Types of Descriptive Term
Author categorises the descriptive term into three types based on its relation to the essence of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a descriptive term as species, genus, differentia, property, or general accident based on its relation to the essence of its individuals.

Core Idea
A descriptive term may be the complete essence, part of it, or external to it.

This passage categorises the descriptive term (al-wasf al-'unwani) of the subject into three types depending on its relation to the essence of the individuals. This classification is essential for building correct definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm) in logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three relations
The descriptive term can be: (1) the complete essence of the individuals – that is the species; (2) a part of the essence – either as genus (if individuals differ in reality) or differentia (if they share reality); (3) external to the essence – either as property (if individuals share reality) or general accident (if they differ).
For example, 'human' is the species for humans; 'animal' is the genus; 'rational' is the differentia; 'laughing' is a property; 'walking' is a general accident.
2
Distinguish genus vs differentia
Genus applies when the individuals covered by the term have different ultimate realities (e.g., 'animal' applies to humans, horses, etc., which are different species). Differentia applies when the individuals have the same reality (e.g., 'rational' applies only to humans, all of whom share the same essence).
Thus 'every animal is sentient' uses 'animal' as a genus (part of essence), while 'every rational is human' uses 'rational' as a differentia.
3
Distinguish property vs general accident
Property (خاصة) applies when the individuals share the same reality (e.g., 'laughing' is a property of humans). General accident (عرض عام) applies when individuals differ in reality (e.g., 'walking' applies to humans, horses, etc., which are different species).
Both are outside the essence, but property is coextensive with the species, while general accident is broader.
4
Apply the classification to a definition
In the definition 'human is a rational animal', 'animal' is the genus (part of essence, different reality for individuals) and 'rational' is the differentia (part of essence, same reality). This gives a complete definition (hadd).
If we said 'human is a laughing animal', 'laughing' is a property (external), which would be a description (rasm), not a definition.

Source Text

واعلمْ أنَّ ما صَدَقَ عليه الموضوع (قَوْلُهُ : وَاعْلَمْ . . . إِلَخ غرضُهُ الفرقُ بينَ الذَّاتِ والوصف الواقعين في المتن؛ أي : في قولِهِ : مَا دامَ ذات الموضوع أو ما دامَ وَصفه، فهو مُتعلّق بالمتن (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ مَا صَدَقَ ... إلخ) فيه إشارة إلى أنَّ المعتبر عندَهُمْ مِنَ الموضوع ؛ الأفرادُ، وَمِنَ المحمول ؛ المفهوم أي الماهيَّة، وإنْ كانَتِ القسمة العقلية أربعةً ؛
(قَوْلُهُ : مَا صَدَقَ عَلَيْهِ الْمَوْضُوْعُ) أي : اتَّصفَ بِهِ، وقد اختلفَ الشَّيخانِ في اتِّصافِ الذَّاتِ بالعنوانِ؛ فقال الفارابي : إِنَّهُ بالإمكان المقابل للامتناع ؛ لا بمعنى القوَّةِ المقابل للفعلِ لَا مُجرَّد الفرضِ ؛ حتّى لا يدخل الحجرُ في : كلَّ إنسان حيوان مثلاً ، وبالفعل عندَ الشَّيخِ الرَّئيس لا بحسب الخارج ؛ بل بأنْ يفرضَهُ العقل بالفعل، فإذا
(1) (قَوْلُ الشَّارح : واعلم ... إلخ) عبارة الشارح فيها خفاء أوجب ارتباك الحواشي في فهمها بتقدير مضافات أو اعتبار مجازات ونحن نكشف عنها النقاب بتقديم مقدمة فنقول : القضية لها طرفان . الموضوع ،والمحمول، ولكلِّ منهما أفراد ووصف عنواني ينطبق عليها انطباق الكلي على جزئياته، ومدلول هذا العنوان هو المفهوم لكل منهما، فالأقسام أربعة : أفراد الموضوع ومفهومه وأفراد المحمول ومفهومه مثلاً : كلَّ إنسان حيوان؛ الموضوع فيها وهو (إنسان) له أفراد كزيد وبكر ... إلخ، وهذه الأفراد معنونة باللفظ الدال عليها وهو إنسان ومفهومه حيوان ناطق والمحمول فيها وهو (حيوان) له أفراد كإنسان وفرس... إلخ، وهذه الأفراد معنونة باللفظ الدال عليها وهو حيوان ومفهومه جسم نامي ... إلخ، ولا يصح وقت الحمل إرادة المفهوم من الموضوع ؛ سواء أريد مفهوم المحمول أيضاً أو أريد أفراده؛ لأنَّ القضية تكون طبيعية وهي مهملة في العلوم كما لا يصح أن يراد به وبالمحمول أفرادهما لما يلزم عليه من حمل الشيء ومباينه على نفسه إن كان المحمول أعم كالمثال المذكور أو حمله على نفسه إن كان مساوياً نحو كل إنسان متكلم فتعين القسم الرابع وهو أن يراد بالموضوع أفراده وبالمحمول مفهومه. إذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن الوصف العنواني للموضوع ينقسم باعتبار مفهومه ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: أن يكون نوعاً إن كان تمام ماهية الأفراد نحو كل إنسان حيوان الثَّاني : أن يكون جزءاً من ماهية أفراده على أنَّه جنس إن كانت مختلفة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ حيوان حساس، أو فصل إن كانت منفقة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ناطق إنسان. الثَّالث: أن يكون خارجاً عن ماهية الأفراد على أنَّه خاصة إن كانت أفراده متفقة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ضاحك متعجب، أو عرض عام إن كانت مختلفة الحقيقة نحو : كلُّ ماش. حيوان. والداعي لهذا كله قول المتن: ما دام ذات الموضوع، وقوله: ما دام وصفه، فإنه في الأولى ليس للوصف العنواني دخل في ضرورة النسبة ، وفي الثاني له دخل . ا.هـ.
Chapter: On the Definition and Its Divisions. Know that what the subject applies to... (His saying: 'Know...' etc., his purpose is to distinguish between the essence (dhat) and the description (wasf) occurring in the text; i.e., in his saying: 'as long as the essence of the subject' or 'as long as its description'; so it relates to the text. His saying: 'that what applies to the subject... etc.' indicates that what is considered by them from the subject is the individuals, and from the predicate is the concept (essence), even though the rational division is fourfold. His saying: 'what the subject applies to' i.e., that which is described by it, and the two masters (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) differed regarding the description of the essence by the title. Al-Farabi said it is by possibility opposed to impossibility, not in the sense of potentiality opposed to actuality, nor mere supposition; so that stone is not included in 'every human is an animal' for example. And according to the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) it is by actuality, not according to external existence, but by the mind's postulating it as actual. Then... (1) (The commentator's saying: 'Know... etc.') The commentator's expression is obscure, causing confusion among the glosses in understanding it, by assuming additional elements or considering metaphors. We will unveil it by providing a preface: The proposition has two sides: subject and predicate, and each has individuals and a descriptive term that applies to them as the universal applies to its particulars, and the denotation of this term is the concept for each. So the divisions are four: individuals of the subject and its concept, individuals of the predicate and its concept. For example: 'Every human is an animal'; the subject (human) has individuals like Zayd, Bakr, etc., and these individuals are named by the word indicating them which is 'human', and its concept is 'rational animal'. The predicate (animal) has individuals like human, horse, etc., and these individuals are named by the word indicating them which is 'animal', and its concept is 'growing body'. During predication, it is not valid to intend the concept from the subject, whether the concept of the predicate is also intended or its individuals; because the proposition would be natural and it is indefinite in the sciences. Nor is it valid to intend from both (subject and predicate) their individuals, because that would entail predication of the thing and its different on itself if the predicate is broader, as in the example, or its self-predication if it is equivalent, like 'every human is rational'. So the fourth division is determined: that the individuals of the subject and the concept of the predicate are intended. If you know that, then know that the descriptive term of the subject, in terms of its concept, divides into three sections: First: that it is a species if it is the complete essence of the individuals, like 'every human is an animal'. Second: that it is a part of the essence of its individuals, as a genus if they are of different realities, like 'every animal is sentient', or as a differentia if they are of agreed reality, like 'every rational is human'. Third: that it is outside the essence of the individuals, as a property if its individuals are of agreed reality, like 'every laughing being is wondering', or as a general accident if they are of different realities, like 'every walking being is an animal'. The reason for all this is the text's saying: 'as long as the essence of the subject' and his saying: 'as long as its description'. For in the first, the descriptive term has no role in the necessity of the relation; in the second it has a role. End.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on definition (المعرف) and its divisions, though it primarily discusses the structure of propositions and the distinction between essence and description. The author begins by setting the purpose: to differentiate between the subject's essence (ذات) and its description (وصف) as used in the earlier text. He emphasizes that in logical predication, what matters from the subject is its individuals (أفراد), while from the predicate it is its concept (مفهوم) or essence (ماهية). This is despite the fact that conceptually there are four possible combinations: subject individuals, subject concept, predicate individuals, predicate concept. The passage then explains why all but one combination are invalid. If we intend the concept of the subject, the proposition becomes 'natural' (طبيعية) and indefinite, which is unsuitable for sciences. If we intend individuals from both subject and predicate, then if the predicate is broader (like 'animal' vs 'human'), we would be predicated something different on itself; if equivalent (like 'human' and 'rational'), we would be predicating the same on itself, which is uninformative. Thus only the fourth combination remains: individuals of subject and concept of predicate. Next, the passage classifies the descriptive term of the subject into three types based on its relation to the essence of the individuals: (1) it may be the complete essence (species), e.g., 'human' in 'every human is an animal'; (2) a part of the essence (genus or differentia), e.g., 'animal' as genus or 'rational' as differentia; (3) outside the essence (property or general accident), e.g., 'laughing' or 'walking'. This classification explains the two phrases 'as long as the essence of the subject' and 'as long as its description': in the first, the descriptive term does not affect the necessity of the relation (since it is the essence itself), while in the second it does (since it is an external description). The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies the ambiguity of the original sharh and provides this detailed breakdown. This passage is foundational for understanding how definitions (حدود) are constructed and how propositions are correctly interpreted in logic.

The Three Types of Indicative Description
Author classifies the indicative description into three relations to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any indicative description as either identical to, part of, or external to the essence of its individuals.

Core Idea
The indicative description is a logical magnifying glass — it may be the whole, a part, or something outside the quiddity.

In logic, the 'indicative description' (al-wasf al-unwani) is the concept attributed to the subject. This lesson unpacks its three possible relationships to the essence (dhat) of the individuals it describes. The passage, enriched by the commentaries of al-Dasuqi and al-Attar, shows you how to distinguish when a predicate is identical to the quiddity, a part of it, or entirely external. Mastering this classification is essential for constructing sound definitions and evaluating propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The author states that the indicative description is either 'identical to the essence', 'a part of it', or 'external to it'. Read the first few lines to grasp this tripartite division.
2
Analyze the 'identical' example
Example: 'Every human is an animal'. Here 'human' is the indicative description and it is identical to the quiddity of its individuals (Zayd, Bakr, etc.). This occurs when the description is the species itself.
3
Analyze the 'part' examples
Examples: 'Every animal is sentient' (genus) and 'Every rational being is a human' (differentia). The concepts 'animal' and 'rational' are parts of the quiddity of their individuals, not the whole.
Dasuqi explains that 'animal' is a part of human quiddity because human is 'animal + rational'.
4
Track the ambiguity of 'dhat'
Dasuqi clarifies that in the first two cases 'dhat' means quiddity, while in the 'external' case it refers to the individuals. This contextual shift is crucial for proper understanding.
Attar further refines: the title (description) itself may be the species, genus, or differentia.

Source Text

الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَالْوَصْفُ الْعُنْوَانِيُّ) أي : الَّذي هُوَ مَفهوم الموضوع؛ قد يكون عين الذَّاتِ ؛ أي: عينَ ماهِيَّةِ الذَّاتِ ؛ أي: الأفراد ؛ بدليل قوله الآتي : (عين ماهيّةِ أفرادِهِ، أَوْ مُرادُهُ بالذَّاتِ هُنَا ؛ الماهيَّةُ لا الأفراد، نَعَمْ قولُهُ فيما بَعْدُ: (وقد يكونُ خارجاً عنه)؛ يعني: عَنِ الذّاتِ بمعنى الأفراد، فيكونُ ذِكْرُ الذَّاتِ أَوَّلاً بمعنى الماهية ، وأعادَ الضَّمير عليها بمعنى آخر . (قَوْلُهُ : مَفْهُوْمَ الْإِنْسَانِ) وهو حيوان ناطق .
(قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ يَكُونُ) أي: الوصْفُ العنواني .
(قَوْلُهُ: جُزْءَاً لَهُ) أي : لِذَاتِ الموضوع؛ أي: لِماهية ذاتِ الموضوع، فَفِي الكلام حذف مضاف، أو أراد بالذَّاتِ ؛ نَفْسَ الماهِيَّةِ ، وَكانَ الأَوْلَى تأنيتُ الضَّميرِ . (قَوْلُهُ : لِلْجِنْسِ ) أي : كَحيوانٍ في المثالِ الَّذي ذكرَهُ.
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوِ الْفَصْل) كَقولِكَ : كلُّ ناطق بشرٌ ، فَمَفهوم ناطق؛ مُتفكِّرُ بالقوَّةِ، والمتفكِّرُ بالقوَّةِ جزء مِن ماهِيَّةِ أفرادِ ناطق وهوَ : زيدٌ وَبكر ، وغيرهما . (قَوْلُهُ: مَفْهُوْمَ الْحَيَوَانِ) وهو جسم نام حَسَّاسٌ مُتحرِّكٌ بِالإرادةِ، وَلَا شَكٍّ أَنَّ هذا جزءٌ مِن ماهِيَّةِ الإنسان؛ لأنَّهُ يزادُ على هذا : مُتفكِّرُ بِالقوة.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يَكُونُ) أي: الوصْفُ العنواني.
قَوْلُهُ : عَنْهُ) أي : ذاتِ الموضوع؛ أي: عن ماهية ذاته.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ كَانَ عُنْوَانَاً لِلنَّوْعِ الأَوْلَى : إنْ كانَ العنوانُ النَّوعَ، وَكَذَا يُقالُ فيما بعدَهُ ؛ فإنَّ المعنونَ عنه : الأفراد، والعنوان تارةً يكون نوعاً لتلك الأفراد، وتارةً يكون جنساً لها ... إلخ .
And the indicative description may be identical to the essence, if it is a title for the species, as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal', for the concept of human is identical to the essence of its individuals. And it may be a part of it, if it is a title for the genus or the differentia, as in our saying: 'Every animal is sentient', for the concept of animal is part of the essence of its individuals. And it may be external to it. [Dasuqi's gloss]: (His saying: and the indicative description) i.e., that which is the concept of the subject; it may be identical to the essence; i.e., identical to the quiddity of the essence; i.e., the individuals, as evidenced by his later saying: (identical to the quiddity of its individuals), or his intended meaning by essence here is the quiddity, not the individuals; yes, his saying later: (and it may be external to it) means: external to the essence in the sense of the individuals, so the mention of essence first is in the sense of quiddity, and he referred back to it with a different meaning. (His saying: the concept of human) — it is a rational animal. (His saying: and it may be) i.e., the indicative description. (His saying: a part of it) i.e., of the essence of the subject; i.e., of the quiddity of the essence of the subject, so in the statement there is an omitted genitive, or he intended by essence the quiddity itself, and it would have been better to use the feminine pronoun. (His saying: for the genus) i.e., like animal in the example he mentioned. (His saying: or the differentia) like your saying: 'Every rational being is a human', so the concept of rational is a potential thinker, and the potential thinker is a part of the quiddity of the individuals of 'rational', namely Zayd, Bakr, and others. (His saying: the concept of animal) — it is a growing, sentient, volitionally moving body, and there is no doubt that this is a part of the quiddity of humans, because what is added to this is 'potential thinker'. (His saying: and it may be) i.e., the indicative description. (His saying: from it) i.e., the essence of the subject; i.e., from its quiddity. [Attar's gloss]: (His saying: if it is a title for the species) — better: if the title is the species, and likewise it is said regarding what follows; for the one titled is the individuals, and the title is sometimes a species for those individuals, and sometimes a genus for them, etc.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage defines the three possible relations of the 'indicative description' (al-wasf al-unwani) — the conceptual predicate that attributes something to the subject's individuals — to the essence (dhat) of those individuals. The author first states the three types: (1) identical to the essence, (2) part of it, and (3) external to it. Each is illustrated with examples from logic. The first type occurs when the description is the species itself, e.g., 'Every human is an animal': here the concept 'human' is exactly the quiddity (mahiyya) of its individuals (like Zayd and Bakr). The second type occurs when the description is the genus or differentia, e.g., 'Every animal is sentient': the concept 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of its individuals (since 'human' adds 'rational' to 'animal'). Similarly, if the description is a differentia like 'rational' in 'Every rational being is a human', its concept is part of the quiddity. The third type is when the description is external (outside the essence), but its full explanation is deferred. The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies that the term 'essence' (dhat) is used ambiguously: in the first two types, it refers to quiddity, while in the third type it refers to the individuals themselves. He also explains that the examples given for genus and differentia show that the description is a part of the quiddity, as 'animal' is part of 'human' and 'rational' adds to it. Al-Attar then refines the phrasing: 'if it is a title for the species' should be understood as 'if the title is the species' because the title (description) itself may be the species, genus, etc. The overall classification is foundational for understanding how predicates relate to subjects in propositions, a key topic in the logic of definition.

الذات
الماهية

Three Relations of the Descriptive Attribute to the Essence
Author classifies the descriptive attribute as identical to, part of, or external to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any descriptive attribute (wasf 'unwani) in a universal affirmative proposition as identical to the essence, a part of it, or external to it, using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The descriptive attribute is either the species, a genus/differentia, or an accidental property.

In logic, when we state a universal affirmative proposition like 'Every human is an animal,' the predicate (the descriptive attribute) has a specific relation to the essence of the subject. This relation is key to constructing correct definitions and avoiding logical errors. This passage from the matn, with commentaries by al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, explains the three possibilities: identity, partial containment, or complete externality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptive attribute
In any universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is animal'), the predicate term is the descriptive attribute. Locate the predicate.
2
Determine the universal type
Check if the predicate is a species (naw'), genus (jins), differentia (fasl), or an accidental property using the five universals framework.
Species is the most specific; genus is broader; differentia is distinctive; accident is non-essential.
3
Classify the relation
If the predicate is a species, it is identical to the essence. If it is a genus or differentia, it is a part of the essence. If it is a common accident or specific property, it is external.
The passage gives 'human' as species, 'animal' as genus, 'rational' as differentia.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Take a proposition from fiqh or kalam, e.g., 'Every mukallaf is accountable.' Determine the relation of 'accountable' to the essence of mukallaf.
Accountable is a property, not part of the definition of mukallaf, so it is external.

والوصفُ العنواني قد يكون عين الذَّاتِ؛ إنْ كانَ عنواناً للنوع، كَقولنا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان»، فإنَّ مفهوم الإنسان عين ماهية أفراده. : وقد يكون جزءاً له، إن كان عنواناً للجنس أو الفصل، كقولنا : كلُّ حيوان حسّاس، فإنَّ مفهوم الحيوان جزء ماهيّة أفراده. وقد يكون خارجاً عنه،
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: وَالْوَصْفُ الْعُنْوَانِيُّ) أي : الَّذي هُوَ مَفهوم الموضوع؛ قد يكون عين الذَّاتِ ؛ أي: عينَ ماهِيَّةِ الذَّاتِ ؛ أي: الأفراد ؛ بدليل قوله الآتي : (عين ماهيّةِ أفرادِهِ، أَوْ مُرادُهُ بالذَّاتِ هُنَا ؛ الماهيَّةُ لا الأفراد، نَعَمْ قولُهُ فيما بَعْدُ: (وقد يكونُ خارجاً عنه)؛ يعني: عَنِ الذّاتِ بمعنى الأفراد، فيكونُ ذِكْرُ الذَّاتِ أَوَّلاً بمعنى الماهية ، وأعادَ الضَّمير عليها بمعنى آخر . (قَوْلُهُ : مَفْهُوْمَ الْإِنْسَانِ) وهو حيوان ناطق . (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَدْ يَكُونُ) أي: الوصْفُ العنواني . (قَوْلُهُ: جُزْءَاً لَهُ) أي : لِذَاتِ الموضوع؛ أي: لِماهية ذاتِ الموضوع، فَفِي الكلام حذف مضاف، أو أراد بالذَّاتِ ؛ نَفْسَ الماهِيَّةِ ، وَكانَ الأَوْلَى تأنيتُ الضَّميرِ . (قَوْلُهُ : لِلْجِنْسِ ) أي : كَحيوانٍ في المثالِ الَّذي ذكرَهُ. (قَوْلُهُ : أَوِ الْفَصْل) كَقولِكَ : كلُّ ناطق بشرٌ ، فَمَفهوم ناطق؛ مُتفكِّرُ بالقوَّةِ، والمتفكِّرُ بالقوَّةِ جزء مِن ماهِيَّةِ أفرادِ ناطق وهوَ : زيدٌ وَبكر ، وغيرهما . (قَوْلُهُ: مَفْهُوْمَ الْحَيَوَانِ) وهو جسم نام حَسَّاسٌ مُتحرِّكٌ بِالإرادةِ، وَلَا شَكٍّ أَنَّ هذا جزءٌ مِن ماهِيَّةِ الإنسان؛ لأنَّهُ يزادُ على هذا : مُتفكِّرُ بِالقوة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَقَدْ يَكُونُ) أي: الوصفُ العنواني. قَوْلُهُ : عَنْهُ) أي : ذاتِ الموضوع؛ أي: عن ماهية ذاته.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ كَانَ عُنْوَانَاً لِلنَّوْعِ الأَوْلَى : إنْ كانَ العنوانُ النَّوعَ، وَكَذَا يُقالُ فيما بعدَهُ ؛ فإنَّ المعنونَ عنه : الأفراد، والعنوان تارةً يكون نوعاً لتلك الأفراد، وتارةً يكون جنساً لها ... إلخ .
And the descriptive attribute may be identical to the essence: if it is a name for the species, as we say 'Every human is an animal,' for the concept of 'human' is identical to the quiddity of its individuals. And it may be a part of it, if it is a name for the genus or differentia, as we say 'Every animal is sentient,' for the concept of 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of its individuals. And it may be external to it.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'and the descriptive attribute') meaning: that which is the concept of the subject; it may be identical to the essence; i.e., identical to the quiddity of the essence; i.e., the individuals; as indicated by his subsequent statement: 'identical to the quiddity of its individuals,' or his intention with 'essence' here is the quiddity, not the individuals. Yes, his statement later: 'and it may be external to it' means: external to the essence in the sense of individuals, so his mention of 'essence' first is in the sense of quiddity, and he referred back to it with another meaning. (His statement: 'the concept of human') which is a rational animal. (His statement: 'and it may be') i.e., the descriptive attribute. (His statement: 'a part of it') i.e., for the essence of the subject; i.e., for the quiddity of the subject's essence; so in the statement there is an omitted annex, or he intended by 'essence' the quiddity itself, and it would have been better to feminize the pronoun. (His statement: 'for the genus') i.e., like 'animal' in the example he mentioned. (His statement: 'or the differentia') as you say: 'Every rational being is human,' so the concept of 'rational' is a potential thinker, and the potential thinker is a part of the quiddity of the individuals of 'rational,' namely Zayd, Bakr, and others. (His statement: 'the concept of 'animal') which is a growing, sentient, voluntarily moving body, and there is no doubt that this is a part of the quiddity of man, because to this is added: potential thinker. (His statement: 'and it may be') i.e., the descriptive attribute. (His statement: 'of it') i.e., the essence of the subject; i.e., concerning the quiddity of its essence.
Al-'Attar: (His statement: 'if it is a name for the species') i.e., if the attribute is the species; and similarly it is said in what follows; for the thing named is the individuals, and the attribute is sometimes a species for those individuals, and sometimes a genus for them... etc.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage defines the three possible relationships of a descriptive attribute (wasf 'unwani, i.e., the concept of the subject) to the essence (dhat or mahiyya) of the individuals it describes. The author states: (1) The attribute can be identical to the essence, as when the attribute is the species (naw'), e.g., 'human' in 'Every human is an animal'—the concept of human is exactly the quiddity of each individual human. (2) It can be a part of the essence, when the attribute is a genus (jins) or differentia (fasl), e.g., 'animal' in 'Every animal is sentient'—the concept of animal is a component of the quiddity of each animal. (3) It can be external to the essence, as when the attribute is a specific property (khassa) or common accident ('arad 'amm). The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies terminology: 'essence' in the first case refers to quiddity, but in the third case refers to individuals. He also expands the examples: 'human' = rational animal; 'rational' = potential thinker; 'animal' = growing, sentient, voluntarily moving body. Al-'Attar emphasizes that the attribute (title) is sometimes a species for the individuals, sometimes a genus, etc. The overall aim is to teach how to analyze predicates in propositions for logical definition.

الذات
النوع
الجنس
الفصل

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'Every human is an animal,' the descriptive attribute 'animal' is related to the essence of humans as:
A) Identical to the essence (species)
B) A part of the essence (genus)
C) External to the essence (accident)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The descriptive attribute in a proposition can be identical to the essence (species), a part of it (genus or differentia), or external to it (accident), and this classification is fundamental for logical definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every believer is responsible for zakah' and determine the relation of 'responsible for zakah' to the essence of believer. Is it identical, a part, or external? Use the three categories.
?
In the definition of 'wudu', the attribute 'cleansing with water' is considered. Is it a species, genus, differentia, or accident relative to the essence of wudu? Explain your reasoning.

Distinguishing Description From Essence in Definitions
Author explains that attributes like 'laugher' and 'walker' are external to the essence of the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an attribute in a definition is essential (dhati) or accidental (wasf) by applying the criterion of the attribute's relation to the quiddity.

Core Idea
Accidents like laughter and walking are not part of the quiddity; definitions must capture essence, not accidents.

This lesson tackles a foundational distinction: the difference between what a thing is (essence) and what it happens to have (description). In Islamic logic, a proper definition must be built from essential attributes, not accidental ones. The passage shows how attributes like 'laugher' and 'walker' are external to the essence of the subject, and teaches you to recognise this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attribute in the proposition
Look at the example given: 'Every laugher is an animal.' The attribute is 'laugher' (the ability to laugh). Determine what it describes.
The attribute is the predicate's basis, e.g., 'laugher' is the subject term.
2
Determine if the attribute is part of the quiddity
Ask: Is the attribute part of the quiddity (essence) of the individuals? For example, is laughter part of the essence of Zayd or Bakr? The author says no—it is external.
Quiddity (mahiyya) is what makes a thing what it is.
3
Apply the rule: external attributes are description, not essence
If the attribute is not part of the quiddity, it is an accidental description (wasf). Such propositions give a description, not a definition of essence.
The author contrasts this with definitions that use genus and differentia.
4
Distinguish between specific property and common accident
Note that 'laugher' is a specific property (khassa) unique to humans, while 'walker' is a common accident ('arad 'amm) shared by many species. Both are external to the essence.
This classification helps in evaluating definitions.

Source Text
