فَصْلٌ: فِي المُعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
إن كان عنواناً للخاصَّة أو العَرَضِ العام، كقولنا : «كل ضاحك أو كلُّ ماش حيوان»، فإنَّ مفهومَ الضَّاحِكِ والماشي خارج عن ذاتِ الموضوع؛ أي : أفراده. وبما ذكرنا يحصُلُ الفرقُ الجلي بين الوصفِ والذَّاتِ، فَلْيُتأَمَّلْ . وإِنَّما سُمِّيَتْ مشروطةً لاشتمالها على شرط الوصف، وعامَّةٌ ؛ لكونها أعم من المشروطة الخاصَّة التي ستعرفُها في المركَّبات .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ مَفْهُوْمَ الصَّاحِكِ) وهو الضَّحِكُ، وقولُهُ: (والماشي)؛ أي: ومفهوم الماشي، وهو : التَنقُلُ بالقَدَمِ مِن موضع لآخر، وقوله: (خارج عن ذاتِ الموضوع)؛ أي : خارج عَن ماهِيَّةِ ذاتِ الموضوع، فليسَ الضَّحِكُ وَلَا المشي جزءاً مِن ماهِيَّةِ زيدٍ وبكرٍ مثلاً، والصَّحكُ هو تقلُّصُ الشَّفتينِ مَعَ الإعجاب، وَهُو مفهوم ضاحك. وقِيلَ : مَفهومُهُ ذات ثبت لها ذلك، وعلى هذا القول؛ فالمعتبرُ أيضاً إنَّما هو الوصفُ ؛ أي: الضَّحِكُ ، والذَّاتُ ليس منظوراً لها . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: أَفْرَادِهِ) تفسيرٌ لِذَاتِ الموضوع، وقد عُلِمَ مِمَّا قَالَهُ: أَنَّ الماهية أخصُّ مِنَ المفهوم؛ لأنَّ مَفهوم الموضوع قد يكون عين ماهية أفرادِهِ، وَقَدْ يكونُ جزءاً من ماهيّة أفرادِهِ، وقد يكون خارجاً عَن ماهية أفرادِهِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ الْوَصْفِ وَالذَّاتِ) أي: الواقعين في المثْنِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى شَرْطِ الْوَصْفِ) وهو قولنا : ما دامَ كاتباً.
(قَوْلُهُ : لِكَوْنِهَا أَعَمَّ ... إِلخَ) أي : فكلُّ مثالٍ صح أن يكونَ مَشروطةٌ خاصَّةً ؛ صح أن يكونَ مَشروطةٌ ،عامَّة وَلَا عَكْسَ.
العطار
قَوْلُهُ : فَلْيْتَأَمَّلْ إنْ كانَ المعنى : فَلْيتأمَّل الفرقَ؛ فَلَا معنى له بعد وصفه بالجلاء، ويحتمل أنَّ الأمر بالتَّأقُلِ : لِلإشارة إلى أنَّ الحُكْمَ غيرُ عام لخروج الطَّبيعيَّةِ كما عرفت، أو للإشارة إلى أنَّ المحكوم عليه فيما ذكر الأفراد؛ لَا مِن حَيثُ كونُ مَفهوم الموضوع حقيقته ... إلخ
Chapter: On the Definition and its Divisions
If it is a title for a specific property or a common accident, as we say: 'Every laugher or every walker is an animal,' then the concept of 'laugher' and 'walker' is external to the essence of the subject, i.e., its individuals. And by what we have mentioned, a clear distinction is obtained between the description and the essence, so let it be contemplated. And it is called 'conditional-general' because it includes a condition of the description, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional which you will learn in the composites.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'for the concept of 'laugher') which is laughter, and his statement: ('and walker') i.e., and the concept of 'walker,' which is moving from place to place by foot. And his statement: ('external to the essence of the subject') i.e., external to the quiddity of the subject's essence, for laughter and walking are not a part of the quiddity of Zayd and Bakr, for example. Laughter is the contraction of the lips with admiration, and that is the concept of 'laugher.' And it is said: its concept is a substance to which that attaches; on this view, the consideration is also only the description, i.e., laughter, and the substance is not intended. (His statement: 'i.e., its individuals') is an interpretation of 'essence of the subject.' And it is known from what he said that the quiddity is more specific than the concept; because the concept of the subject may be identical to the quiddity of its individuals, may be a part of the quiddity of its individuals, and may be external to the quiddity of its individuals. (His statement: 'between the description and the essence') i.e., the two occurring in the text. (His statement: 'on the condition of the description') which is our saying: 'as long as he is writing.' (His statement: 'because it is more general... etc.') i.e., for every example that is valid as a specific conditional, it is valid as a general conditional, but not vice versa.
Al-'Attar: His statement: 'so let it be contemplated' — if the meaning is 'contemplate the difference,' then there is no meaning to that after describing it as clear. It is possible that the command to contemplate is to indicate that the ruling is not general because of the exclusion of the natural universal as you know, or to indicate that what is predicated in what was mentioned is the individuals, not insofar as the concept of the subject is its reality... etc.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the definition (mu'arraf) and its divisions. The author explains that when the descriptive attribute is a specific property (khassa) like 'laugher' or a common accident ('arad 'amm) like 'walker,' the concept is external to the essence (dhat) of the subject, i.e., external to the quiddity of its individuals. For example, 'Every laugher is an animal'—the concept 'laugher' (the ability to laugh) is not part of the quiddity of a human; it is an accident. The author emphasizes that this yields a clear distinction between description (wasf) and essence (dhat). Then he notes that such propositions are called 'conditional-general' (mashrutah 'ammah) because they include a condition of the description (i.e., 'as long as he laughs'), and 'general' because they are broader than the specific conditional (mashrutah khassah) to be studied later. Al-Dasuqi's commentary expands on the examples: 'laugher' means laughter (an accident), 'walker' means moving on foot, both external to quiddity. He also clarifies that 'essence of the subject' means individuals. Al-'Attar raises a subtle point: if the difference is already clear, why command contemplation? He proposes it may signal that the ruling is not universal (excluding natural universals) or that the predicate applies to individuals as such, not as having that concept as their reality. This passage trains the student to distinguish between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) predicates in definitions.

General and Specific Conditional Propositions (Mashrutah)
Author defines the conditional-general proposition and contrasts it with the specific conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a general conditional (mashrutah 'ammah) and a specific conditional (mashrutah khassah) by identifying the condition attached to the subject.

Core Idea
General conditionals include a condition on the subject, making them broader than specific conditionals.

This lesson focuses on a special type of categorical proposition called the 'conditional-general' (mashrutah 'ammah). The author explains that propositions like 'Every laugher as long as he laughs is an animal' contain a condition ('as long as he laughs') that qualifies the subject. Understanding the difference between this and the more restricted specific conditional (mashrutah khassah) is key to analysing how attributes condition logical statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition phrase in the proposition
Look for a phrase like 'as long as' (ma dama) attached to the subject. In the example, 'as long as he laughs' is the condition.
The condition is a temporal or circumstancial restriction.
2
Determine whether the condition is on the subject or the predicate
In the general conditional, the condition modifies the subject. The proposition says 'every laugher, as long as he laughs, is an animal.' The condition is tied to the subject's attribute.
The condition limits the scope of the subject's description.
3
Differentiate general from specific conditional
The general conditional is broader: every specific conditional is a general conditional, but not vice versa. The specific conditional will have a more restricted condition (to be studied later in 'composites').
The author says the specific conditional will be introduced later.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic example
For instance, 'Every fasting person, as long as he is fasting, is obeying Allah' is a general conditional. The condition is 'as long as he is fasting.'
This helps distinguish between essential and accidental attributes in conditional statements.

Chapter: On the Definition and its Divisions
If it is a title for a specific property or a common accident, as we say: 'Every laugher or every walker is an animal,' then the concept of 'laugher' and 'walker' is external to the essence of the subject, i.e., its individuals. And by what we have mentioned, a clear distinction is obtained between the description and the essence, so let it be contemplated. And it is called 'conditional-general' because it includes a condition of the description, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional which you will learn in the composites.
Al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'for the concept of 'laugher') which is laughter, and his statement: ('and walker') i.e., and the concept of 'walker,' which is moving from place to place by foot. And his statement: ('external to the essence of the subject') i.e., external to the quiddity of the subject's essence, for laughter and walking are not a part of the quiddity of Zayd and Bakr, for example. Laughter is the contraction of the lips with admiration, and that is the concept of 'laugher.' And it is said: its concept is a substance to which that attaches; on this view, the consideration is also only the description, i.e., laughter, and the substance is not intended. (His statement: 'i.e., its individuals') is an interpretation of 'essence of the subject.' And it is known from what he said that the quiddity is more specific than the concept; because the concept of the subject may be identical to the quiddity of its individuals, may be a part of the quiddity of its individuals, and may be external to the quiddity of its individuals. (His statement: 'between the description and the essence') i.e., the two occurring in the text. (His statement: 'on the condition of the description') which is our saying: 'as long as he is writing.' (His statement: 'because it is more general... etc.') i.e., for every example that is valid as a specific conditional, it is valid as a general conditional, but not vice versa.
Al-'Attar: His statement: 'so let it be contemplated' — if the meaning is 'contemplate the difference,' then there is no meaning to that after describing it as clear. It is possible that the command to contemplate is to indicate that the ruling is not general because of the exclusion of the natural universal as you know, or to indicate that what is predicated in what was mentioned is the individuals, not insofar as the concept of the subject is its reality... etc.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the definition (mu'arraf) and its divisions. The author explains that when the descriptive attribute is a specific property (khassa) like 'laugher' or a common accident ('arad 'amm) like 'walker,' the concept is external to the essence (dhat) of the subject, i.e., external to the quiddity of its individuals. For example, 'Every laugher is an animal'—the concept 'laugher' (the ability to laugh) is not part of the quiddity of a human; it is an accident. The author emphasizes that this yields a clear distinction between description (wasf) and essence (dhat). Then he notes that such propositions are called 'conditional-general' (mashrutah 'ammah) because they include a condition of the description (i.e., 'as long as he laughs'), and 'general' because they are broader than the specific conditional (mashrutah khassah) to be studied later. Al-Dasuqi's commentary expands on the examples: 'laugher' means laughter (an accident), 'walker' means moving on foot, both external to quiddity. He also clarifies that 'essence of the subject' means individuals. Al-'Attar raises a subtle point: if the difference is already clear, why command contemplation? He proposes it may signal that the ruling is not universal (excluding natural universals) or that the predicate applies to individuals as such, not as having that concept as their reality. This passage trains the student to distinguish between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) predicates in definitions.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
المشروطة الخاصة
شرط الوصف
ما دام

In Islamic logic, general conditionals contain a condition attached to the subject and are broader in scope than specific conditionals.

Descriptive Definitions Using Accidental Properties
Commentary on descriptive definition using accidental properties (الرسم بالخاصة والعرض العام).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a specific property (خاصة) and a general accident (عرض عام) in a descriptive definition, and identify whether a given attribute is part of the essence or external to it.

Core Idea
Not all attributes that define a thing belong to its essence; some are merely descriptive signs.

This lesson examines how logicians classify definitions based on whether the defining property is a specific accident unique to the species or a general accident shared across species. The passage from the Tadhheeb al-Sharh explains the key difference and how the concept of the property relates to the quiddity of the subject. By understanding this, you will be able to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of descriptive definitions
Look for the terms 'خاصة' (specific property) and 'عرض عام' (general accident). The passage gives examples: 'كل ضاحك حيوان' (every laughing one is an animal) and 'كل ماش حيوان' (every walking one is an animal).
The specific property belongs exclusively to one species; the general accident can belong to multiple.
2
Extract the relationship between concept and essence
The passage states that the concept (مفهوم) of the property is 'خارج عن ذات الموضوع' (external to the subject's essence). This means the property is not part of the quiddity (ماهية) of the individual.
For example, laughter is not part of the quiddity of Zayd; it is an accidental attribute.
3
Analyze the commentators' expansions
Al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'مفهوم' may be identical to, partly included in, or entirely external to the quiddity. Al-Attar suggests that the command to ponder indicates that the judgment applies to individuals insofar as the concept is true of them, not as their reality or accidental attribute.
This prevents misunderstanding: a property like 'laughing' is true of all humans but does not define their essence.
4
Test the distinction with examples
Given 'كل ضاحك إنسان' (every laughing one is a human), is 'laughing' a specific property or general accident? It is specific because it only applies to humans. For 'كل ماش حيوان', is 'walking' a specific property? No, because many animals walk.
The distinction affects whether the definition is considered a complete description or not.
5
Summarize the logical point
A descriptive definition using an accident must use an attribute that is true of all individuals of the defined species but is not part of their essence. The attribute serves as a sign, not a constituent.
This is crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إن كان عنواناً للخاصَّة أو العَرَضِ العام، كقولنا : «كل ضاحك أو كلُّ ماش حيوان»، فإنَّ مفهومَ الضَّاحِكِ والماشي خارج عن ذاتِ الموضوع؛ أي : أفراده. وبما ذكرنا يحصُلُ الفرقُ الجلي بين الوصفِ والذَّاتِ، فَلْيُتأَمَّلْ . وإِنَّما سُمِّيَتْ مشروطةً لاشتمالها على شرط الوصف، وعامَّةٌ ؛ لكونها أعم من المشروطة الخاصَّة التي ستعرفُها في المركَّبات .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ مَفْهُوْمَ الصَّاحِكِ) وهو الضَّحِكُ، وقولُهُ: (والماشي)؛ أي: ومفهوم الماشي، وهو : التَنقُلُ بالقَدَمِ مِن موضع لآخر، وقوله: (خارج عن ذاتِ الموضوع)؛ أي : خارج عَن ماهِيَّةِ ذاتِ الموضوع، فليسَ الضَّحِكُ وَلَا المشي جزءاً مِن ماهِيَّةِ زيدٍ وبكرٍ مثلاً، والصَّحكُ هو تقلُّصُ الشَّفتينِ مَعَ الإعجاب، وَهُو مفهوم ضاحك. وقِيلَ : مَفهومُهُ ذات ثبت لها ذلك، وعلى هذا القول؛ فالمعتبرُ أيضاً إنَّما هو الوصفُ ؛ أي: الضَّحِكُ ، والذَّاتُ ليس منظوراً لها . (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ: أَفْرَادِهِ) تفسيرٌ لِذاتِ الموضوع، وقد عُلِمَ مِمَّا قَالَهُ: أَنَّ الماهية أخصُّ مِنَ المفهوم؛ لأنَّ مَفهوم الموضوع قد يكون عين ماهية أفرادِهِ، وَقَدْ يكونُ جزءاً من ماهيّة أفرادِهِ، وقد يكون خارجاً عَن ماهية أفرادِهِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : بَيْنَ الْوَصْفِ وَالذَّاتِ) أي: الواقعين في المثْنِ.
(قَوْلُهُ : عَلَى شَرْطِ الْوَصْفِ) وهو قولنا : ما دامَ كاتباً.
(قَوْلُهُ : لِكَوْنِهَا أَعَمَّ ... إِلخَ) أي : فكلُّ مثالٍ صح أن يكونَ مَشروطةٌ خاصَّةً ؛ صح أن يكونَ مَشروطةٌ ،عامَّة وَلَا عَكْسَ.
العطار
قَوْلُهُ : فَلْيْتَأَمَّلْ إنْ كانَ المعنى : فَلْيتأمَّل الفرقَ؛ فَلَا معنى له بعد وصفه بالجلاء، ويحتمل أنَّ الأمر بالتَّأقُلِ : لِلإشارة إلى أنَّ الحُكْمَ غيرُ عام لخروج الطَّبيعيَّةِ كما عرفت، أو للإشارة إلى أنَّ المحكوم عليه فيما ذكر الأفراد؛ لَا مِن حَيثُ كونُ مَفهوم الموضوع حقيقته ... إلخ كَمَا قد يتوهم ، وإلا ؛ لخرجَ : كُلُّ ضاحك إنسان، وَلَا حيث كونه صفةٌ عارضةً لها ، وإلا لخرجَ كُلُّ إنسان حيوان، بل من حيث كونه صادقاً عليها ، فتكون جزئياتٍ له ؛ سواء كانَ حقيقتها أو جزء حقيقتِها أو وَصْفاً لها
Chapter: On the definiens (al-mu'arraf) and its divisions. If it is a title for the specific property or the general accident, like our saying: 'Every laughing one or every walking one is an animal', then the concept of the laughing one and the walking one is external to the subject's essence; i.e., its individuals. By what we have mentioned, the clear distinction between the description and the essence is obtained, so let it be pondered. It is called 'conditional' because it includes the condition of the description, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional which you will learn in the compounds. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'then the concept of the laughing one' – it is laughter. His statement: 'and the walking one' – i.e., the concept of the walking one, which is movement from place to place by foot. His statement: 'external to the subject's essence' – i.e., external to the quiddity of the subject's essence, so laughter and walking are not parts of the quiddity of Zayd and Bakr, for example. Laughter is the contraction of the lips with admiration, and that is the concept of 'laughing one'. It is said: its concept is the essence that possesses that, and on this view, the consideration is only the description, i.e., laughter, and the essence is not regarded. His statement: 'i.e., its individuals' – an interpretation of 'subject's essence'. It has been known from what he said: that the quiddity is more specific than the concept, because the concept of the subject may be identical to the quiddity of its individuals, or a part of the quiddity of its individuals, or external to the quiddity of its individuals. His statement: 'between the description and the essence' – i.e., occurring in the text. His statement: 'on the condition of the description' – which is our saying: 'as long as he is writing'. His statement: 'because it is more general... etc.' – i.e., every example that is valid to be a specific conditional is valid to be a general conditional, but not conversely. Al-Attar: His statement: 'so let it be pondered' – if the meaning is 'let him ponder the distinction', then there is no sense in it after describing it as clear. It is possible that the command to ponder is to indicate that the judgment is not general due to the exclusion of the natural (al-tabi'iyya) as you know, or to indicate that what is judged upon in what was mentioned are individuals, not insofar as the concept of the subject is its reality... etc., as might be mistakenly thought. Otherwise, every laughing one is a human would be excluded, and not insofar as it is an accidental attribute to them, otherwise every human is an animal would be excluded, but insofar as it is true of them, so they are particulars of it, whether it is their reality, a part of their reality, or an attribute of them.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a section about the definiens (المعرف) and its divisions. It begins by stating that if the definiens is a title for a specific property (خاصة) or a general accident (عرض عام), such as 'every laughing one' or 'every walking one' when defining 'animal', then the concept (مفهوم) of that property lies outside the essence (ذات) of the subject, meaning it is not part of the quiddity (ماهية) of the individuals. This establishes a clear distinction between the attribute and the essence. The commentator Al-Dasuqi expands on the definitions: 'laughing one' refers to laughter, which is a specific contraction of the lips, and 'walking one' refers to locomotion by foot. He emphasizes that these are not part of the quiddity of a person like Zayd or Bakr. The phrase 'outside the subject's essence' is interpreted to mean 'outside the quiddity' of the subject. Al-Dasuqi adds that the quiddity is more specific than the concept because the concept may be identical to, partly included in, or entirely external to the quiddity. Al-Attar then comments on the phrase 'so let it be pondered', suggesting possible reasons for the command: either to indicate that the judgment is not universal because of the exclusion of natural propositions, or to clarify that the subject of judgment is the individuals insofar as the concept is true of them, not insofar as it is their reality or an accidental attribute. This guards against misunderstanding that any property can serve as a definition; the property must be true of all individuals (e.g., 'laughing' applies to all humans, but 'walking' may not apply to all humans at all times).

Definition of the Absolute Temporal Proposition
Author defines the absolute temporal proposition with examples from lunar eclipse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the absolute temporal proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions using examples from astronomy and human actions.

Core Idea
The absolute temporal proposition is a necessary judgment at a specific time, unrestricted by permanence or non-necessity.

In this passage, the author defines a key type of modal proposition: the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa). Using the example of the lunar eclipse, he explains that such a proposition asserts necessity at a particular time without claiming that the judgment holds at all times or that it is unnecessary. The commentators expand on the definition, addressing potential misunderstandings and providing further illustrations.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find where the author states the condition for a proposition to be called absolute temporal. It begins with 'إن كان الحكم بضرورةِ النسبة في وقتٍ معيَّن' (If the judgment is about the necessity of the relation at a specific time).
2
Extract the defining characteristics
Note the two crucial elements: necessity (ضرورة) and a specific time (وقت معين). Also note the negation of two restrictions: not restricted by permanence (لادوام) and not by non-necessity (لاضرورة).
3
Examine the example
The passage gives a positive example: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of interposition' and a negative example: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature'. Identify which part demonstrates positive necessity and which negative necessity.
4
Understand the naming
The author explains why it is called temporal (وقتية) because time is considered, and absolute (مطلقة) because it is not restricted to permanence or non-necessity. If restricted by permanence, the term 'absolute' is dropped.
5
Follow the commentator's refinement
Al-Dasuqi critiques the inclusion of 'non-necessity' as a possible restriction, arguing that the absolute temporal is necessary and thus cannot be qualified by non-necessity. He reasons that the validity of negating something from something depends on its capability of receiving it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرِفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
(
إن كان الحكم بضرورةِ النسبة في وقتٍ معيَّن؛ (فَوَقْتِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) كقولنا بالضَّرورةِ: كلُّ قَمَرٍ مُنخَسِفٌ وقت حيلولةِ الْأَرضِ بينه وبين الشَّمسِ، ولا شيء من القمرِ بمنخسف وقتَ التَّربيع ؛ فإنَّ ثبوت الانخسافِ للقمر وسلبه عنه ضروري في وقتٍ معيَّن ؛ أي : وقتَ الحيلولة والتربيعِ . وإِنَّما سُمِّيَتْ وقتيَّةً؛ لاعتبار تعين الوقتِ فيها، ومطلقةً؛ لعدم تقييدها باللادوام، أو اللاضرورة.
ولهذا إذا قُيِّدَتْ بِاللَّأدوام ؛ حذف الإطلاقُ من اسمها، فكانت وقتيَّةٌ كما سيجيء في المركبات.
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : كَقَوْلِنَا : بِالضَّرُورَةِ: كُلُّ قَمَرِ مُنْخَسِفٌ وَقْتَ... إلخ) المراد بوقتِ التربيعِ : وقتُ عدمِ حيلولةِ الأرضِ بينَ الشَّمس والقمرِ؛ أي: وَكَقولنا : بالضرورة كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع وقتَ الكتابة.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ ... إِلَخ) أي : وَكَقولنا : بالضّرورةِ لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الكاتب بساكن الأصابع وقت الكتابة.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقْتَ التَّرْبِيعِ) أي : وقتَ عدمِ الحيلولة.
(قَوْلُهُ : أَوِ اللَّا ضَرَوْرَةِ فِيهِ أَنَّهُ لَا يصحُ أنْ تقيَّدَ بِاللاضرورةِ لِلتَّنافي، وذلك لأنَّ الوقتيّة المطلقة ضروريَّةٌ، وحينئذ ; فَلَا يعقل تقييدها باللأضرورة، وحينئذ ; فَلَا يصح قوله : واللاضرورة; لأنَّ صحة نفي الشَّيء عَن شيءٍ ; فرعٌ عَن صحة قبُولِهِ لَهُ، وقد عَلِمْتَ أَنَّهُ لَا يصح هنا، تأمَّل.
(قَوْلُهُ : وَلِهَذَا ) أي : لِعَدَمِ التَّقييد باللادوامِ؛ لو قيَّدته ... إلخ .
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ قَمَرٍ مُنْخَسِفٌ وَقْتَ حَيْلُوْلَةِ الْأَرْضِ ... إِلَخ) زعمَ أَهلُ الهيئةِ أَنَّ نورَ القمرِ مُستفادٌ مِن نورِ الشَّمسِ، وأنَّهُ في نفسِهِ كمد، وأنَّ مدار حركتِهِ يقاطع مدار حركةِ الشَّمس على نُقطتَيْنِ، فإذا كانَ أحدهما في نقطة والآخَرُ في أُخرَى; تقعُ الأرضُ حائلاً بينهما ; فتمنعُ مِن وصول ضوء الشَّمسِ إليهِ، فَيُرى على ظُلمتِهِ الأصلية، وَهُوَ الانخسافُ
Chapter: On Definition and Its Divisions. If the judgment is about the necessity of the relation at a specific time, then it is an absolute temporal (proposition), like our saying necessarily: Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature. For the affirmation and negation of the eclipse to the moon is necessary at a specific time, i.e., the time of interposition and quadrature. It is called 'temporal' because of the consideration of the determination of time in it, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by permanence or non-necessity. Hence, if it is restricted by permanence, the term 'absolute' is dropped from its name, so it becomes (simply) temporal, as will come in the compound (propositions). Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: like our saying necessarily: Every moon is eclipsed at the time... etc.) what is meant by the time of quadrature is the time of absence of the earth's interposition between the sun and the moon; i.e., and like our saying necessarily: Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing. (His saying: and nothing of the moon... etc.) i.e., and like our saying necessarily: No writer is motionless of fingers at the time of writing. (His saying: the time of quadrature) i.e., the time of absence of interposition. (His saying: or non-necessity) in it is that it is not valid to restrict it by non-necessity due to contradiction, because the absolute temporal proposition is necessary, and then its restriction by non-necessity cannot be conceived, and then his saying: and non-necessity is not valid, because the validity of negating something from something depends on its being capable of receiving it, and you have known that this is not valid here, reflect. (His saying: and therefore) i.e., because of the non-restriction by permanence; if you restrict it... etc. Al-'Attar: (His saying: Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition... etc.) The astronomers claim that the moon's light is derived from the sun's light, and that it is inherently dark, and that its orbit intersects the sun's orbit at two points. When one is at one point and the other at another, the earth becomes a barrier between them, preventing the sun's light from reaching it, so it appears in its original darkness, which is the eclipse.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the 'absolute temporal proposition' (الوقتية المطلقة), a type of modal proposition in logic. The author begins by stating the condition: if the judgment is about the necessity of a relation at a specific time, then it is called an absolute temporal proposition. He gives an example from astronomy: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun' and its negative counterpart 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature'. The affirmation and negation are both necessary at their respective times. The author then explains why it is called 'temporal' (لأنها تعتبر وقتاً معيناً) and 'absolute' (لأنها غير مقيدة بالدوام أو اللاضرورة). If it were restricted by permanence (i.e., the judgment holds at all times), the term 'absolute' would be dropped and it would simply be a 'temporal' proposition, as will be explained later in compound propositions. The commentary by al-Dasuqi clarifies the example: the time of quadrature is when there is no interposition, and he provides an alternative example involving a writer moving his fingers at the time of writing, which is a more accessible illustration. He also critiques the inclusion of 'non-necessity' (اللاضرورة) as a possible restriction, arguing that an absolute temporal proposition is inherently necessary, so restricting it by non-necessity would be contradictory. Al-'Attar's commentary gives background on the astronomical claim about the moon's light being derived from the sun and the cause of the eclipse, explaining the physical basis of the example.

Key Terms
وقتية مطلقة
مشروطة
ضرورة
انخساف

Checkpoint

B) Every writer is a human.
C) Some animals are rational.
D) No stone is a tree.

Key Takeaway
The absolute temporal proposition is defined by necessity at a specific time, without being restricted to permanence or non-necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In the lunar eclipse example, identify which part is the positive absolute temporal and which is the negative. Then, create a similar pair using the writer example (positive: 'Every writer moves fingers at the time of writing', negative: 'No writer is motionless at the time of writing').

Definitions as Qualified Simple Propositions
Author begins chapter on definition equating definitions with simple propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the eight simple modal propositions can serve as definitions and explain why the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual are excluded.

Core Idea
Definitions are the six modally qualifiable simples, not all eight.

The author opens a new chapter on definition (al-ma'rif) by stating that definitions are exactly the same as the simple propositions introduced earlier. Yet the count seems off: eight simples versus seven compounds. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that 'the same' means definitions are a subset of the simples, excluding those that cannot be qualified without contradiction. This lesson unpacks that resolution and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the author's statement: definitions are the same as the previously mentioned simples. Note the apparent numerical inconsistency: eight simples, seven compounds.
2
Examine the commentator's resolution
Al-Dasuqi argues that 'the same' does not imply equality in number; rather, the compounds (seven) are a subset of the simples (eight). Definitions are not all eight but only those that can be qualified.
Key phrase: 'المركبات لا تخرج عن البسائط' (compounds do not exit from the simples).
3
Understand the exclusion of two simples
The absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) and absolute perpetual (الدائمة المطلقة) are excluded. Necessity in the first is essential and entails essential perpetuity; qualifying it with 'non-necessary' or 'non-perpetual' would be contradictory. Similarly, essential perpetuity cannot be negated.
Thus, these two simples cannot serve as definitions because they cannot be further qualified.
4
Determine the remaining six
Subtract the two excluded from the eight simples, leaving six. These six are the ones that admit qualification and thus can function as definitions in this context.
The text mentions 'فالباقي من البسائط ستة' (the remainder of the simples are six).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ وهي بعينها البسائط المذكورة،
الدسوقي: قوله : وهي بعينها ... إلخ فيه أن البسائط ثمانية، والمركبات سبعة، ففي الكلام تناف؛ إلا أن يقال : إن قوله : ((بعينها) بمعنى : أن المركبات لا تخرج عن البسائط، وإن كانت أقل منها. ا . هـ . ياسين نقلاً .
ثم قال : البسائط على حذف مضاف؛ أي: هي بعض البسائط، أو يقال : قوله : هي وذلك لأن الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة لا يقيَّدان أصلاً؛ لأن الضرورة في الأولى بحسب الذات وهي تستلزم الدوام الذاتي، فلو قيَّدت الأولى باللاضرورة أو اللادوام الذاتي؛ كان تناقضاً، والدوام في الثانية بحسب الذات، فلو قيِّدت باللادوام الذاتي؛ كان تناقضاً، فالباقي من البسائط ستة، وهي التي تقيَّد، لكن من خروج
Chapter: On Definition (al-ma'rif) and its divisions. And they are the same as the previously mentioned simples.
Al-Dasuqi: his saying: 'And they are the same...' etc. There is in it that the simples are eight and the compounds are seven, so in the speech there is contradiction, except that it can be said that his saying 'the same' means that the compounds do not exit from the simples, even if they are fewer than them. End, quoted from Yasin.
Then he said: 'the simples' with an omitted genitive, i.e., they are some of the simples, or it can be said: his saying 'they are' is because the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual are not qualified at all, because necessity in the first is according to essence and it entails essential perpetuity, so if the first were qualified by non-necessity or non-essential perpetuity, it would be a contradiction. And perpetuity in the second is according to essence, so if it were qualified by non-essential perpetuity, it would be a contradiction. So the remainder of the simples are six, and they are the ones that are qualified. But from the exit...

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on definition (al-ma'rif) and its divisions. The author's main statement is that the definitions (ma'arif) are exactly the same as the previously mentioned simple modal propositions. This is a striking claim because the simples were eight in number, while the compounds were seven. Al-Dasuqi notes an apparent contradiction: if definitions are the same as the simples, then there should be eight, but earlier the number of compounds was seven. He resolves this by explaining that the statement 'the same' means that the compounds do not go beyond the simples; they are a subset. In other words, the definitions in this chapter are not all the simples but only those that can be qualified (i.e., six of them). He clarifies that the absolute necessary and absolute perpetual are not included as definitions because they cannot be qualified without contradiction. The necessity in the absolute necessary is essential and entails essential perpetuity, so adding a qualification like 'non-necessary' would be contradictory. Similarly, the absolute perpetual is essentially perpetual, so 'non-perpetual' is impossible. Therefore, only six simple propositions remain that can serve as definitions. The passage ends abruptly with 'من خروج', likely leading to further explanation.

Restricting Descriptive Permanence: The Correct Method
Impossibility of restricting descriptive permanence with its negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct type of non-permanence (descriptive vs. essential) when restricting a descriptive permanence proposition in compound modal logic.

Core Idea
Restricting descriptive permanence with its own negation creates contradiction; only essential non-permanence works.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle point: when you have a proposition that expresses descriptive permanence (e.g., 'every A is necessarily B by virtue of its description'), you cannot restrict it by saying 'not always B by that description'—that would be self-contradictory. Instead, any valid restriction must be on the essence of the subject. Let's unpack this principle and see how it applies to the general conditional and conventional propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the restriction
Locate the phrase 'al-dawam al-wasfi' (descriptive permanence). Understand that it expresses a predicate holding for the subject as long as the description applies.
2
Understand why restricting with its negation is impossible
The author states that restricting descriptive permanence with its negation (al-la-dawam al-wasfi) leads to contradiction. Recognize that combining 'always A' with 'not always A' is logically contradictory.
Contradiction arises because the restriction directly opposes the original permanence.
3
Identify the alternative: essential non-permanence
If a restriction is needed, the author says it must be with 'al-la-dawam al-dhati' (essential non-permanence), which negates permanence with respect to the essence or individuals of the subject, not the description.
This avoids contradiction because it changes the scope from description to essence.
4
Apply the rule to an example
Consider the general conditional (al-mashruta al-amma). The author explains that it can be restricted with essential non-permanence (correct and considered) but not with descriptive non-permanence or other invalid restrictions.
This example illustrates the practical application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعْرُفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
يمتنع أن يقيَّد باللادوام الوصفي، بل إذا أُريد تقييده بقيد صحيح؛ فلا بد أن يقيَّد باللادوام الذاتي، ويكون الحكم حينئذ بضرورة النّسبة أو دوامها بحسب الوصف مقيَّداً باللادوام بحسَبِ الذَّاتِ.
الدسوقي
أما في المنتشرة المطلقة؛ فَلا ؛ إذ الوقتُ فيها غيرُ مُعيَّن، اللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقال : المراد بالدَّوامِ الوصفيّ وَمَا فِي قُوَّتِهِ؛ أي: الدَّوامِ الَّذي اعتبر بحسب الوصْفِ، وَمَا في قوَّتِهِ في الوصف.

العامتين، فلو قيدتا باللادوام الوصفي لزم التنافي بين القيد ومقيده فتعين العدول عنه إلى اللادوام الذاتي كالعرفيتين بدون فرق، ومن هنا قال العطار وغيره : ينبغي الشارح أن يذكر قوله : وإنما قيد ... إلخ بعد أن يفرغ من الوقتيتين لأن التوجيه في الجميع واحد . . . .هـ. الشَّرنوبي.
وتسميتُهُمَا بِالخَاصَّتَيْنِ ؛ لِكَونِهِمَا أخصَّ مِنَ المشروطة والعرفية العامتين اللتين عَرَفْتَهُما في البسائط ؛ إذ كلَّما (۱) وُجِدَ الخاصَّتان وُجِدَ العامَّتان ولا عكس
Section: On the Known and Its Divisions. It is impossible to restrict with descriptive non-permanence, but if one intends to restrict it with a correct restriction, then it must be restricted with essential non-permanence, and the judgment then of necessity of the relation or its permanence according to the description is restricted by non-permanence according to the essence. al-Dusuqi: As for the absolute widespread, no; because the time therein is unspecified, unless it is said: by descriptive permanence and what is in its force is meant the permanence considered according to the description and what is in its force in the description. (His statement: 'it is impossible' etc.) meaning: due to contradiction then; i.e., when it was restricted with essential non-permanence. (His statement: 'restricted') i.e., that judgment. (His statement: 'according to the essence') i.e., the individuals of the subject. al-Attar: (His statement: 'it is impossible') the reason for impossibility is the necessity of contradiction. (His statement: 'if one intends to restrict it') i.e., restricting the descriptive permanence. (His statement: 'with a correct restriction') in this restriction is a discussion; because if descriptive permanence were restricted in the general conditional with the restriction of essential non-necessity, it would be correct; so if he said: with a correct and considered restriction, this discussion would not arise; because this restriction although correct, is not considered. Therefore al-Sayyid said: the general conditional can be restricted with essential non-necessity, but it is an unconsidered composition; it can be restricted with essential non-permanence as he mentioned, and it cannot be restricted with descriptive non-necessity, which is clear, nor with descriptive permanence, nor with negation of general absoluteness, nor with negation of general possibility; because it is more general than descriptive necessity, and it is not permissible to restrict the specific with negation of the general, for that is an incorrect restriction. And analogize based on what was mentioned the case of all other compounds; so it appears to you that for composition there are many aspects, some not correct, some correct but unconsidered, and some correct and considered. End. And that has preceded. The two general ones, if they were restricted with descriptive non-permanence, incompatibility would occur between the restriction and its restricted, so it was necessary to turn away from it to essential non-permanence like the two conventional ones without difference. Hence al-Attar and others said: It is proper for the commentator to mention his statement: 'Rather, he restricted...' etc. after he finishes from the two temporal ones because the direction in all is one. End. al-Sharnubi. And their naming as the two specific ones is due to their being more specific than the general conditional and general conventional which you knew among the simple ones; since whenever the two specific ones exist, the two general ones exist, and not the converse.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is part of a commentary (sharh) on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq, specifically a section on the known and its divisions. The author first establishes a key principle: it is impossible to restrict a descriptive permanence (dawam wasfi) with its own negation (la-dawam wasfi). For example, if a proposition states that something is always A, you cannot restrict it to 'not always A' because that would be contradictory. Instead, if a valid restriction is intended, it must be with an essential non-permanence (la-dawam dhati), which relates to the essence of the subject. The commentator then explains that the judgment becomes restricted by this essential non-permanence, meaning it is not permanent in terms of the subject's individuals. al-Dusuqi adds that this principle does not apply to the absolute widespread proposition because its time is unspecified, unless the term 'descriptive permanence' is taken in a broader sense. al-Attar elaborates on the impossibility, stating that it leads to contradiction. He further discusses various possible restrictions of the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) and conventional (urfiyya 'amma) propositions, identifying which combinations are correct but unconsidered, and which are correct and considered. For instance, restricting the general conditional with essential non-necessity is correct but not considered in logic, whereas restricting with essential non-permanence is both correct and considered. The author then notes that the two general propositions cannot be restricted by descriptive non-permanence, leading to the adoption of essential non-permanence, similar to the two conventional propositions. Finally, the naming of the resulting compound propositions as the 'two specific ones' (khassatan) is explained: they are more specific than the general ones, because whenever the specific ones exist, the general ones exist, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
اللادوام الوصفي
اللادوام الذاتي
المشروطة العامَّة

The Two Specific Compound Propositions: Naming and Entailment
Definition and entailment of the two specific compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two specific compound propositions and explain why they are called 'the two specific ones' based on their entailment relation to the general ones.

Core Idea
The two specific propositions are more restrictive than the general ones; whenever the specific holds, the general holds, but not vice versa.

In the conclusion of this passage, the author introduces a pair of compound propositions called 'the two specific ones' (al-khassatan). They are the result of properly restricting the general conditional and general conventional propositions with essential non-permanence. Their name is justified by a logical entailment: they are more specific than the general propositions, meaning they imply the general ones but are not implied by them. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two general compound propositions
The general conditional (al-mashruta al-amma) and the general conventional (al-urfiyya al-amma) are simple compound propositions that express necessity or permanence in a general sense.
They are 'general' because they cover all individuals of the subject.
2
Understand the restriction that yields the specific ones
The two general propositions are restricted by essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati), as established in the previous principle. This restriction produces the two specific compound propositions.
For example, the specific version of the general conditional is called the 'specific conditional' (al-mashruta al-khassa).
3
Observe the entailment relationship
The author states that whenever the two specific ones exist, the two general ones exist, but not the converse. This means the specific propositions entail the general ones.
This is a logical consequence of the restriction: the specific ones add an extra condition (non-permanence in essence), so they are stronger.
4
Confirm the naming rationale
Because of this one-way entailment, the resulting propositions are called 'the two specific ones' (al-khassatan); they are a subclass of the general ones.
The term 'specific' (khassa) here means more restricted in scope.

وتسميتُهُمَا بِالخَاصَّتَيْنِ ؛ لِكَونِهِمَا أخصَّ مِنَ المشروطة والعرفية العامتين اللتين عَرَفْتَهُما في البسائط ؛ إذ كلَّما (۱) وُجِدَ الخاصَّتان وُجِدَ العامَّتان ولا عكس
Section: On the Known and Its Divisions. It is impossible to restrict with descriptive non-permanence, but if one intends to restrict it with a correct restriction, then it must be restricted with essential non-permanence, and the judgment then of necessity of the relation or its permanence according to the description is restricted by non-permanence according to the essence. al-Dusuqi: As for the absolute widespread, no; because the time therein is unspecified, unless it is said: by descriptive permanence and what is in its force is meant the permanence considered according to the description and what is in its force in the description. (His statement: 'it is impossible' etc.) meaning: due to contradiction then; i.e., when it was restricted with essential non-permanence. (His statement: 'restricted') i.e., that judgment. (His statement: 'according to the essence') i.e., the individuals of the subject. al-Attar: (His statement: 'it is impossible') the reason for impossibility is the necessity of contradiction. (His statement: 'if one intends to restrict it') i.e., restricting the descriptive permanence. (His statement: 'with a correct restriction') in this restriction is a discussion; because if descriptive permanence were restricted in the general conditional with the restriction of essential non-necessity, it would be correct; so if he said: with a correct and considered restriction, this discussion would not arise; because this restriction although correct, is not considered. Therefore al-Sayyid said: the general conditional can be restricted with essential non-necessity, but it is an unconsidered composition; it can be restricted with essential non-permanence as he mentioned, and it cannot be restricted with descriptive non-necessity, which is clear, nor with descriptive permanence, nor with negation of general absoluteness, nor with negation of general possibility; because it is more general than descriptive necessity, and it is not permissible to restrict the specific with negation of the general, for that is an incorrect restriction. And analogize based on what was mentioned the case of all other compounds; so it appears to you that for composition there are many aspects, some not correct, some correct but unconsidered, and some correct and considered. End. And that has preceded. The two general ones, if they were restricted with descriptive non-permanence, incompatibility would occur between the restriction and its restricted, so it was necessary to turn away from it to essential non-permanence like the two conventional ones without difference. Hence al-Attar and others said: It is proper for the commentator to mention his statement: 'Rather, he restricted...' etc. after he finishes from the two temporal ones because the direction in all is one. End. al-Sharnubi. And their naming as the two specific ones is due to their being more specific than the general conditional and general conventional which you knew among the simple ones; since whenever the two specific ones exist, the two general ones exist, and not the converse.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is part of a commentary (sharh) on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq, specifically a section on the known and its divisions. The author first establishes a key principle: it is impossible to restrict a descriptive permanence (dawam wasfi) with its own negation (la-dawam wasfi). For example, if a proposition states that something is always A, you cannot restrict it to 'not always A' because that would be contradictory. Instead, if a valid restriction is intended, it must be with an essential non-permanence (la-dawam dhati), which relates to the essence of the subject. The commentator then explains that the judgment becomes restricted by this essential non-permanence, meaning it is not permanent in terms of the subject's individuals. al-Dusuqi adds that this principle does not apply to the absolute widespread proposition because its time is unspecified, unless the term 'descriptive permanence' is taken in a broader sense. al-Attar elaborates on the impossibility, stating that it leads to contradiction. He further discusses various possible restrictions of the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) and conventional (urfiyya 'amma) propositions, identifying which combinations are correct but unconsidered, and which are correct and considered. For instance, restricting the general conditional with essential non-necessity is correct but not considered in logic, whereas restricting with essential non-permanence is both correct and considered. The author then notes that the two general propositions cannot be restricted by descriptive non-permanence, leading to the adoption of essential non-permanence, similar to the two conventional propositions. Finally, the naming of the resulting compound propositions as the 'two specific ones' (khassatan) is explained: they are more specific than the general ones, because whenever the specific ones exist, the general ones exist, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
الخاصتان
المشروطة العامَّة
العرفية العامَّة

The Temporal Proposition (Waqtiyya): Structure and Example
Author defines the temporal proposition with example and explains its composition from two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two components of a temporal proposition and apply the definition to examples from Islamic astronomy and fiqh.

Core Idea
The temporal proposition captures 'not always' by combining a specific-time affirmation with a universal negation.

This lesson introduces the temporal proposition (al-waqtiyya), a key type of compound modal proposition in Islamic logic. Using the concrete example of lunar eclipses, the author unpacks how such propositions are constructed from an affirmative part about a specific time and a negative part covering all other times. Understanding this composition is essential for correctly analyzing temporal claims in kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the term 'waqtīyya' in the Arabic and its definition. Note the example: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth’s interposition, not always.'
The example from astronomy is chosen because it is observable and well-known.
2
Decompose the proposition
Separate the example into two parts: (1) an affirmative absolute temporal part: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth’s interposition' and (2) a negative general absolute part: 'Not always' meaning 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at all times.'
The negative part is equivalent to a universal negative: 'No moon is eclipsed at any other time.'
3
Understand the commentary
Read al-Dusuqi’s remark that the core is 'non-permanence' (lā dawām). Notice how al-Attar explains the physical process of eclipses to ground the temporal specification.
The commentators reinforce that the temporal proposition is not just a simple statement but a compound of two quantifications.
4
Apply to other examples
Think of another temporal phenomenon in Islamic sciences, e.g., 'Every fast is valid at the time of dawn until sunset, not always.' Identify its two components.
Practice with both positive and negative temporal propositions to master the concept.

3. The Temporal: As for the temporal, if it is affirmative, like our saying necessarily: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always.' So its composition is from: an affirmative absolute temporal first part, and a negative general absolute. al-Dusuqi: It is the concept of non-permanence. (His statement: 'it is the concept of non-permanence') and it is: Nothing of the moon is eclipsed with general absoluteness. (His statement: 'not always') is in the force of our saying: Every moon is eclipsed actually. al-Attar: (His statement: 'Every moon is eclipsed') The eclipse is the emptying of the moon, wholly or partially, of the light falling on it from the sun, due to the interposition of the earth between them, just as the solar eclipse is the interposition of the moon between the sun and us, so it veils its light from us wholly or partially. So the blackness that appears in the sun is the color of the moon's body, and for this the blackness of the sun begins from the west; because...
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage defines the temporal proposition (waqtiyya), which is one of the compound modal propositions. The author gives an example: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, not always.' This proposition asserts that the predicate (being eclipsed) holds at a specific time, but does not hold always. Its composition has two parts: (1) an affirmative absolute temporal proposition as the first part, meaning that at the specified time, the moon is eclipsed; and (2) a negative general absolute proposition, meaning that it is not eclipsed at all times. The commentator al-Dusuqi explains that the core of the temporal proposition is the concept of non-permanence (la-dawam). He clarifies that 'not always' in the example is equivalent to saying 'Every moon is actually eclipsed' at that moment. al-Attar then provides a detailed astronomical explanation of lunar and solar eclipses: a lunar eclipse occurs when the earth comes between the sun and the moon, blocking the sunlight from reaching the moon; a solar eclipse occurs when the moon comes between the sun and the earth. He notes that the darkness seen in the moon during a lunar eclipse is the moon's own color, just as the darkness in the sun during a solar eclipse is the moon's body. This illustration serves to ground the logical definition in a familiar observable phenomenon.

الخسوف

Key Takeaway
A temporal proposition affirms a predicate at a specific time while denying it at all other times, consisting of an affirmative absolute temporal part and a negative general absolute part.

Classification of Restricted Propositions: Temporal and Distributed
Defines the two types of restricted propositions and their composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporal and distributed propositions and identify their compositional parts.

Core Idea
Restricted propositions tie necessity to a time, either specific (temporal) or indefinite (distributed).

Not all propositions are true at all times. Some are restricted to specific times or indefinite periods. This lesson defines the two main types of restricted propositions – temporal and distributed – and explains how they are structured, using examples from natural phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Examine the negative temporal example
Read the example: 'By necessity, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not permanently.' Notice that 'not permanently' indicates the judgment is restricted to a specific time.
The moon is not always eclipsed; the negation holds only at quadrature.
2
Analyze the definition of temporal proposition
Find the formal definition: a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation for a subject in a specific time among its existence times, restricted by non-permanence according to the essence.
The time is specific (mu'ayyan), and the restriction is intrinsic to the essence.
3
Extract the definition of distributed proposition
Find the formal definition: a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation in a non-specific time, not permanently, according to the essence.
The time is non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyan), like 'at some time'.
4
Break down the composition of temporal proposition
From the negative example, the composition is: a negative temporal absolute part (the first part) and a positive absolute part (the concept of non-permanence).
For an affirmative temporal, the parts would be analogous: a positive temporal absolute plus a negative absolute.
5
Break down the composition of distributed proposition
For an affirmative distributed: a positive distributed absolute part and a general negative absolute part. For a negative distributed: a negative distributed absolute part and a general positive absolute part.
The 'general' absolute proposition is the concept of non-permanence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْمَعْرَفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ. وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً كَقَوْلِنَا: بِالضَّرُورَةِ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْقَمَرِ بِمُنْخَسِفٍ وَقْتَ التَّرْبِيعِ لَا دَائِمَاً؛ فَتَرْكِيبُهَا مِنْ: سَالِبَةٍ وَقْتِيَّةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ هِيَ الْجُزْءُ الْأَوَّلُ، وَمُوجَبَةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ اللَّادَوَامِ. فَالْوَقْتِيَّةُ هِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ ثُبُوتِ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ، أَوْ سَلْبِهِ عَنْهُ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنٍ مِنْ أَوْقَاتِ وُجُودِ الْمَوْضُوعِ مُقَيَّدَاً بِاللَّادَوَامِ بِحَسَبِ الذَّاتِ. وَالْمُنْتَشِرَةُ هِيَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ الثُّبُوتِ، أَوِ السَّلْبِ فِي وَقْتٍ غَيْرِ مُعَيَّنٍ لَا دَائِمَاً بِحَسَبِ الذَّاتِ. وَتَرْكِيبُهَا مِنْ: مُوجَبَةٍ مُنْتَشِرَةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ هِيَ الْجُزْءُ الْأَوَّلُ، وَسَالِبَةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ اللَّادَوَامِ إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةً. وَمِنْ: سَالِبَةٍ مُنْتَشِرَةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ، وَمُوجَبَةٍ مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ هِيَ مَفْهُومُ اللَّادَوَامِ إِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةً.
Chapter: On the restricted and its divisions. And if it is negative, like our saying: 'By necessity, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not permanently'; then its composition is from: a negative temporal absolute proposition as the first part, and a positive absolute proposition as the concept of non-permanence. So the temporal is that in which is judged the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject, or its negation from it, in a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, restricted by non-permanence, according to the essence. And the distributed is that in which is judged the necessity of affirmation, or negation, in a non-specific time, not permanently, according to the essence. And its composition is from: a positive distributed absolute proposition as the first part, and a general negative absolute proposition as the concept of non-permanence, if it is positive. And from: a negative distributed absolute proposition, and a general positive absolute proposition as the concept of non-permanence, if it is negative.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the two main types of restricted propositions (al-muqayyad) in Aristotelian logic: the temporal (al-waqtiyya) and the distributed (al-muntashira). The author begins with a chapter heading on the restricted and its divisions, then immediately gives an example of a negative temporal proposition: 'By necessity, nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not permanently.' The phrase 'not permanently' indicates that the negation is not absolute but restricted to a specific time. The composition of such a proposition is then explained: it consists of a negative temporal absolute part (the first part) and a positive absolute part representing the concept of non-permanence (meaning the negation holds only at that time, not always). Next, the formal definition of a temporal proposition is given: it is a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation of a predicate for a subject during a specific time among the times of the subject's existence, restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence. For example, 'the moon is eclipsed at opposition' is true only at a specific time, not always. The second type is the distributed proposition (al-muntashira), defined as a judgment of necessary affirmation or negation in a non-specific time, not permanently. An example would be 'every moving thing is changing place at some time,' where the time is not specified. The composition of a distributed proposition is then given: if it is affirmative, it consists of a positive distributed absolute part and a general negative absolute part (the concept of non-permanence); if it is negative, it consists of a negative distributed absolute part and a general positive absolute part. Notably, the definition of the temporal proposition includes the condition of a specific time (mu'ayyan), while the distributed is in a non-specific time (ghayr mu'ayyan). The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar (not included in the clean Arabic) clarifies that 'specific time' is not necessarily a particular designated moment but any time that is more specific than the times of the subject's existence, and it can be multiple specific times. This classification is crucial for understanding modal propositions and their use in syllogisms.

المنتشرة
اللادوام

The True Disjunctive and Its Positive and Negative Types
Defines the true disjunctive proposition and its positive and negative forms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a given disjunctive proposition as a true disjunctive (haqiqiyya) and classify it as positive (mujaba) or negative (saliba) by testing mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
The true disjunctive requires that its two parts cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false.

Disjunctive propositions are essential in logical arguments, especially in theological proofs. The true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) is the most complete form, where the two alternatives are mutually exclusive in both truth and falsehood. This lesson will walk you through its definition, its distinguishing characteristics, and how to recognize its positive and negative subtypes as taught in the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relationships (nisbatayn)
Read the disjunctive proposition and isolate the two relationships that are being connected by 'either…or'. For example, in 'the world is either eternal or originated', the two relationships are 'the world is eternal' and 'the world is originated'.
2
Test for mutual exclusion in truth (sidq)
Ask: Can both relationships be true at the same time? If they can (e.g., 'this is black' and 'this is a writer' can both be true for a black writer), then it is not a true disjunctive. If they cannot both be true, proceed to test falsehood.
Truth here means existence (thubut) of the relationship in reality.
3
Test for mutual exclusion in falsehood (kidhb)
Ask: Can both relationships be false at the same time? If they can (e.g., 'this is black' and 'this is a writer' can both be false for a non-black non-writer), then it is not a true disjunctive. If they cannot both be false, then it is a true disjunctive (haqiqiyya).
Falsehood means non-existence (irtifa') of the relationship.
4
Determine if the proposition is positive or negative
Check the judgment: If the proposition asserts mutual exclusion (i.e., it says the two cannot be true together nor false together), it is positive (mujaba). If it denies mutual exclusion (i.e., it says they can be true together or false together), it is negative (saliba). The negative form is indicated by a negation like 'laysa' or 'ma'.
5
Apply the test to the passage's examples
For the positive example 'this number is either even or odd': evenness and oddness cannot be true together nor false together for any integer, so it is a true disjunctive positive. For the negative example 'not: this is either black or a writer': both can be true (black writer) and both false (neither), so it is a true disjunctive negative (denying mutual exclusion).

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيةِ

المنفصلة الحقيقية : هي التي حُكمَ فيها بتنافي نسبتين، أو عدمِ تنافيهما في الصدق والكذب معاً.

وقوله: (أو لا تنافيهما)؛ أي أو بعدم تنافيهما في الصدق والكذب، وهذا في السَّالبةِ، فَقولُهُ: (صدقاً وكذباً راجع لكلِّ مِنَ الأمرين، والمراد بالصدق؛ التبوتُ، وَبِالكذب؛ الارتفاع.

قَوْلُهُ : وَهِيَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةُ الأَوْلَى : فَهِيَ الحقيقيَّةُ، سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ لاحتوائها على كمال الانفصال، فكأنَّها حقيقة المنفصلة، وضابطها(۱) : أنْ تتركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّيء ونقيضه، نحو: هذا العددُ إمَّا زوج أو لا زوج، وَمِنَ الشَّيء والمساوي لنقيضه، نحو : هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد. فظهرَ مِن هذا عدم اجتماع طرفَيْهَا في التُّبوتِ ، وعدم ارتفاعِهِمَا ؛ لأَنَّ النَّقيضَيْنِ لَا يجتمعانِ وَلَا يَرتفعانِ، وَكَذلِكَ الشَّيءُ والمساوي لنقيضه، لا يرتفعانِ وَلَا يجتمعان.

وهي : إمَّا موجبةٌ، أو سالبةٌ .

فالموجبةُ : هي التي حكم فيها بتنافي نسبتين في الصدق والكذب معاً. كقولنا : «هذا العددُ إمَّا زوج أو فرد»، فإنَّ زوجيَّةَ العددِ وفرديَّته متنافيان في الصِّدقِ والكذب؛ أي: لا يصدقانِ وَلَا يكذبان .

والسَّالبة : هي حُكِمَ فيها بعدم تنافي نسبتَين في الصّدقِ والكذبِ كَقولِنَا : «ليس أَلْبَيَّةَ إمَّا أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً»، فإنَّهما يصدقان ويكذبانِ، وَلَا مُنافاة بينهما صدقاً وكذباً
Chapter: On the Divisions of the Conditional [Proposition]. The true disjunctive: it is that in which there is a judgment of mutual exclusion of two relationships, or of the absence of their mutual exclusion, in truth and falsehood together. And his statement: 'or the absence of their mutual exclusion' means the judgment of the absence of their mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood, and this is in the negative. So his statement: 'in truth and falsehood' refers to each of the two matters, and by 'truth' is meant existence, and by 'falsehood' is meant non-existence. His statement: 'and it is the true [disjunctive]'—so it is the true one, named thus because it contains complete disjunction, as if it is the real disjunctive; its rule is that it is composed of a thing and its contradictory, like 'this number is either even or not even', and of a thing and what is equal to its contradictory, like 'this number is either even or odd'. So from this it appears that its two sides do not combine in existence and do not both cease to exist, because two contradictories neither combine nor both cease, and similarly a thing and that which is equal to its contradictory do not both cease nor combine. And it is either positive or negative. The positive: it is that in which mutual exclusion of two relationships is judged in truth and falsehood together, like our saying 'this number is either even or odd', for the evenness and oddness of the number are mutually exclusive in truth and falsehood—meaning they are not both true and not both false. The negative: it is that in which the absence of mutual exclusion of two relationships is judged in truth and falsehood, like our saying 'not the [؟] is such that this is either black or a writer', for they can be both true and both false, and there is no mutual exclusion between them in truth and falsehood.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the disjunctive conditional proposition (al-munfasila). It first defines the true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) as that which judges either the mutual exclusion of two relationships or the absence of that mutual exclusion, considered together in truth and falsehood. The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'or the absence of their mutual exclusion' refers to the negative form, and that 'truth' here means existence and 'falsehood' means non-existence. The passage then explains why it is called 'true' (haqiqiyya): because it embodies complete disjunction, like the real disjunctive. Its defining characteristic (dabit) is that it is composed either of a thing and its contradictory (like 'this number is either even or not even') or of a thing and what is equal to its contradictory (like 'this number is either even or odd'). In such propositions, the two sides never coincide in truth or falsehood. Next, the passage divides the true disjunctive into positive and negative. The positive (mujaba) affirms mutual exclusion, as in the example of a number being either even or odd—these two cannot be true together nor false together. The negative (saliba) denies mutual exclusion, as in the example of something being either black or a writer—these two can both be true (if it is a black writer) or both false (if it is a non-black non-writer). This classification is essential for understanding the types of disjunctive conditionals used in logical arguments, particularly in syllogisms and dialectical exchanges in the Islamic sciences.

Key Takeaway
The true disjunctive (haqiqiyya) is defined by the mutual exclusion of its two parts in both truth and falsehood, and it is divided into positive (affirming exclusion) and negative (denying exclusion).

Exclusive vs Inclusive Disjunctives in Islamic Logic
Defines the exclusive disjunctive and distinguishes it from the inclusive disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mani'at al-jam' (exclusive disjunction) and mani'at al-khuluw (inclusive disjunction) and identify their composition rules using the concepts of specific and general contradictories.

Core Idea
Mani'at al-jam' excludes truth together; mani'at al-khuluw excludes falsehood together.

Islamic logic distinguishes two types of disjunctive propositions: exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') and exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw). Understanding their truth conditions and composition is essential for analyzing conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) used in fiqh and kalam. This passage provides the definitions and structural rules for constructing both types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types and their truth conditions
Read the passage to find the definitions: mani'at al-jam' rules that the two parts cannot be true together but may be false together; mani'at al-khuluw rules that they cannot be false together but may be true together. Note the example of even and odd numbers illustrating mani'at al-jam'.
In a positive mani'at al-jam', both parts cannot be true, but both can be false. In a positive mani'at al-khuluw, both parts cannot be false, but both can be true.
2
Understand the composition rule for positives
The passage states: mani'at al-jam' is composed of a thing and the more specific (a'khass) than its contradictory; mani'at al-khuluw is composed of a thing and the more general (a'amm) than its contradictory. For example, if the thing is 'human', its contradictory is 'non-human'. The more specific than 'non-human' might be 'animal' (since non-human includes animals, but not all non-humans are animals? Actually, 'animal' is more specific than 'non-human'? Wait, 'non-human' includes everything not human, so 'animal' is a subset, so it is more specific. The more general than 'non-human' might be 'living being' (since non-human includes non-living things as well, so 'living being' is more general? Let's stick to the passage logic).
The rule applies to positive disjunctives. For negatives, the composition is reversed, as the commentator notes.
3
Apply the composition to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqh statement: 'This act is either wajib or mubah.' Wajib and mubah are mutually exclusive (cannot both be true) but can both be false (if the act is haram or makruh). Therefore, it is a case of mani'at al-jam'. Now identify the thing and its contradictory: let the thing be 'wajib'. Its contradictory is 'non-wajib'. The more specific than 'non-wajib' that excludes conjunction with wajib? Actually, 'mubah' is a specific type of non-wajib (since non-wajib includes mubah, haram, makruh, etc.), so it fits the composition rule for mani'at al-jam'.
This shows how the rule helps construct valid disjunctive propositions in Islamic reasoning.
4
Distinguish the two types in a syllogism
In a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), the validity depends on the type. For mani'at al-jam', denying one part does not necessarily affirm the other, because both can be false. For mani'at al-khuluw, affirming one part does not negate the other, because both can be true. Practice recognizing which type is used in a given argument.
This distinction prevents logical mistakes when drawing conclusions from disjunctive premises.

قَوْلُهُ : لَا يَصْدُقَانِ أي: لا يجتمعان، وقولُهُ: (وَلَا يكذبانِ)؛ أي: وَلَا يرتفعان، فليس شيء مِنَ العددِ زوج وفرد في آن واحدٍ، وَلَا شيء منه غيرُ زوج وغيرُ فرد.

(قَوْلُهُ : المركبة ... إلخ) هذا في موجبتهما، وأما في سالبتهما فعلى العكس، كما سيبينه
Or only in truth, then it is the exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'). And it is also either positive or negative. His statement: 'they are not both true' meaning they do not combine; and his statement: 'and they are not both false' meaning they do not both cease to exist; so nothing of the number is both even and odd at the same time, and nothing of it is neither even nor odd. His statement: 'judged [؟] in it with absence of mutual exclusion in two relationships'—in it, there is no mutual exclusion at all, so it is a terminological negation. His statement: 'so the exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') is that composed of a thing and the more specific (a'khass) than its contradictory; and the exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) is that composed of a thing and the more general (a'amm) than its contradictory.' (His statement: 'composed... etc.') This is for their positives; as for their negatives, the opposite, as he will clarify.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage defines the exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam') and distinguishes it from the inclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw). The matn states: 'Or only in truth, then it is the exclusive of conjunction'—meaning if the judgment of mutual exclusion is restricted to truth alone (i.e., the two parts cannot be true together but can be false together), the proposition is called mani'at al-jam' (exclusive of conjunction). This type is also divided into positive and negative. The commentator explains that in a positive mani'at al-jam', the two sides cannot combine in existence (truth) but can both cease to exist (falsehood), using the example of a number being both even and odd (impossible) and being neither even nor odd (possible for non-numbers? Actually, the example illustrates that for numbers, it is impossible to be both or neither, but the logical point stands). He then clarifies that the negative of mani'at al-jam' denies the exclusive relation. The passage then gives the structural rule: mani'at al-jam' is composed of a thing and something more specific (a'khass) than its contradictory, while mani'at al-khuluw is composed of a thing and something more general (a'amm) than its contradictory. For example, 'This is either a human or a non-human' would be haqiqiyya; but 'This is either a human or an animal'—here animal is more general than non-human (since non-human includes animals and plants, etc.), so it is mani'at al-khuluw? Actually, the rule states the opposite: mani'at al-khuluw is composed of a thing and the more general than its contradictory. So if the thing is 'human', its contradictory is 'non-human'; the more general than non-human is something like 'living being' (since all non-humans are living? Not exactly). The commentary notes that the rule given applies to positives; for negatives, the composition is reversed. This careful distinction is crucial for analyzing disjunctive conditional propositions in logical reasoning, especially in qiyas istithna'i (hypothetical syllogism) used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.
