Understanding Mani'at al-Jam' and Mani'at al-Khulu (Exclusive vs Exhaustive Disjunctions)
Defines two types of disjunctive conditionals: exclusive and exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exclusive (مانعة الجمع) and exhaustive (مانعة الخلو) disjunctive conditionals and identify each type in Islamic legal propositions.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunctions prevent both true; exhaustive disjunctions prevent both false.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive conditionals are not simply 'either/or'. They come in two varieties: one that prevents both statements from being true (مانعة الجمع) and one that prevents both from being false (مانعة الخلو). This distinction is critical for analyzing legal rulings and theological assertions that use 'either...or' phrasing. In this lesson, we will parse the definitions, test examples, and apply them to real cases from fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parts
Look for a disjunctive proposition that presents two options (e.g., 'This action is either wajib or mubah'). Identify the two parts (A and B).
The two parts are usually mutually exclusive in some sense.
2
Test for Conjunction (both true)
Ask: Can A and B both be true (sadiq) at the same time? If no, then it is a candidate for مانعة الجمع (exclusive). If both can be true, it is not exclusive.
In fiqh, wajib and mubah cannot both be true for the same act, so they satisfy this condition.
3
Test for Emptiness (both false)
Ask: Can both A and B be false (kadhib) at the same time? If no, then it is a candidate for مانعة الخلو (exhaustive). If both can be false, it is not exhaustive.
In fiqh, 'valid' and 'invalid' cannot both be false for a given contract; it must be one or the other.
4
Determine the Type
Use the two tests: if it prevents conjunction (both true) but allows emptiness (both false), it is مانعة الجمع. If it prevents emptiness (both false) but allows conjunction (both true), it is مانعة الخلو.
Some disjunctions may prevent both (مانعة الجمع والخلو) but that is a third type not covered here.
5
Apply to the Negative Form
Practice converting an exclusive disjunction to its negative form (which becomes exhaustive). For example, the negative of 'It is either A or B (not both)' is 'It is not the case that it is neither A nor B' – this permits both being true but prevents both being false.
This transformation helps understand the logical relationship between the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ
فالموجبةُ : هي التي حُكم فيها بتنافي الجزأين في الصدق فقط كَقولِنَا : هذا الشَّيءُ إِمَّا شجرٌ وإِما حجرٌ»، فإنَّهما لا يصدقان، ولكن يكذبان بأن يكون إنساناً .
والسَّالبةُ : هي التي حُكِمَ فيها بعدم تنافي الجزأين في الصدق فقط كَقولنا : «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الشَّيء لا شجراً وَلا حجراً». فإنَّهما يصدقان ولا يكذبان وإلا ؛ لَكان شجراً وحجّراً معاً .
والحاصلُ : أنَّ مانعة الجمعِ هِي التي تمنعُ الجمع، وتجوزُ الخلوّ كَقولك: هذا الشَّيءُ؛ إمَّا شجرٌ أو حجر، وهذه هي الموجبة، وأما سالبتُهَا ؛ فهي تنفي مَنْعَ الجمعِ ، وتمنعُ الخلوّ ، فَهِيَ عكس الموجبةِ كَقولنا : ليس هذا الشَّيءُ إِمَّا لَا شجراً ولا حجراً، وهي تجوِّزُ الجمع بأن يكون إنساناً، وتمنع الخلو؛ لأنَّ رفع لا شجرٍ ؛ شجر، ورفع لا حجر ؛ حَجَرٌ ، فَلَو جُوِّزَ الخلو؛ لَزِمَ أنْ يكونَ الشَّيءُ حَجَراً وشجراً، وهو باطل لما يلزم عليه من جمع النقيضين.
(قَوْلُهُ : فَمَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ) ضابطها : أنْ تتركَّبَ مِنَ الشَّيء، والأعمُّ مِن نقيضِهِ؛
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional.
So the affirmative [exclusive] is that in which the two parts are judged to be mutually exclusive in truth only, like our saying: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' – for they are not both true, but they are both false if it is a human being.
And the negative [exclusive] is that in which the two parts are judged not to be mutually exclusive in truth only, like our saying: 'It is not the case that this thing is neither tree nor stone' – for they are both true and not both false; otherwise it would be tree and stone together.
And the conclusion: that 'preventer of conjunction' is that which prevents conjunction and permits emptiness, like your saying: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' – this is the affirmative. As for its negative, it negates the prevention of conjunction and prevents emptiness, so it is the opposite of the affirmative, like our saying: 'It is not the case that this thing is neither tree nor stone.' It permits conjunction (e.g., being a human) and prevents emptiness because lifting 'not tree' gives tree, and lifting 'not stone' gives stone. If emptiness were permitted, the thing would have to be both stone and tree, which is false because it would entail the conjunction of two contradictories.
(His saying: 'So the preventer of emptiness') Its rule is that it is composed of the thing and the more general of its contradictory.
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage defines the two main types of disjunctive conditional propositions (الشرطية المنفصلة). The author first defines the affirmative exclusive disjunction (الموجبة من مانعة الجمع): it judges that the two parts cannot both be true, but they can both be false. The example given is 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' – if it is a human, both parts are false, which is allowed. Then the negative exclusive disjunction (السالبة من مانعة الجمع) is defined as judging that the two parts are not mutually exclusive in truth only – i.e., they can both be true. The example is 'It is not the case that this thing is neither tree nor stone' – which is equivalent to saying it is either tree or stone or both? Actually the text says they are both true if it is a human (since it is not a tree and not a stone? Wait, careful: 'ليس إما أن يكون هذا الشيء لا شجراً ولا حجراً' – that is a negation of an exclusive 'either not... or not...'? The commentary clarifies that the negative of the exclusive prevents emptiness and permits conjunction. The author then summarizes: 'مانعة الجمع' (preventer of conjunction) is the affirmative that prevents both being true but allows both false. Its negative (السالبة) negates that prevention and instead prevents emptiness (khulu), i.e., it does not allow both to be false. The rule for 'مانعة الخلو' (preventer of emptiness) is that it is composed of the thing and the more general of its contradictory. This classification is crucial for understanding how disjunctive propositions function in logical inference, especially in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). In Islamic legal theory, such disjunctions appear in rulings like 'This action is either obligatory or recommended' – if it is not obligatory, it may be recommended, and if it is not recommended, it may be obligatory, but it cannot be neither (i.e., emptiness is prevented).

مانعة الخلو

Key Takeaway
The two types of disjunctive conditionals – مانعة الجمع and مانعة الخلو – are distinguished by whether they prevent both parts being true or both being false, and this distinction is fundamental for correct logical inference in Islamic sciences.

The Strict Definition of Contradiction: Mutamāniʿān bi al-Dhāt
Refining the definition of contradiction with two interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the strict conditions for contradiction (mutamāniʿān bi al-dhāt) and explain why concepts alone do not meet them.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires mutual exclusion in existence, not just conceptual opposition.

The passage refines the definition of contradiction by distinguishing two senses of 'opposite'. The strict sense, mutamāniʿān bi al-dhāt, applies only to propositions. This lesson unpacks that strict definition and its implications, including what it excludes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of 'naqīḍān'
In the passage, 'Abd al-Ḥakīm presents two interpretations: one strict (mutamāniʿān bi al-dhāt) and one broad (mutanāfiyān). Locate where he defines each.
The strict interpretation requires mutual exclusion in reality; the broad only conceptual remoteness.
2
Extract the conditions of the strict interpretation
Under the strict sense, two things must by their essence necessitate the non-existence of the other in reality. For example, affirmation and negation: if one obtains, the other is negated.
This is the sense used in logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) between propositions.
3
Apply the strict interpretation to concepts vs. propositions
Concepts like 'human' and 'non-human' do not satisfy this because both can be mentally present. Only when related to something (forming a proposition) does contradiction arise.
Thus, strict contradiction is limited to propositions, not isolated concepts.
4
Understand what the definition excludes
The passage notes that the definition excludes differences between two individuals (e.g., 'Zayd' and 'not-Zayd') or between a term and a proposition. These are not contradictions because they lack the same subject and predicate with conflicting quality.
This ensures the definition is precise.
5
Conclude why only propositions satisfy the strict sense
Only propositions involve predication, where affirmation and negation can mutually exclude each other in truth value. Concepts in themselves do not involve truth values.

فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ

ومِن هَهُنا قِيلَ : نقيضُ كُلِّ شيءٍ رَفْعُها . هـ. وذكرَ السَّيِّدُ في حاشية المطالع أنَّ المفهوم المفرد إذا اعتُبِرَ في نفسِهِ ؛ لمْ يُتصوَّرُ لَهُ نقيضُ ، إلا بأنْ ينضم إليه معنى كلمةِ النَّفي، فيحصلُ مفهوم آخَرُ في غاية البعد عنه، ويُسمَّى رفع المفهوم في نفسِهِ وإِنِ اعتُبِرَ صِدقُ المفهوم على شيءٍ ؛ فَنَقيضُ ذلك المفهوم بهذا الاعتبارِ سَلبُهُ ؛ أي : سلبُ صِدقِهِ ورَفعُهُ عَمَّا اعْتُبِرَ بالاعتبارين، وأما في التصديقاتِ ؛ فَلَا يتحقق فيها إلا القسم الأوَّلُ؛ إذْ لا يمكن اعتبار صدقها وحملها على شيء، وإنَّ معنى قولهم : نقيضُ كُلِّ شيءٍ رفعُهُ ، سواء كانَ رفعُهُ في نفسه أو رفعه عن شيءٍ (قَوْلُهُ : أَيْ : لِذَاتِ الاخْتِلَافِ قال في شرح المطالع : ثمَّ إِنَّهُ رَبَّما وقع في
Chapter on Contradiction. Then what was mentioned is based on the fact that concepts have opposites, and it was said: they have no opposites. And the logicians' statement: 'The opposites of two equal things are equal', and 'the converse of the opposite is such' etc., is interpreted metaphorically as al-Khayyalī verified, because if the relation between them is considered, mutual repulsion occurs, either in truth and falsehood, or in truth only, as will be explained. 'Abd al-Ḥakīm said in his Ḥāshiyah on al-Khayyālī: The truth is that if 'two opposites' are interpreted as two things that mutually exclude each other intrinsically, i.e., two things that mutually repel and push each other such that by its essence the realization of one in reality necessitates the non-existence of the other, and vice versa, like affirmation and negation—for when affirmation obtains between two things, negation is negated, and vice versa—then a concept (i.e., a form) has no opposite, since the realization of a form does not necessitate the non-existence of another form. Indeed, the two forms of 'human' and 'non-human' are both present with no repulsion between them, unless their relation to something is considered; then two contradictory propositions arise, contradictory in truth if the negation particle is not referred to the relation of 'human' to something but is considered a part of it, and if the negation particle is referred to it, they are contradictory in truth and falsehood. Similarly for restrictional and relational concepts: there is no repulsion between them except by observing the occurrence of that relation and its removal according to the two considerations mentioned for simple terms. And if 'two opposites' are interpreted as two things that are mutually repelling, i.e., each of which is repelling to the other by its essence, whether there is mutual prevention in realization and non-realization as in propositions, or merely remoteness in concept such that when one is measured against the other it is more distant than anything else, then a concept has an opposite, like 'human' and 'non-human'. Hence it is said: 'The opposite of every thing is its removal.' End. And al-Sayyid mentioned in his Ḥāshiyah on al-Maṭāliʿ that a simple concept when considered in itself has no opposite conceived for it, except by adding the meaning of the negation word, resulting in another concept extremely distant from it, called the removal of the concept in itself. And if the truth of the concept of something is considered, then the opposite of that concept in this respect is its negation, i.e., the negation of its truth and its removal from what was considered true of it. The first is an opposite in the sense of the modified (maʿdūl), and the second in the sense of negation (salab). End. ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm said: So it is known from this that the opposite in concepts is realized in its two divisions: its removal in itself and its removal from something, according to the two considerations. As for assents (taṣdīqāt), only the first division is realized, because it is impossible to consider their truth and predication to something. And the meaning of their statement 'the opposite of every thing is its removal' is whether its removal in itself or its removal from something. End. (His statement: 'The difference of two individuals is excluded') i.e., excluded by his statement 'two propositions.' That is the same whether it is a differentia or a property, based on whether the definition is a complete definition (ḥadd) or a description (rasm). (His statement: 'i.e., by its essence') meaning by the essence of the difference, he said in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ: Then indeed, sometimes there occurs in their statements difference...
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (ḥāshiyah) on a previous text, refining the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and its application to concepts versus propositions. It begins by noting that logicians typically restrict contradiction to propositions, not individual concepts, but some claim concepts have opposites. The main discussion is given by ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm, who presents two interpretations of 'two opposites' (naqīḍān). The first interpretation defines them as two things that mutually exclude each other in existence (e.g., affirmation and negation). Under this strict sense, concepts like 'human' and 'non-human' are not opposites because both can be mentally present simultaneously. They only become contradictory when considered in relation to something, i.e., when formed into a proposition. The second interpretation is broader: opposites are things that are mutually exclusive in concept (extreme remoteness). In this sense, 'human' and 'non-human' are opposites. This is the basis for the saying 'the opposite of everything is its removal' (rafʿuhu). Al-Sayyid adds that a simple concept in itself cannot have an opposite unless the negation particle is attached, creating a modified concept (maʿdūl). Applying the concept to something yields its negation (salab). ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm concludes that in concepts both types of opposite (removal in itself and removal from something) are possible, but in propositions (taṣdīqāt), only the first type occurs because propositions cannot be considered as predicates. The passage then explains the definition's condition 'by its essence' and notes that the definition excludes differences between individuals (e.g., 'Zayd' and 'not Zayd') or between a term and a proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'not ʿAmr'). These are not genuine contradictions because they do not involve the same subject and predicate with conflicting quality.

Checkpoint
According to the strict interpretation (mutamāniʿān bi al-dhāt), which of the following is a genuine contradiction?
A) The concepts 'standing' and 'not-standing' considered in themselves
B) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not-standing'
C) 'Human' and 'non-human'
D) 'Zayd' and 'not-Zayd'

Key Takeaway
In the strict sense, contradiction requires mutual exclusion in existence; only propositions, not isolated concepts, can be contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The world is temporally originated' and 'The world is eternal'. Check if they are contradictory in the strict sense. What conditions must hold?
?
Identify a pair of concepts (e.g., 'human' and 'non-human') that are not strictly contradictory. Explain why using the mutamāniʿān condition.

Hadd and Rasm
advanced
10 min
Contradiction in Concepts: Broad vs. Narrow Sense
Clarifying the two types of opposite for concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of opposite for concepts (rafʿu nafsih and rafʿu ʿan shay’) and identify which applies to concepts vs. propositions.

Core Idea
Concepts have opposites in a broad sense via removal (rafʿ), but only one type applies to propositions.

While the strict definition reserves contradiction for propositions, a broader interpretation allows concepts to have opposites. This lesson explores the two kinds of 'removal' – in itself and from something – and how they align with the modified (maʿdūl) and negative (salab) opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the broader interpretation of 'naqīḍān'
After presenting the strict sense, 'Abd al-Ḥakīm gives a broader reading: mutanāfiyān, where things are mutually repelling even if only in concept. Locate this definition.
This sense includes mutual remoteness in meaning, not necessarily existential exclusion.
2
Understand the two types of removal (rafʿ)
Al-Sayyid explains that a simple concept in itself gets an opposite by adding the negation particle, resulting in rafʿu nafsih (removal in itself). When considered as applied to something, the opposite is rafʿu ʿan shay’ (removal from something).
The first corresponds to maʿdūl (e.g., 'non-human'), the second to salab (e.g., 'not human' as a predicate).
3
Recognize the distinction between maʿdūl and salab
Maʿdūl is the negation of the concept itself, creating a new concept. Salab is the negation of the truth of the concept for a subject.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how concepts can be opposites.
4
Apply the distinction: concepts have both, propositions only one
'Abd al-Ḥakīm concludes that concepts can have both types of opposite. However, assents (propositions) have only the first type (rafʿu nafsih) because propositions cannot themselves be predicated of something.
This limits the scope of opposition for propositions.

(His statement: 'i.e., by its essence') meaning by the essence of the difference, he said in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ: Then indeed, sometimes there occurs in their statements difference...
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (ḥāshiyah) on a previous text, refining the definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and its application to concepts versus propositions. It begins by noting that logicians typically restrict contradiction to propositions, not individual concepts, but some claim concepts have opposites. The main discussion is given by ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm, who presents two interpretations of 'two opposites' (naqīḍān). The first interpretation defines them as two things that mutually exclude each other in existence (e.g., affirmation and negation). Under this strict sense, concepts like 'human' and 'non-human' are not opposites because both can be mentally present simultaneously. They only become contradictory when considered in relation to something, i.e., when formed into a proposition. The second interpretation is broader: opposites are things that are mutually exclusive in concept (extreme remoteness). In this sense, 'human' and 'non-human' are opposites. This is the basis for the saying 'the opposite of everything is its removal' (rafʿuhu). Al-Sayyid adds that a simple concept in itself cannot have an opposite unless the negation particle is attached, creating a modified concept (maʿdūl). Applying the concept to something yields its negation (salab). ʿAbd al-Ḥakīm concludes that in concepts both types of opposite (removal in itself and removal from something) are possible, but in propositions (taṣdīqāt), only the first type occurs because propositions cannot be considered as predicates. The passage then explains the definition's condition 'by its essence' and notes that the definition excludes differences between individuals (e.g., 'Zayd' and 'not Zayd') or between a term and a proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'not ʿAmr'). These are not genuine contradictions because they do not involve the same subject and predicate with conflicting quality.

Key Terms
تناقض
نقيض
متمانعان
متنافيان
رفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following has only one type of opposite (rafʿu nafsih)?
A) A simple concept like 'human'
B) A compound concept like 'tall human'
C) An assent (tasdiq) like 'Zayd is standing'
D) A negation of a concept like 'non-human'

Key Takeaway
Concepts have two types of opposites (removal in itself and removal from something), but propositions have only one (removal in itself).

Definition of Contradiction and Its Three Conditions
Author defines contradiction and explains its three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for two propositions to be contradictory according to classical Mantiq.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference, mutual exclusivity, and inherence by essence.

In this passage, the author defines logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) by stating what it excludes and what it requires. Understanding these conditions is crucial for any argument that relies on contradiction, such as proof by contradiction in theology or jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
What is the broad category under which contradiction falls? The author says 'difference' (ikhtilāf).
2
Extract the first differentia
Difference must be between two propositions, not between terms or a term and a proposition.
3
Extract the second differentia
The truth of each proposition must necessitate the falsehood of the other, and vice versa.
4
Extract the third differentia
This necessitation must be 'by essence' (li-dhātihi), not accidental.
5
Check the completeness of the definition
Does it include all contradictory pairs? Does it exclude non-contradictory pairs? The condition 'by essence' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الشَّنَاقُضِ. خَرَجَ اخْتِلَافُ مُفْرَدَيْنِ وَمُفْرَدٍ وَقَضِيَّةٍ. ثُمَّ الِاخْتِلَافُ قَدْ يَكُونُ بِحَيْثُ يَلْزَمُ لِذَاتِهِ مِنْ صِدْقِ كُلِّ مِنَ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ كَذِبُ الْأُخْرَى، وَمِنْ كَذِبِ كُلٍّ صِدْقُ الْأُخْرَى، وَقَدْ لَا يَكُونُ كَذَلِكَ. وَقَوْلُهُ: بِحَيْثُ يَلْزَمُ لِذَاتِهِ أَيْ لِذَاتِ الِاخْتِلَافِ. قَالَ فِي شَرْحِ الْمُطَالِعِ: ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ رُبَّمَا وَقَعَ فِي عِبَارَاتِهِمْ اخْتِلَافُ قَضِيَّتَيْنِ بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي لِذَاتِهِ صِدْقُ إِحْدَاهُمَا كَذِبَ الْأُخْرَى، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ لِذَاتِهِ عَائِداً إِلَى الصِّدْقِ لَا إِلَى الِاخْتِلَافِ، وَيَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ الْكُلِّيَّتَانِ كَقَوْلِنَا: كُلُّ ج ب، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنْ ج ب، فَإِنَّهُمَا مُخْتَلِفَانِ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي صِدْقُ إِحْدَاهُمَا لِذَاتِهِ كَذِبَ الْأُخْرَى، وَلَكِنْ يُجَابُ بِأَنَّ اقْتِضَاءَ صِدْقِ إِحْدَى الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ كَذِبَ الْأُخْرَى لَا لِذَاتِهِ بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى نَقِيضِ الْأُخْرَى.
Chapter on Contradiction. There emerges [the exclusion of] difference of two single terms, and of a single term and a proposition. Then difference may be such that it necessarily follows – by its essence – from the truth of each of the two propositions the falsehood of the other, and from the falsehood of each the truth of the other, and it may not be so. And his saying: 'such that it necessarily follows, by its essence' means by the essence of the difference. He said in Sharh al-Mutali‘: Then indeed sometimes in their expressions occurs 'difference of two propositions such that by its essence the truth of one necessitates the falsehood of the other'. In that case, 'by its essence' refers back to truth, not to difference. And both universal propositions, like our saying: 'Every J is B' and 'No J is B', are problematic for it, for they differ by affirmation and negation, such that the truth of one by its essence necessitates the falsehood of the other. But it can be answered that the necessitation of the falsehood of the other by the truth of one of the two propositions is not by its essence, but rather by virtue of its inclusion of the contradictory of the other.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of logical contradiction (tanāquḍ). It begins by stating the genus of the definition: 'difference' (ikhtilāf). Then it specifies the differentiae. First, the difference must be between two propositions (not between two terms or a term and a proposition). Second, the difference must be such that the truth of each proposition necessitates the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. Third, this necessitation must be 'by essence' (li-dhātihi), meaning it arises from the very nature of the propositions themselves, not from accidental factors. The passage then presents a quote from Sharh al-Mutali‘, which raises an objection: In universal propositions like 'Every J is B' and 'No J is B', the truth of one does necessitate the falsehood of the other, but is this 'by essence'? The answer is that it is not by essence; rather, it is because each proposition contains the contradictory of the other. Thus, the condition 'by essence' excludes cases where the necessity is mediated by something else, such as the material content of the terms.

Key Terms
تناقض
قضية
لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for contradiction?
A) The two propositions must differ by quality (affirmation/negation).
B) The truth of each must entail the falsehood of the other.
C) The falsehood of each must entail the truth of the other.
D) The entailment must be by essence (li-dhātihi).

Key Takeaway
The precise definition of contradiction includes three conditions: difference between propositions, mutual entailment of truth values, and the necessity of that entailment by the essence of the difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the pair: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' and 'Some intoxicants are not forbidden'. Are they contradictory according to the definition? Check each condition: difference between propositions, mutual entailment of truth/falsehood, and by essence.
?
Consider the pair: 'Zayd is in the house' and 'Zayd is in the market'. Do they meet the conditions for contradiction? Why or why not?

The 'By Essence' Condition: Objection and Resolution
Commentator resolves an objection regarding universal propositions and the 'by essence' condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal affirmative and a universal negative are not contradictory by essence according to the commentator, and how the answer resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative and universal negative are contradictory in effect, but not by essence, because the necessity is mediated by the content of the terms.

The author has defined contradiction with the condition 'by essence' (li-dhātihi). But a problem arises: universal propositions like 'Every J is B' and 'No J is B' seem to satisfy the condition, yet they are not considered true contradictories by essence. The commentator explains why and offers a resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
What is the problematic pair of propositions? 'Every J is B' and 'No J is B'.
2
Understand the objection
These two differ by affirmation and negation, and the truth of one necessitates the falsehood of the other. Why might someone think this is contradictory by essence?
3
Analyze the response
The commentator says the necessity is not by essence, but because each proposition contains the contradictory of the other. What does that mean?
4
Apply the distinction
Can you think of another pair where the necessity is by essence? For example, 'Every J is B' and 'Some J is not B' are true contradictories because the truth of one directly entails the falsehood of the other without intermediary.

Source Text

Three Conditions for Contradiction in Propositions
Author explains the three conditions for contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions required for two propositions to be contradictory and verify whether a given pair satisfies them.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference in quality, quantity (if quantified), and modality.

In this passage, the author lays out the essential conditions for two propositions to be truly contradictory. Without these conditions, two propositions may be contrary, subcontrary, or not logically opposed at all. You will learn to apply these conditions to any pair of propositions from Islamic theology or jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify difference in quality
Check whether one proposition is affirmative (ījāb) and the other negative (salab). This is the most fundamental condition and always required.
If both are affirmative or both negative, they cannot be contradictory.
2
Check quantity if propositions are quantified
If the propositions are not personal (i.e., they are universal or particular), they must also differ in quantity: one universal and one particular. Personal propositions (e.g., Zaid is standing) only require quality difference.
Two universal propositions are contrary, not contradictory; two particular propositions are subcontrary.
3
Verify difference in modality
Ensure the propositions have different modalities (e.g., necessity vs. possibility, perpetuity vs. absoluteness). This avoids temporal or modal ambiguity.
Modality ensures the propositions are about the same mode of existence.
4
Apply the conditions to a sample pair
Take an example like 'Every prayer is valid' (universal affirmative) and 'Some prayer is not valid' (particular negative). They differ in quality and quantity, so they are contradictory. Confirm that modalities match (e.g., both about validity in the same timeframe).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخرج ما عدا التَّناقضِ عن التعريف، وانطبق عليه. ثمَّ بَيَّنَ الاختلاف المعتبر في تحقق التناقض، فقال: (وَلَا بُدَّ) التَّناقضِ مِنَ الاِخْتِلَافِ؛ أي: اختلافِ القضيَّتين (فِي الْكَيْفِ)؛ أي: الإيجاب والسلب. (وَ) في (الكم)؛ أي: الكلية والجزئية. فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ (وَ) في (الجِهَةِ)؛ أي: الضَّرورة، والإمكان، والدوام، والإطلاق، وغيرها من الجهات. فالقضيَّتانِ إنْ كانتا شَخصيَّتين؛ فلا بدَّ من الاختلاف في الكيف، وإن كانتا محصورتين؛ فلا بدَّ مع ذلك من الاختلافِ في الكم؛ لصدق الجزئيتين.
So whatever is other than contradiction exits from the definition, and it applies to it. Then he explained the valid difference in the realization of contradiction, saying: (And it is necessary) for contradiction to have difference; i.e., difference of the two propositions (in quality); i.e., affirmation and negation. (And) in (quantity); i.e., universality and particularity. Section: On Contradiction. (And) in (modality); i.e., necessity, possibility, perpetuity, absoluteness, and other modalities. So if the two propositions are individual, then difference in quality is necessary; and if they are quantified, then along with that difference in quantity is necessary, because the two particular propositions are true.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for contradiction between two propositions. The author begins by stating that the definition of contradiction applies only to contradictory pairs. He then explains the necessary differences: first, difference in quality (kayf), meaning one is affirmative and the other negative. This is the most fundamental condition, as any contradictory pair must differ in affirmation vs. negation. Second, difference in quantity (kamm), i.e., universality vs. particularity. This condition is required when the propositions are quantified; for individual (personal) propositions, quantity is irrelevant. The reason given is that two particular propositions (e.g., 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human') can both be true, so they are not contradictory without a quantity difference. Third, difference in modality (jiha), such as necessity, possibility, perpetuity, etc. This ensures that the propositions are about the same time or mode of existence. The author then distinguishes: for personal propositions (like 'Zaid is standing' and 'Zaid is not standing'), only quality matters. For quantified propositions (universal or particular), both quality and quantity must differ. This guards against the mistake of thinking that two universal propositions (e.g., 'Every human is animal' and 'No human is animal') are contradictory—they are actually contrary, since both can be false. Similarly, two particular affirmatives are not contradictory. Thus, the correct contradictory pair is a universal affirmative and a particular negative (or vice versa).

Key Terms
تناقض (tanāquḍ): contradiction between two propositions such that they cannot be simultaneously true or false; requires difference in quality, quantity (if quantified), and modality.
كيف (kayf): quality of a proposition: affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salab).
كم (kamm): quantity of a proposition: universality (kulliyya) or particularity (juz’iyya).
جهة (jiha): modality of a proposition: necessity, possibility, perpetuity, absoluteness, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which pair of propositions is contradictory according to the conditions in this passage?
A) Every prayer is valid / Some prayer is not valid
B) Every act of worship is valid / No act of worship is valid
C) Some act of worship is valid / Some act of worship is not valid
D) Every act of worship is valid / Every act of worship is not valid

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be contradictory, they must differ in quality, quantity (if quantified), and modality – this ensures the logical square of opposition is correctly applied.

The Unity of Relative Judgment in Contradiction
Author defines contradiction and establishes unity of relative judgment as essential condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two propositions are truly contradictory by checking the unity of their relative judgment.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity of the relative judgment, not just subject and predicate.

In classical logic, contradiction is defined by several conditions, but the author here argues that the most fundamental is the unity of the relative judgment. This means that the affirmative and negative propositions must be about exactly the same relation, including implicit qualifications of time, place, modality, etc. Without this, two statements that seem contradictory may actually both be true or both false.

Guided Path
1
Locate the affirmative and negative statements
Find the pair of statements being tested for contradiction. Identify which is affirmative and which is negative.
2
Identify the explicit subject and predicate
Write down the subject and predicate independently. This gives the surface structure of each statement.
3
Uncover implicit qualifications
Look for any implied time, place, modality (potential/actual), or relation (e.g., father of whom). These are often hidden in the relative judgment.
In the examples, 'Zayd is a father' implies a relation to a specific offspring; wine intoxicating implies a modality.
4
Examine the relative judgment
Check if the relation between subject and predicate, including all qualifications, is identical in both statements. This is the core condition.
The author states that unity of relative judgment automatically implies other unities.
5
Determine if contradiction holds
If the relative judgment is identical, it is a true contradiction. If not, they are not contradictory and may both be true or both false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ - وعند المحققين أنَّ المعتبر في تحقق التناقض وحدةُ النِّسبةِ الحكميَّة، حتَّى يردَ الإيجابُ والسَّلبُ على شيءٍ واحدٍ فإنَّ وحدتها تستلزم الوحدات. يرجعُ قولُنَا : زيد أب، وتريد لعمرو، زيد ليس بأَب، وتريد لعمرو، إلى قولنا : زيد أب لعمرو، زيد ليس بأَب لعمرو . ويرجع قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنّ مُسكِرٌ، وتريد بالقوَّةِ، الخمر في الدَّنِّ ليس بِمُسكر، وتريد بالقوَّة إلى قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنّ مُسكر بالقوَّةِ، الخمر في الدَّنِّ ليسَ بِمُسكر بالقوة. ويرجعُ قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنَّ مُسكر، تريد بالفعل، الخمر في الدَّنِّ بِمُسكر، وتريد بالفعل، إلى قولنا : الخمرُ في الدَّنَّ مُسكر بالفعل، الخمرُ في الدَّنِّ ليسَ بِمُسكر بالفعل .
Chapter: On Contradiction. According to the verified scholars, what is considered in the realization of contradiction is the unity of the relative judgment, so that affirmation and negation refer to one thing; for its unity necessitates the unities. Our statement: 'Zayd is a father' meaning for 'Amr, and 'Zayd is not a father' meaning for 'Amr, reduces to our statement: 'Zayd is a father of 'Amr, Zayd is not a father of 'Amr.' And our statement: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning potentially, and 'Wine in the jar is not intoxicating' meaning potentially, reduces to our statement: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating potentially, Wine in the jar is not intoxicating potentially.' And our statement: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning actually, and 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating' meaning actually, reduces to our statement: 'Wine in the jar is intoxicating actually, Wine in the jar is not intoxicating actually.'
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of contradiction (tanāquḍ) by specifying its essential condition: the unity of the relative judgment (waḥdat al-nisbah al-ḥukmiyyah). The author begins by stating that, according to the verified scholars (muḥaqqiqīn), what matters for contradiction is that the affirmative and negative judgments have the same relative relation to a single subject. This unity of the relative judgment automatically implies the other unities (such as subject, predicate, time, place, etc.) because if the relation is one, the related terms are necessarily unified. To illustrate, the author provides examples where implicit qualifications are made explicit. For instance, saying 'Zayd is a father' meaning 'of 'Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father' meaning 'of 'Amr' reduces to 'Zayd is a father of 'Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of 'Amr', thus making the relative judgment explicit. Similarly, for wine being intoxicating potentially or actually, the implicit modal qualification (bi-al-quwwa / bi-al-fiʿl) must be made explicit to achieve true contradiction. The key point is that without unifying the relative judgment—the relation between subject and predicate with all its conditions—the statements can both be true or both false, and thus not contradictory.

Key Terms
التناقض
النسبة الحكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs is a true contradiction according to the condition of unity of relative judgment?
A) 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father' when the first means of 'Amr and the second of Khalid.
B) 'Wine is intoxicating' and 'Wine is not intoxicating' without specifying potential or actual.
C) 'Zayd is a father of 'Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of 'Amr'.
D) 'The world is created' and 'The world is not created' but the first means in essence and the second in time.

Key Takeaway
The essential condition for contradiction is the unity of the relative judgment; without it, statements may both be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Given the two statements: 'Salah is obligatory' and 'Salah is not obligatory', but the first means obligatory at its prescribed time and the second means obligatory at all times (i.e., a general obligation). Determine if they are truly contradictory by examining the unity of the relative judgment. (Answer: They are not contradictory because the relative judgment differs: one is temporally qualified, the other is not.)
?
Task: In a debate, a mutakallim says 'The world is temporally originated' and his opponent says 'The world is not temporally originated'. The first means in terms of time, the second means in terms of essence. Are these contradictory? Use the condition of unity of relative judgment to decide. (Answer: No, because the relative judgment differs; one is about temporal origination, the other about essential origination.)

The Wavering Concept: Not the Contradictory but a Necessary Concomitant
Author explains the wavering concept as a necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the real contradictory of a compound proposition from its wavering concept, and explain why the wavering concept is a necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
The wavering concept is equivalent to the true contradictory but is not it.

This passage from a classical logic commentary clarifies a subtle point: the wavering concept (mafhūm muraddad) is often called the contradictory of a compound proposition, but strictly speaking, it is only a necessary concomitant (lāzim musāwin). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when dealing with compound propositions in logical analysis and ensures precise usage of terminology in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the real contradictory
Locate the author's statement: 'the contradictory of a thing is the raising of that thing.' For a compound proposition, the contradictory is the raising (negation) of the entire sum. This is the strict definition.
The real contradictory is the direct negation of the whole compound.
2
Identify the wavering concept
Find the description of the wavering concept: it is a disjunction between the negations of the two parts (e.g., 'either not-A or not-B'). Notice it is not the same as the negation of the whole.
The wavering concept is a compound proposition itself (a disjunction).
3
Understand why it is a necessary concomitant
The commentator explains that raising one part indeterminately occurs only when the whole is raised. Thus, the wavering concept is co-extensive (musāwin) with the true contradictory: whenever one is true, the other is true.
They are logically equivalent, yet conceptually distinct.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a compound proposition like 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid', produce its real contradictory ('It is not the case that either holds') and its wavering concept ('Either the prayer is not valid or it is not invalid'). Verify their equivalence.
This exercise solidifies the difference between real definition and convenient concomitant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للنَّقيض لا باعتبار أنَّه نقيضُ حقيقةً؛ إذ نقيضُ الشَّيء بالحقيقة هو رفعُ ذلك الشيء . والقضيَّة المركّبة لمَّا كانت عبارة عن مجموع قضيَّتين مختلفتين بالإيجاب والسَّلبِ؛ فَنقيضُها رفعُ ذلك المجموع، والمفهوم المردَّدُ ليس نفسَ الرَّفع ، لكنَّه لازم مساو له تأمَّلْ . (قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ نَقِيضُ الشَّيْء... إلخ) علَّةٌ للنَّفي، وهو قوله: (لا باعتبار). (قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ مُسَاءٍ لَهُ أي : لأنَّ المفهوم المردَّد رفع لأحد الجزئين لا على التَّعيين، وارتفاع ذلك الأحدِ لا يتحقق إلا بارتفاع المجموع الذي هو النقيض . (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ أمر بالتأمل ؛ لكون المقام دقيقاً . فإنْ قُلْتَ : مَا وجه تخصيص هذه بالتسامح ؟! ، فإنَّ جميع ما تقدَّمَ مِن الحمليَّاتِ أيضاً ليست بنقائض حقيقة، فإطلاق النقيض عليها تسامح، وذلك لأنَّ نقيض الشَّيء العطار يندفعُ مَا يُقالُ: إِنَّهُ لَا اختلاف بين المفهوم المردَّدِ والقضيَّةِ المركَّبة في الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ، ولا اتحادَ في النَّوعِ لِكون إحدَاهُمَا حَمليَّةٌ والأُخرَى مُنفصلةٌ، وَلَا في الجهةِ؛ لأنَّ المنفصلة لَيْسَتْ مِنَ الموجّهات في شيءٍ وإِنْ كانَ طرفَاهَا اختلاف هنا منها ؛ تَأَمَّلْ. (قَوْلُهُ: فَنَقِيضُهَا ) أي : نقيضُ القضيَّةِ المركَّبةِ رفعُ ذلك المجموع، وبيانه أنَّ نقيض الجزء الأوَّلِ مِنَ القضيَّةِ المركَّبة، ونقيض الجزء الثاني منها لَمَّا كانَ عبارةً عَن رفعِ مَجموعِهِمَا ؛ لزم اجتماعُ الرَّفْعَيْنِ، وَلَمَّا لم يمكن اجتماعُ الرَّفْعَيْنِ في النَّقيض ؛ لزمَ مِن ذلكَ الرَّفْعَيْن حصول القضيَّةِ المنفصلة المانعة الخلو؛ لأنَّ الجزأين في المانعة الخلوّ يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فيكون رفع الجزأين ملزوماً، والمفهوم المردَّدُ لازماً مُساوياً ، فَإطلاق اسم النقيض على المفهوم المردَّدِ باعتبارِ أنَّه لازم ؛ مُساءٍ لِذينك الرفعينِ . قَوْلُهُ: تَأَمَّلْ) أي : فى المثالِ المذكور لتقيس البقيَّةَ عليه.
For the contradictory not considering that it is the actual contradictory; because the actual contradictory of a thing is the raising of that thing. And since the compound proposition is the sum of two propositions differing in affirmation and negation, its contradictory is the raising of that sum. The wavering concept is not the raising itself, but it is a necessary concomitant of it. Reflect. (His saying: 'since the contradictory of the thing... etc.') is a reason for the negation, i.e., his saying: 'not considering'. (His saying: 'but it is a necessary concomitant') meaning: because the wavering concept is the raising of one of the two parts indeterminately, and that indeterminate raising is only realized by raising the sum which is the contradictory. (His saying: 'Reflect') is a command to reflect because the context is subtle. Then you say: What is the reason for specifying these with tolerance? For all the categorical propositions that preceded are also not actual contradictories, so applying the name contradictory to them is tolerance, because the contradictory of a thing... The 'Attar: It is repelled what is said: There is no difference between the wavering concept and the compound proposition in affirmation and negation, nor unity in type because one is categorical and the other is disjunctive, nor in modality because the disjunctive is not among the modalized [propositions] at all, even if its two terms differ here from them; reflect. (His saying: 'so its contradictory') i.e., the contradictory of the compound proposition is the raising of that sum, and its explanation is that when the contradictory of the first part of the compound proposition and the contradictory of its second part are each the raising of their sum, the combination of the two raisings follows, and since combining the two raisings in the contradictory is impossible, the two raisings necessitate the occurrence of the disjunctive proposition that prevents emptiness (man'at al-khulw); because the two parts in man'at al-khulw can be combined but cannot be both raised, so the raising of both parts is a consequent, and the wavering concept is a necessary concomitant. Thus applying the name contradictory to the wavering concept by virtue of its being a concomitant is a concomitant of those two raisings. (His saying: 'Reflect') i.e., in the mentioned example to analogize the rest to it.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory passage from a commentary on logic, dealing with the nature of the contradictory (naqīḍ) of a compound proposition. The author begins by clarifying that the term 'contradictory' applied to the wavering concept (al-mafhūm al-muraddad) is not used in the strict sense, because the real contradictory of a thing is simply the negation (raf‘) of that thing. A compound proposition, which consists of two simple propositions joined by a connective (like 'either...or' or 'if...then'), its contradictory would be the negation of the entire sum. The wavering concept—which is a disjunction between the negations of the two parts (e.g., 'either not-A or not-B')—is not itself the negation of the whole, but it is a necessary concomitant (lāzim musāwin) of that negation. The commentator then expands on the reasoning: the wavering concept raises one of the two parts indeterminately, but that indeterminate raising only occurs when the whole sum is negated. Thus it is a concomitant. A question is raised: why specify just compound propositions for this tolerance? After all, categorical propositions are also not literal contradictories (their contradictories are different forms). The answer (via al-'Attar) is that there is a difference: categorical and disjunctive are different types, and the disjunctive has no modality. Finally, a detailed explanation shows that the contradictory of a compound proposition is the raising of both parts, which yields a disjunctive proposition of the 'man'at al-khulw' type (i.e., exclusive disjunction where both cannot be true but can both be false). The conjunctive raising of both parts is impossible, so the disjunction between the negative parts arises. Hence the wavering concept is a necessary concomitant of the true contradictory. The passage guards against mistaking the wavering concept for the actual contradictory, emphasizing that it is only a necessary concomitant used for convenience in logical operations like conversion and syllogism.

Key Terms
نقيض
قضية مركبة
مفهوم مردد
لازم مساو

B) The simple negation of the entire compound proposition (raf‘ al-majmū‘).

Key Takeaway
The wavering concept is not the contradictory of a compound proposition but a necessary concomitant that is logically equivalent to it.

The Contradictory of Partial Compound Propositions: Beyond the Wavering Concept
Author states that the wavering concept suffices only for universal compound propositions, not for partial ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to (1) explain why the wavering concept is insufficient for partial compound propositions, and (2) identify the additional operation required to form the correct contradictory.

Core Idea
The wavering concept works for universal compound propositions but needs an extra step for partial ones.

In previous lessons, we learned that the contradictory of a universal compound proposition is formed by wavering between the contradictories of its two simple parts. However, this method fails when the compound proposition is partial (particular). The source passage from the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq explains why and introduces the true contradictory: the simple negation 'it is not so'. Let's explore this distinction and the three methods for forming the contradictory of a partial compound.

Guided Path
1
Recall the wavering concept for universal compound
Remember that for a universal compound proposition like 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational', the contradictory is formed by taking the disjunction of the contradictories of each part: 'Either some human is not an animal or some human is not rational'. This is the wavering concept.
2
Identify the problem with partial compound propositions
For a partial compound proposition like 'Some human is a writer and some human is not a writer', the wavering concept would yield 'Either every human is not a writer or every human is a writer', which is not the correct contradictory because it does not cover the possibility that both parts are false simultaneously. The wavering concept is insufficient.
3
Discover the true contradictory according to the source
The passage states that the real contradictory is simply the negation of the entire proposition: 'it is not so' (laysa kadhālik). For 'Some human is a writer and some human is not a writer', the contradictory is 'it is not the case that some human is a writer and some human is not a writer'.
This is the original, straightforward contradictory.
4
Learn the three methods for technical usage
The commentator outlines three methods logicians use to form the contradictory in a more convenient form for logical operations: (1) simple negation, (2) taking the wavering concept as a disjunction but adding a third disjunct to cover all possibilities (e.g., a triple disjunction), and (3) a similar but different modification (not fully detailed).
These methods are metaphorical conveniences, not the real contradictory in origin.
5
Understand the reason for the technical convention
Logicians adopted specific propositional forms as 'contradictories' metaphorically because they found them easier to use in conversions and syllogisms. The true contradictory (simple negation) is less convenient for systematic logical manipulation.
This explains why the wavering concept is still taught despite its insufficiency for partial compounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ. ثمَّ هذا المفهوم المردَّدُ إنَّما هو نقيضُ المركبة الكلِّيَّةِ، (لكن) في المركَّبة (الجُزْئِيَّةِ) لا يكفي في نقيضها ما ذكرنا من المفهوم المردَّدِ، بل الحقُّ في نقيضها الدسوقي في الحقيقة؛ رفْعُهُ بأنَّهُ ليس كذلك، حتَّى إنَّ نقيض قولنا مثلاً : كلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب؛ ليس كذلك، وكونُ النّقيض قضيَّةً مَخصوصةً على هيئة مخصوصةٍ هو خلافُ الأصل. والجواب : أنَّه في الأصل كذلك، ولكنْ لَمَّا أرادوا أخذَ النَّقيض قضيَّةً لها مفهوم محصل من القضايا المعتبرة فى الفنّ يسهل استعمالها في العكوس والأقيسة؛ أطلقوا اسمَ النَّقيض عليها ؛ لأنَّها من اللوازم المساوية تجوزا(۱)، وصار ذلك هو مُرادهم في حدّ التَّناقض، فقولهم : إنَّ نقيضها الحقيقي حملية؛ يعني: يجب ما ذكر في حقيقةِ التَّناقض عند أهل الفنّ . (قَوْلُهُ : مِنَ الْمَفْهُوْمِ) بيان لـ «ما ) . العطار (قَوْلُهُ: ثُمَّ هَذَا الْمَفْهُوْمُ الْمُرَدَّدُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ ذِكرُ الكلام بطريق الحصر يصير الاستدراكَ ضائعاً، فلو قال : ثمَّ هذا المفهوم المردَّدُ وإِنْ كفى في نقيض المركبةِ ؛ لكنَّه في الجزئيَّةِ لَا يكفي يكفي؛ لكان حسناً . (قَوْلُهُ : لَا يَكْفِي فِيهِ إشارة إلى أنَّ نقيضها مُسْتَمِلٌ على المفهومِ المردَّدِ بينَ نقيض الجزأين، إلا أنَّهُ وقعَ فِيهِ زيادةُ تَصرُّفٍ كَمَا سَنُبيِّنُ، فَالمراد نفي الكفاية بالطَّريقِ المذكور في الكُلِّيَّةِ ؛ أعني : تحليلها إلى بسيطتين والتَّرديد بينَ نَقيضهما (قَوْلُهُ : بَل الْحَقُّ) أي : الرَّاجحُ ، وهذا أحدُ طُرُقِ ثلاثة؛ ثانيها : أَنْ يُؤخذ المفهوم المردَّدُ على أصلِهِ مُنفصلةً، ولكنْ يُضم إليها جزء آخَرُ فَيُقالُ في المثالِ الآتي دائماً : إمَّا كُلُّ جسم حيوان دائماً، وإمَّا لا شيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً، وإمَّا بعضُ الجسم حيوان دائماً، وبعضُ الجسم ليس بحيوانٍ دائماً، فتكونُ المنفصلةُ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ أجزاء ثلاثة، ثالثها: أن يؤخذ المفهوم المردَّدُ كذلك، ولكن (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : تجوزاً ... إلخ علة لأطلقوا ؛ أي: بحسب الأصل وإن صار حقيقة عرفية عند أهل الفن، فلا ينافيه قوله الآتي أن نقيضها الحقيقي حملية ... إلخ . ا.هـ. أنَّ الشَّرنوبي .
Chapter on Contradiction. Then this wavering concept is only the contradictory of the universal compound [proposition]. (But) in the partial [compound proposition], what we mentioned from the wavering concept is not sufficient for its contradictory. Rather, the truth regarding its contradictory [Dassūqī] in reality is its negation by [saying] 'it is not so', such that the contradictory of our saying for example 'Every human is a writer' is 'it is not so'. And that the contradictory is a specific proposition in a specific form is contrary to the original. The answer: It is as such in origin, but when they wanted to take the contradictory as a proposition with a determinate concept from the propositions considered in the art, easy to use in conversions and syllogisms, they applied the name contradictory to it because it is from among the necessary concomitants metaphorically (1), and that became their intent in the definition of contradiction. So their saying that its real contradictory is categorical means: what was mentioned must be in the reality of contradiction according to the practitioners of the art. (His saying: 'from the concept' is an explanation of 'what'). The 'Attar: (His saying: 'Then this wavering concept...' is a mention of the speech in a way of restriction that makes the exception wasted. So if he said: 'Then this wavering concept, even if it suffices for the contradictory of the compound [universal], but in the partial it does not suffice...' it would be better. (His saying: 'does not suffice in it' indicates that its contradictory includes the wavering concept between the contradictories of the two parts, but there occurs in it an additional operation as we will explain. The intent is the denial of sufficiency by the way mentioned in the universal; i.e., analyzing it into two simple propositions and wavering between their contradictories. (His saying: 'Rather, the truth') i.e., the preponderant, and this is one of three methods; the second: that the wavering concept is taken as it is as a disjunction, but another part is added to it, so it is said in the following example always: 'Either every body is an animal always, or no body is an animal always, or some body is an animal always and some body is not an animal always', so the disjunction becomes composed of three parts. The third: that the wavering concept is taken similarly, but... (1) (His saying: metaphorically... etc.) is the reason for 'they applied'; i.e., according to the origin, even if it became a customary reality among the practitioners of the art, so it does not contradict his later saying that its real contradictory is categorical... etc. End. That is al-Sharnūbī.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological discussion focusing on the contradictory of compound propositions that are partial (juz'iyya), i.e., particular in quantifier. The author begins by stating that the previously discussed wavering concept (mafhūm muraddad) is sufficient only for universal compound propositions. For partial compound propositions, the wavering concept alone is insufficient. The commentator Dassūqī then clarifies that the true contradictory of a partial compound proposition is simply the negation of the whole (raf‘uhu bi-annahu laysa kadhālik), similar to a simple categorical proposition. This contradicts the usual technical usage where the contradictory is a specific proposition form. The answer is provided: originally, contradictions are simple negations, but for convenience in logical operations like conversion (aks) and syllogism (qiyās), the logicians accepted the wavering concept and other constructions as 'contradictories' metaphorically, as they are necessary concomitants. The commentator al-'Aṭṭār then critiques the phrasing of the main text, suggesting that it should have been expressed as an exception to emphasize that the wavering concept suffices for universal but not for partial. He then explains that for partial compound propositions, the contradictory includes the wavering concept but with an added operation (ziyādat taṣarruf). Three methods are mentioned: (1) the 'truth' method (the one described), (2) taking the wavering concept as a disjunction but adding a third disjunct (e.g., using a triple disjunction covering all cases), and (3) similarly taking the wavering concept but with another modification (not fully detailed here). The passage guards against assuming that the simple wavering concept works for all compound propositions, stressing the need for different treatments for universal vs. partial propositions.

Key Terms
مركبة كلية
مركبة جزئية
رفعه بأنه ليس كذلك
زيادة تصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because partial propositions have no contradictory.
B) Because the wavering concept only works for categorical propositions.
C) Because an additional operation is needed.
D) Because partial compound propositions cannot be analyzed into simple parts.

Key Takeaway
For partial compound propositions, the wavering concept alone is insufficient; the true contradictory is simply the negation of the whole, but logicians use more specific forms metaphorically for convenience in logical operations.

Ibn Sina's Condition for Inversion of Possible Propositions
Ibn Sina's condition on inversion of possible propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a possible proposition inverts according to Ibn Sina's requirement that the subject's description be actual.

Core Idea
Without actual instances, inversion of possibility fails.

Ibn Sina refines the rule of straight inversion by requiring that the subject's description be actually true in reality. This condition blocks the inversion of possible propositions when the subject lacks actual instances. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correct modal reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Locate the modal proposition. It will have a subject, predicate, and the modality 'possibly' (bi-l-imkan). In the text, the example is 'Every donkey is ridden by Zayd possibly'.
Ensure the proposition is of type 'possible' (general or specific).
2
Check the actuality condition
According to Ibn Sina, the subject's description (e.g., 'donkey') must be actually true of the subject in reality (thabit lil-mawdu' bi al-fi'l). This means the subject must have real instances.
If the description is only potential, inversion fails.
3
Form the inverse
The inverse of 'Every J is B possibly' is 'Some B is J possibly'. For the example, the inverse would be 'Some thing ridden by Zayd is a donkey possibly'.
The inversion swaps subject and predicate.
4
Test truth under Ibn Sina's condition
If Zayd never actually rides a donkey, then there is no actual instance of 'ridden by Zayd' that is a donkey, so the inverse is false. Thus the original proposition does not invert.
Contrast this with views that allow potential instances, where inversion would hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي

وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ الْعَامَّةِ وَالْخَاصَّةِ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّيْخِ. فَإِنَّهُ يَشْتَرِطُ فِي وَصْفِ الْمَوْضُوعِ أَنْ يَكُونَ ثَابِتًا لِلْمَوْضُوعِ بِالْفِعْلِ. فَعَلَى هَذَا؛ يَكُونُ مَفْهُوْمُ كُلُّ ج ب بِالْإِمْكَانِ: «أَنَّ كُلَّ مَا هُوَ ج بِالْفِعْلِ ب بِالْإِمْكَانِ». وَمِثَالُهُ: كُلُّ حِمَارٍ مَرْكُوبُ زَيْدٍ بِالْإِمْكَانِ؛ فَعَكْسُهَا: بَعْضُ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ حِمَارٌ بِالْإِمْكَانِ، وَلَا يَصْدُقُ إِنْ لَمْ يَرْكَبْ زَيْدٌ حِمَارًا بِالْفِعْلِ.
Chapter on Straight Inversion. And there is no inversion for the two possible propositions, general and specific, according to the doctrine of the Sheikh. For he requires in the description of the subject that it be actually true of the subject. Accordingly, the concept of 'Every J is B possibly' is: 'Everything that is J actually is B possibly.' And its example: Every donkey is ridden by Zayd possibly; its inverse: Some thing ridden by Zayd is a donkey possibly, but it is not true if Zayd did not actually ride a donkey.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage defines the rule of inversion for modal propositions, specifically the two possible propositions (general and specific possibility) according to the doctrine of Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh). It states that on his view, these propositions do not invert. The reason is that Ibn Sina requires that the description of the subject (i.e., the quality of being the subject) be actually true of the subject in reality, not just potentially. For example, in the proposition 'Every donkey is ridden by Zayd possibly', the subject 'donkey' must actually exist and be a donkey in actuality. The proposition is interpreted as: Everything that is actually a donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd. The inverse would be 'Some thing ridden by Zayd is a donkey possibly', but if Zayd never actually rides a donkey, then the inverse is false because there is no actual instance of 'ridden by Zayd' that is a donkey. Thus the inversion fails. This contrasts with al-Farabi's view, who allows the subject's description to be merely possible, in which case the inversion would hold. The passage thus clarifies a specific condition that affects the validity of immediate inference in modal logic.

Key Terms
الممكنتان
الشيخ
ثابت للموضوع بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the predicate 'ridden by Zayd' is not actual.
C) Because Ibn Sina requires the subject's description to be actual.
D) Because possibility does not allow inversion.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's actuality condition ensures that inversion of possible propositions only holds when the subject has real instances.

Understanding the Compound Modality 'Customary Not Always in Some'
Definition of the compound modality and its place in the system of modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two component modalities that make up the compound 'customary not always in some' and explain how the total number of modal propositions becomes twenty-one.

Core Idea
A compound modality combines two simpler modalities: customary and absolute, but restricted.

The commentator introduces a new modality not previously listed among the modal propositions. By analyzing its definition, we can see how it fits into the complete system of twenty-one modal propositions. This lesson will guide you through the components of this compound modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the new modality
Find the phrase 'عرفية لا دائمة في البعض' in the Arabic text. Note that the commentator says it was not mentioned earlier among the modal propositions.
This modality is a later addition to the classification.
2
Break down the definition
The commentator defines it as a judgement of permanence as long as the subject's description holds, with the restriction that this permanence is not essential in some cases. This implies it combines two aspects: a universal customary (permanent as long as description) and a particular absolute (not always essentially).
Think of it as 'always (customarily) but not always (essentially) for some'.
3
Understand the 'not always in some' part
The phrase 'لا دائمة في البعض' is explained as a particular absolute universal. This means that in some instances, the permanence is not essential, i.e., it is only customary.
This is the particular absolute component (جزئية مطلقة عامة).
4
Confirm the total count
The commentator concludes that with this addition, the total number of modal propositions becomes twenty-one. This shows how systematic the classification is.
Count the previously known modalities (twenty) plus this new one makes twenty-one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: عرفية لا دائمة في البعض هذه الجهة لم تتقدم في الموجهات، فحصل مما تقدم في الموجهات، وفي التناقض، ومما هنا أن الموجَّهات إحدى وعشرون موجَّهة. وقوله: عرفية لا دائمة في البعض هي ما حكم فيها بدوام النسبة ما دام وصف الموضوع، وقيد ذلك بعدم الدوام الذاتي في البعض. قوله: لا دائمة في البعض أي: جزئية مطلقة عامة. الموضوع والمحمول، ثم إن أريد بإثبات الشيء لنفسه وسلبه عنه أن الشيء بعد اعتبار ثبوته تثبت له نفسه أو تسلب عنه كما في سائر الصفات، فبطلانه ظاهر، وإن أريد به إثباته في نفسه وسلبه كذلك؛ صح ذلك، فإن الشيء إذا كان معدوماً؛ يصدق سلبُهُ عن نفسه، بمعنى أنه مرتفع بالمرة وليس في نفسه ثابتا، فاندفع ما قيل: كيف يصدق سلب الشيء عن نفسه مع أن السلب نسبة لا بد له من أمرين؟ ا.هـ. قال العصام: وما يجاب به عنه من أن معنى سلب الشيء عن نفسه: سلب الشيء عن أفراد نفسه قاصر؛ لأنه لا ينفع في قولنا: الجزئي ليس بجزئي، فإن فيه سلب الشيء عن نفسه لا بمعنى سلب الشيء عن أفراد نفسه. ا.هـ. وردة عبد الحكيم بأنه ليس من قبيل سلب الشيء عن نفسه، فإن معناه الجزئي ليس موصوفاً بالجزئية ا.هـ.
His statement: 'Customary not always in some' – this modality did not precede among the modal propositions. So from what preceded among the modal propositions, and in contradiction, and from what is here, it results that the modal propositions are twenty-one in number. And his statement: 'Customary not always in some' is what is judged in it with the permanence of the relation as long as the description of the subject [holds], and this is restricted by the lack of essential permanence in some. His statement: 'Not always in some' means a particular absolute universal. The subject and predicate – then if what is meant by affirming a thing of itself and negating it of itself is that the thing after consideration of its existence is affirmed of itself or negated of itself as in other attributes, then its falsity is apparent. But if what is meant is the affirmation of it in itself and its negation in itself likewise, then it is correct. For if the thing is non-existent, its negation of itself is truthful, meaning that it is absolutely non-existent and not fixed in itself. So what was said [as an objection] is rebutted: How can the negation of a thing of itself be truthful while negation is a relation that must have two terms? End. Al-'Isam said: What is answered with regarding it – that the meaning of negating a thing of itself is negating the thing of its individuals – is insufficient, because it does not benefit in our statement: the particular is not a particular, for it contains negation of a thing of itself not in the sense of negating the thing of its individuals. End. And 'Abd al-Hakim rejected it as not being of the type of negation of a thing of itself, for its meaning is: the particular is not described by particularity.
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage comes from the commentary on the conversion chapter in logic. The commentator first identifies a specific modality: 'customary not always in some' (عرفية لا دائمة في البعض). He explains that this modality was not mentioned earlier in the list of modal propositions, but now that we have it, the total count of modal propositions becomes twenty-one. He then defines it: it is a proposition that judges the relation as permanent as long as the description of the subject holds, but with the restriction that this permanence is not essential in some cases. In other words, it is a compound modality. The phrase 'not always in some' is explained as a particular absolute universal (جزئية مطلقة عامة). The commentator then addresses a subtle logical objection: how can it be meaningful to say 'a thing is negated of itself'? He clarifies that if we mean affirming a thing of itself after considering its existence, that is false because it would be a tautology. But if we mean affirming or negating it in itself (i.e., considering it as a concept without assuming existence), then it is correct. For example, a non-existent thing can truthfully be negated of itself, meaning it is not existent. This rebuts the objection that negation requires two distinct terms. Al-'Isam offers a further critique: the standard answer that 'negation of a thing of itself' means 'negation of the thing of its individuals' fails in cases like 'the particular is not a particular', which is not about individuals. 'Abd al-Hakim then responds that this case is not really a self-negation; it means 'the particular is not described by particularity'. Thus the passage clarifies both the compound modality and the logical nuances of self-negation.

Key Terms
عرفية لا دائمة في البعض
جزئية مطلقة عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A particular existential proposition
B) A particular absolute universal proposition
C) A universal conditional proposition
D) A particular customary proposition

Key Takeaway
The compound modality 'customary not always in some' is formed by combining a universal customary proposition with a particular absolute proposition, bringing the total modal propositions to twenty-one.

Resolving the Objection to Self-Negation
Commentary addresses and defends the logical possibility of self-negation, with later refinements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the logical problem of 'salb al-shay' 'an nafsih' and evaluate the responses of al-'Isam and 'Abd al-Hakim.

Core Idea
Self-negation is valid only when the subject is considered as non-existent; otherwise it is a tautology.

The commentator addresses a subtle objection: how can it be meaningful to negate a thing of itself? He distinguishes two interpretations and shows that self-negation is valid in a specific sense. Later commentators refine this further.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the question: 'How can the negation of a thing of itself be truthful while negation is a relation that must have two terms?' The objector argues that negation requires two distinct entities.
This is a classic logical puzzle.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
The commentator says: if 'negation of a thing of itself' means negating it after considering its existence, then it is false. But if it means negating it in itself (without assuming existence), then it is correct, because a non-existent thing can be negated of itself.
First interpretation leads to tautology/incoherence; second is meaningful.
3
Understand al-'Isam's critique
Al-'Isam objects that the standard answer (negation of a thing of its individuals) fails for 'the particular is not a particular', which is not about individuals.
This critique targets the adequacy of the common response.
4
See how 'Abd al-Hakim resolves it
'Abd al-Hakim says that 'the particular is not a particular' is not a case of self-negation; it means 'the particular is not described by particularity', which is a different logical form.
This reframes the statement to avoid the self-negation issue.

The Definition of Qiyas and the Condition of Necessity
Definition of qiyas and condition 'li-dhatih'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of qiyas and explain why the condition 'li-dhatih' excludes induction and analogy.

Core Idea
Qiyas is necessary inference from premises themselves, without foreign premises.

In this lesson, we examine the precise definition of qiyas (syllogism) as a compound statement from which another statement follows necessarily by itself. The key condition 'li-dhatih' ensures that no external premise is needed. We'll see how this distinguishes genuine syllogism from inductive and analogical reasoning, which only yield probable conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Identify the definition of qiyas: a compound statement from which another statement follows necessarily by itself. Underline the phrase 'li-dhatih' (for itself).
2
Analyze the condition 'li-dhatih'
Understand that this condition means the conclusion must follow from the premises without any foreign premise (ajnabiyya). Foreign premises can be non-necessary for the premises or necessary but in the strength of the mentioned ones.
The passage discusses two types of foreign premises: non-necessary (like equality syllogism) and necessary (like 'part of substance' example).
3
Test with the inductive example
Consider the inductive example: 'Horse moves lower jaw; donkey moves lower jaw; etc.' This does not produce the universal conclusion 'Every animal moves lower jaw' because the crocodile is a possible exception. The conclusion is not necessary, so it fails the condition.
The crocodile counterexample shows why induction is not qiyas.
4
Test with the analogical example
Consider the analogical example: 'Nabidh is like khamr due to intoxication; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' The conclusion does not follow necessarily because prohibition may be due to another cause. This fails the condition.
The possibility of another cause means the conclusion is not certain.
