⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : «لذاته» يُخرجُ الدسوقي وقولُهُ: الَّذي هو إجراء... إلخ، فيه تسامح؛ لأنَّ هذا الحكم مطلوبٌ مِنَ الاستقراء لا نفْسه، فكأنَّهم أرادوا أنَّ إثبات المطلوب بالاستقراء هو حكم على كلّي. والصَّحيحُ : أنَّه تصفُحُ أمور جزئية ليحكم بحكمها على أمرٍ يشتمل على تلك الجزئيات، كذا ذكر بعض الفضلاء، ويؤيِّدُهُ ما سيأتي في المتن. مثال قياس الاستقراء كما إذا قُلْتَ: الفرسُ حيوان يحرّكُ فَكَهُ الأسفل عند المضغ، والحمار حيوان يحرّك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ ... وهَلُمَّ جرًا، فَإِنَّ هذا القياس مؤلَّفٌ من قولين، ولكن لا يلزم منه قول آخرُ، وهو أنَّ كلَّ حيوان يحرّك فكَّهُ الأسفل عندَ المضغ؛ لإمكانِ التَّخلُفِ عقلاً، وقد حُكي لنا أنَّ التمساح إنَّما يحرّكُ فكَّهُ الأعلى. ومثال قياس التمثيل؛ أي: التشبيه: النَّبيذُ كَالخمر بجامع الإسكار، فهذا قول مؤلّفٌ مِن قولين؛ لأنَّ قولَه: بجامع الإسكار، خبر مبتدأ محذوف؛ أي: وهو متلبس بجامع الإسكار، ولكن لا يلزمُ منه أنَّ النَّبيذَ حرام عقلاً؛ لإمكان أنْ تكون الحرمة لعلَّةٍ أخرى غيرِ هذه، أمَّا إذا لم نَقُلْ: (إنَّ) بجامع الإسكار خبراً لمحذوف؛ فإنَّهُ يكون خارجاً بقولِهِ: مؤلَّفٌ مِن قَولَيْن، وحينئذ؛ فَلَا يُسمى الاستقراء والتمثيل قياساً؛ = العطار الآخَرُ معَ لزوم علمه للعلم بشيءٍ آخَرَ؛ لأنَّ المعلوم قد يتخلَّفُ عَنِ العلم، وأجابَ عنه السَّيِّدُ بأنَّ المرادَ مِنَ اللَّزوم في تعريفِ الدَّليل؛ المناسبة المصححة للانتقال. (قَوْلُهُ: وَقَوْلُهُ: لِذَاتِهِ يُخْرِجُ ... إِلَخ) إذ المعنى أنْ يكونَ اللَّازِمُ لِذاتِ القولِ المؤلَّفِ؛ أي: لا يكونُ بواسطة مقدّمةٍ غريبةٍ؛ إمَّا غيرِ لازمة لإحدى المقدمتين؛ وَهِيَ الأجنبية، أو لازمة لإحداهما؛ وهي في قوَّةِ المذكورة، والأوَّلُ كَمَا في قياس المساواة، والثاني كَقولنا: جزء الجوهر يوجب ارتفاعُه ارتفاع الجوهر، وكلَّ مَا ليس بجوهرٍ لا يُوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهر، فإنَّهُ يلزمُ منهُما أنَّ جزء الجوهر (1) (قَوْلُهُ: أَمَّا إذا . إلخ) هو كالاستقراء خارج بالقيد الأوّل على كل حال، إذ المراد بالتأليف إحداث صورة تنشأ من تكرار الحدّ الوسط على وجه مخصوص، وهذا المعنى مفقود في قياس الاستقراء والتمثيل . . . هـ . الشَّرنوبي.
And his statement: 'by itself' excludes, al-Dasuqi said: And his statement: 'that which is conducting... etc.,' there is leniency in it, because this ruling is sought from induction, not from itself. So they intended that proving the desired by induction is a ruling on a universal. And the correct is: it is a survey of particular things to judge by their ruling on a matter that encompasses those particulars, as mentioned by some virtuous ones, and it is supported by what will come in the text. An example of the inductive syllogism is when you say: The horse is an animal that moves its lower jaw when chewing, and the donkey is an animal that moves its lower jaw when chewing... and so forth. So this syllogism is composed of two statements, but no other statement follows from it, which is that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because of the possibility of exception intellectually, and it has been narrated to us that the crocodile only moves its upper jaw. And an example of the analogical syllogism, i.e., comparison: Wine is like khamr due to the commonality of intoxication. So this is a statement composed of two statements, because his statement: 'due to the commonality of intoxication' is the predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., and it is characterized by the commonality of intoxication, but it does not necessarily follow from it that wine is forbidden intellectually, because it is possible that the prohibition is due to another cause besides this. As for if we do not say: 'indeed' due to the commonality of intoxication as a predicate of a deleted subject, then it becomes external to his statement: composed of two statements, and then induction and analogy are not called syllogism. = al-Attar: the other with the necessity of its knowledge for knowledge of something else, because the known may be separate from knowledge, and al-Sayyid answered this by saying that what is meant by necessity in the definition of evidence is the suitability that corrects the transition. (His statement: And his statement: by itself excludes... etc.) So the meaning is that the necessary is by itself of the compound statement, i.e., not by the intermediary of a foreign premise, either non-necessary for one of the two premises, and it is the foreign one, or necessary for one of them, and it is in the strength of the mentioned one. The first is as in the syllogism of equality, and the second is like our statement: The part of the substance, its destruction necessitates the destruction of the substance, and everything that is not a substance, its destruction does not necessitate the destruction of the substance, then from them it follows that the part of the substance (1) (His statement: As for if... etc.) It is like induction excluded by the first condition in any case, because what is meant by composition is the generation of a form arising from repeating the middle term in a specific manner, and this meaning is absent in the inductive and analogical syllogism. h. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical commentary on the nature of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by discussing the condition 'lizatihi' (for itself) in the definition of qiyas, which requires that the conclusion follow necessarily from the premises themselves, without external assistance. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that this condition excludes inductive (istiqra') and analogical (tamthil) reasoning from being considered absolute qiyas. The correct definition of induction is given: a survey of particular instances to derive a ruling covering them. An example illustrates induction: from horses and donkeys moving their lower jaws, one concludes that all animals do so, but the crocodile's exception shows the conclusion is not necessary. Similarly, analogy (tamthil) is explained with the example of comparing nabidh (date wine) to khamr (grape wine) due to the common cause of intoxication, but the prohibition of nabidh does not necessarily follow because another cause may be involved. The passage further explains that if the commonality is not explicitly stated as a predicate, the argument is not even a two-statement composition. The commentary by al-Attar then addresses a subtlety about necessity in definitions, citing al-Sayyid's response that necessity means appropriate transition. Finally, al-Sharnubi's footnote reiterates that induction and analogy are excluded from the definition of qiyas because they lack the repetition of a middle term in a specific manner that produces a form.

لذاته
مقدمة غريبة

Checkpoint
Why does the condition 'li-dhatih' exclude inductive reasoning (istiqra')?
A) Induction does not have two premises.
B) Induction uses a foreign premise.
C) Induction's conclusion does not follow necessarily; a counterexample is possible.
D) Induction is not a compound statement.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is defined by its necessary entailment from premises alone, a condition that induction and analogy cannot meet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism on a theological topic (e.g., proving the existence of God) and label the condition 'li-dhatih'. Explain why your syllogism satisfies this condition.
?
Analyze the following argument: 'All intoxicants are forbidden. This drink is intoxicating. Therefore it is forbidden.' Is this a genuine qiyas? Why or why not?

Why Istiqra' and Tamthil Are Not Absolute Qiyas
Exclusion of istiqra' and tamthil from qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) are excluded from absolute qiyas and provide Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Induction and analogy yield probable, not necessary, conclusions.

We have seen that qiyas requires necessary entailment. Now we delve deeper into why induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) fail this condition. Using the examples of the crocodile for induction and nabidh for analogy, the passage demonstrates that their conclusions are not certain. Understanding this distinction is crucial for using logical tools properly in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of istiqra'
Induction is a survey of particular instances to derive a universal ruling. The passage defines it as 'a survey of particular things to judge by their ruling on a matter that encompasses those particulars'.
2
Examine the inductive example
From observing horses and donkeys moving lower jaws, one might conclude all animals do. But the crocodile, which moves its upper jaw, is a counterexample. The conclusion is not necessary—it is only probable.
The logical failure: the universal does not follow necessarily from the particulars.
3
Recall the definition of tamthil
Analogy is transferring a ruling from one thing to another due to a shared attribute. The example: nabidh is like khamr through intoxication.
4
Examine the analogical example
Even though nabidh shares intoxication with khamr, the prohibition of khamr may be due to another cause (e.g., being made from grapes). So the conclusion that nabidh is forbidden does not follow necessarily.
The logical failure: the shared attribute may not be the effective cause ('illah).
5
Understand the role of the middle term
Al-Sharnubi notes that qiyas requires a repetition of the middle term in a specific manner to generate a form; this is absent in istiqra' and tamthil, which lack the necessary structure.
This structural deficiency further disqualifies them from being absolute qiyas.

(قَوْلُهُ: وَقَوْلُهُ: لِذَاتِهِ يُخْرِجُ ... إِلَخ) إذ المعنى أنْ يكونَ اللَّازِمُ لِذاتِ القولِ المؤلَّفِ؛ أي: لا يكونُ بواسطة مقدّمةٍ غريبةٍ؛ إمَّا غيرِ لازمة لإحدى المقدمتين؛ وَهِيَ الأجنبية، أو لازمة لإحداهما؛ وهي في قوَّةِ المذكورة، والأوَّلُ كَمَا في قياس المساواة، والثاني كَقولنا: جزء الجوهر يوجب ارتفاعُه ارتفاع الجوهر، وكلَّ مَا ليس بجوهرٍ لا يُوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهر، فإنَّهُ يلزمُ منهُما أنَّ جزء الجوهر (1) (قَوْلُهُ: أَمَّا إذا . إلخ) هو كالاستقراء خارج بالقيد الأوّل على كل حال، إذ المراد بالتأليف إحداث صورة تنشأ من تكرار الحدّ الوسط على وجه مخصوص، وهذا المعنى مفقود في قياس الاستقراء والتمثيل . . . هـ . الشَّرنوبي.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical commentary on the nature of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by discussing the condition 'lizatihi' (for itself) in the definition of qiyas, which requires that the conclusion follow necessarily from the premises themselves, without external assistance. The commentator al-Dasuqi clarifies that this condition excludes inductive (istiqra') and analogical (tamthil) reasoning from being considered absolute qiyas. The correct definition of induction is given: a survey of particular instances to derive a ruling covering them. An example illustrates induction: from horses and donkeys moving their lower jaws, one concludes that all animals do so, but the crocodile's exception shows the conclusion is not necessary. Similarly, analogy (tamthil) is explained with the example of comparing nabidh (date wine) to khamr (grape wine) due to the common cause of intoxication, but the prohibition of nabidh does not necessarily follow because another cause may be involved. The passage further explains that if the commonality is not explicitly stated as a predicate, the argument is not even a two-statement composition. The commentary by al-Attar then addresses a subtlety about necessity in definitions, citing al-Sayyid's response that necessity means appropriate transition. Finally, al-Sharnubi's footnote reiterates that induction and analogy are excluded from the definition of qiyas because they lack the repetition of a middle term in a specific manner that produces a form.

Refining definitions in logic: the case of independent propositions
Author refines the definition of qadaya to exclude compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the logical strength of a definition refinement in a commentary chain, identifying when a condition is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Adding conditions to a definition must be self-evident; otherwise, the definition remains vulnerable.

In this passage, al-Attar and al-Dasuqi critique an attempt to save the definition of 'proposition' by adding the condition 'independent expression'. You'll see how commentators engage in refining definitions and how one must assess whether the refinement is logically sound. This analysis sharpens your ability to evaluate definitional debates in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition and the objection
The original definition of 'propositions' (al-qadāyā) seemed to include compound propositions like 'non-permanent'. This was an objection: the definition was not restrictive (māni').
The base definition is not explicitly quoted but is implied.
2
Understand the proposed refinement
The answer proposed that 'propositions' means 'independent propositions' (al-qadāyā al-mustaqilla), where each judgment is expressed separately. Thus, compound propositions are excluded because they use non-independent expressions like 'non-permanent'.
3
Analyze the critique of the refinement
Al-Attar notes the refinement is weak because the condition 'independent expression' is not self-evident (ghayr mutabādir). Rajab Effendi adds that if taken strictly, it would exclude valid concepts from being propositions.
The critique questions both the clarity and the logical consequences of the refinement.
4
Assess the meta-critique
Al-Attar then defends the original definition against Rajab's critique, arguing that there is no necessary implication between the syllogism's premises and compound propositions. The refinement was only to repel the objection, not to affect compound propositions in general.
This shows the subtlety of definitional debates: a refinement can be limited in scope.
5
Summarize the logical outcome
The refinement is rejected as weak, and the original definition stands despite the objection. The passage illustrates how commentators refine and defend classical definitions.

اللَّهُمَّ إلَّا أن يقال : المراد بالقضايا هو القضايا المستقلةُ الَّتي عُبّر فيها عن الحكم الإيجابي والسَّلبيّ بعبارة مستقلة، والقضيَّةُ المركبة ليست كذلك ؛ إذ لم يُعبّر فيها عن الحكم الإيجابي والسلبي بعبارة مستقلة، بل عُبّرَ باللادوام ،واللاضرورة فعلى هذا يكونُ التّعريفُ مانعاً .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : الْقَضَايَا الْمُسْتَقِلَّةُ) أي: الَّتى ليس بعضُها تابعاً لبعض، وقولُهُ: (الَّتي عبّر فيها عن الحكم ... إلخ)؛ أي: عن كلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الحُكمين بعبارة مستقلة. (قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ عُبْرَ بِاللَّا دَوَامِ وَاللَّا ضَرَوْرَةِ) أي: بلْ عُبّر عن أحدِهِمَا باللادوامِ أو اللأضرورةِ، وهو ليس عبارةً مُستقلّة، بل جزء من العبارة المستقلة، وهي عبارةٌ عن القضيَّة المركبة، فتأمل.
العطار
بِاللَّادَوَامِ
(قَوْلُهُ : اَللَّهُمَّ إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ ... إلخ) هذا الجواب بمعنى مَا أَجابَ بِهِ عبد الحكيمِ سابقاً ، فَعَنْوَنتهُ بِمَا يُشعِرُ بِضعفِهِ من أجلِ أَنَّهُ غيرُ مانعِ مِن ورودِ الاعتراض بحسب الظَّاهرِ ، والقيدُ المذكورُ - أعني قوله : (بعبارةٍ مُستقلَّةٍ) - غيرُ مُتبادرٍ، فَلَا يُعوَّلُ على إرادتِهِ في مقام التّعريفات، وقولُ رجب أفندي : وَلَا يذهب عليكَ أنَّ هذا الجواب في غايةِ الضَّعْفِ، إذ لو كان المرادُ بِهَا القضايا المستقلَّةَ؛ لَخَرَجَ مَفهومُ اللادوامِ واللاضرورةِ عَن كونه قضيَّةٌ، وذلكَ بَيِّنُ البطلانِ، فَلِأَجْلِ هذا ؛ أورده بصيغة التّمريضِ .ا.هـ. في غايةِ الضَّعْفِ ؛ لأنَّ الملازمة في قوله : لو كان المراد بها القضايا المستقلة؛ لخرجَ مَفهوم ... إلخ ، إذْ لَا تلازم بينَ مقدِّماتِ القياس والقضايا المركَّبةِ حتَّى يلزمَ من إرادة معنى في إحداهما إرادته في الأخرى، إذ تلك الإرادة لتصحيحِ التَّعريف حتّى يندفع عنه النقضُ، وليست تلك الإرادة محتاجاً إليها في المركَّبَاتِ حَتَّى يلزمَ مَا ذكر . (قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ عُبّرَ بِالْلًا دَوَام ... إِلَخ) يعني : عُبّرَ عَنِ الحكمِ الإيجابي والسَّلْبيّ بهما ، وَهُمَا لَيْسَا بِعبارة مُستَقلَّةٍ ؛ لأنَّ اللَّأدوام ليس مدلولُهُ الصَّرِيحُ مُطلقةً عامة، وَلَا الأضرورة مدلولهُ الصَّريح ممكنة عامَّةٌ، بَلْ مَفهُومَاهُمَا يَستلزمانِ هَاتَيْنِ المقدمتين .
Except if it is said: The intended meaning of 'propositions' is the independent propositions in which the affirmative and negative judgments are expressed in an independent expression, and the compound proposition is not like that; because in it the affirmative and negative judgments are not expressed in an independent expression, but rather are expressed by 'non-permanent' and 'non-necessary', so on this basis the definition is restrictive.
Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'the independent propositions' means those whose parts are not subordinate to one another. His statement 'in which the judgment is expressed...' means: about each of the two judgments in an independent expression. His statement 'rather expressed by non-permanence and non-necessity' means: rather one of them is expressed by non-permanence or non-necessity, and it is not an independent expression, but rather a part of the independent expression, and it is an expression for the compound proposition, so reflect.
Al-Attar: 'By non-permanence'... His statement 'Except if it is said...' This answer is in the sense of what 'Abd al-Hakim answered previously, so he titled it with what suggests its weakness because it is not preventive of the objection in appearance, and the mentioned condition - I mean his statement 'by an independent expression' - is not readily understood, so it is not relied upon for its intent in the context of definitions. And the statement of Rajab Effendi: 'And let it not escape you that this answer is extremely weak, because if the intended meaning by it is the independent propositions, then the concept of non-permanence and non-necessity would exit from being a proposition, and that is clearly false.' So because of this, he presented it in the form of suggestion. End quote. In extreme weakness because of the implication in his statement: 'if the intended meaning is the independent propositions, then the concept... would exit' etc., since there is no implication between the premises of the syllogism and the compound propositions such that intending a meaning in one necessitates intending it in the other, because that intention is to correct the definition so that the objection is repelled, and that intention is not needed in the compounds such that what he mentioned would follow. His statement 'Rather expressed by non-permanence...' means: the affirmative and negative judgments are expressed by them, and they are not an independent expression; because 'non-permanence' does not have as its explicit meaning absolute universal, and 'non-necessity' does not have as its explicit meaning possible universal; rather their concepts imply these two premises.
Source pages p. 774

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar on a previous objection and answer concerning the definition of 'propositions' (al-qadāyā) in logic. The initial problem was that the definition of qadāyā as 'propositions' seemed to include compound propositions like 'the non-permanent' or 'the non-necessary', which are not simple independent propositions. A proposed answer was that by 'propositions' they meant 'independent propositions' (al-qadāyā al-mustaqilla) in which each judgment (affirmative or negative) is expressed in its own separate expression. The compound proposition does not do that; instead it expresses one of the judgments via a negated term like 'non-permanent', which is not an independent expression. Thus the definition becomes restrictive (māni').

Al-Dasuqi first clarifies the technical terms: 'independent propositions' are those whose parts are not subordinate to each other; 'expressed in an independent expression' means each of the two judgments (affirmative and negative) has its own separate statement. He then explains that 'non-permanence' or 'non-necessity' are not independent expressions because they are parts of the compound proposition.

Al-Attar then critiques this answer, noting that it is weak and was presented by its author (al-Sayyid 'Abd al-Hakim?) in a tentative form. He quotes Rajab Effendi who argues that if we restrict 'propositions' to independent ones, then the concepts 'non-permanent' and 'non-necessary' would cease to be propositions, which is absurd. But al-Attar disagrees with Rajab's reasoning: there is no necessary link between the premises of the syllogism and compound propositions; the intention to restrict the definition is only to repel the objection against the definition of qadāyā, not to affect the status of compound propositions elsewhere. Finally, al-Attar explains that 'non-permanence' and 'non-necessity' are not independent expressions because their explicit meanings are not 'absolute universal' and 'possible universal' respectively; rather those meanings are implied. This preserves the integrity of the original definition while showing the artificiality of the 'independent expression' condition.

All examples are taken from the logical definitions themselves, not from external sciences.

Key Terms
القضايا المستقلة
اللادوام
اللاضرورة
عبارة مستقلة
قضية مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the answer that 'propositions' means 'independent propositions' considered weak?
A) Because then the definition would no longer include any modal propositions.
B) Because the condition of 'independent expression' is not self-evident and the objection about non-permanence and non-necessity would still apply.
C) Because it contradicts the definitions given by earlier logicians.
D) Because it would make the definition too broad.

Key Takeaway
A definition refinement must be based on a self-evident condition; otherwise, it fails to protect the definition from objections and may itself become vulnerable to counter-critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition in a mantiq or usul text that was refined by adding a condition (e.g., 'qiyas' requiring 'jami' al-wasf'). Using the criteria from this lesson, evaluate whether the condition is self-evident and whether the refinement is logically effective.
?
In a commentary chain, identify where a commentator (sharih) adds a condition to a base definition. Assess whether the condition is 'mutabādir' (readily understood) and whether it makes the definition restrictive or descriptive.

Defining Induction: Beyond the Act of Examination
Definition of induction and its refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definitional error in equating induction with the act of examining particulars and propose a corrected definition.

Core Idea
Induction is a composite proof, not a simple act of looking.

This passage presents the standard definition of induction as 'examination of particulars to establish a universal judgment.' But commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar detect a looseness: induction is not the examination itself but a logical argument composed of premises that include examination. This lesson unpacks why that distinction matters and how to define induction accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definition
Locate the definition offered at the start: 'al-istiqra' huwa: tasaffuh al-juz'iyyat li ithbat hukm kulli.' This is the common introductory definition.
2
Spot al-Dasuqi's critique
He says there is tasamuh (looseness) in defining it as examination or as judgment on the universal. Why? Because induction is a proof (hujja), a composite of known things leading to assent.
The critique targets defining induction by its cause rather than its essence.
3
Analyze al-Attar's refinement
Al-Attar says defining by 'examination' is a definition by the cause (sabab), not the essence. Induction is a type of evidence (dalil), composed of premises. So the better definition: 'the composite of propositions that include judgment on particulars to establish judgment on the universal.'
This refinement shifts focus from the process to the logical structure.
4
Apply the refinement to an example
The example of animals: if we examine animals and conclude all move lower jaw, we should say 'most' because induction is typically incomplete. This shows the importance of precise wording reflecting the inductive strength.
Incomplete induction yields probable, not certain, conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا الاستقراء فهو: تَصَفُحُ الجُزْئِيَّاتِ؛ لِإِثْبَاتِ حُكْم كُلِّيٍّ. قَالَ الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ: تَصَفُحُ ... إلخ) فيهِ تسامح، كما أنَّ تفسيره بالحكم على الأمرِ الكُلِّيّ كذلك أيضاً؛ لأنَّ الاستقراءَ حجّة؛ أي: أمورٌ معلومةٌ مُوصلةٌ إلى التّصديقِ بالتصفح ليس تسامحاً، فتأمل. ياسين. وَقَالَ العطار: فِي تفسيرِهِ بِالتَّصَفُحِ أَيْضًا مسامحة؛ لأنَّه تعريف بالسبب، والاستقراء قسم مِنَ الدليل، فيكون مُركَّباً مِن مقدِّماتٍ تَشتمل على التَّصفُحِ؛ أي: التَّتبع لَا نفسِهِ، فَالأَوْلَى أَنْ يُقالَ: هو المؤلَّفُ مِن قضايا تشتمل على الحكم على الجزئيات لإثباتِ الحكم على الكُلِّيّ. وَقَوْلُهُ: كَمَا إِذَا تَصَفَّحْنَا جُزْئِيَّاتِ الْحَيَوَانِ لو قال: أكثر جزئيات الحيوانِ؛ لَكَانَ أَوْلَى.
As for induction, it is: the examination of particulars to establish a universal judgment. Al-Dasuqi said: 'His statement: examination... etc. contains looseness, and similarly his interpretation of it as judgment upon the universal; because induction is a proof, i.e., known things that lead to assent through examination is not looseness, so ponder.' Al-Attar said: 'In interpreting it as examination there is also looseness because it is a definition by the cause, while induction is a type of evidence, thus it is composed of premises that include examination, i.e., investigation, not the examination itself. So it is better to say: it is the composite of propositions that include judgment upon particulars to establish the judgment upon the universal. And his statement: "as when we examine the particulars of animals" – if he said "most particulars of animals" it would be better.'
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage defines induction (istiqra') and immediately subjects that definition to critical scrutiny. The definition offered is 'the examination of particulars to establish a universal judgment.' However, the commentator Al-Dasuqi notes that this definition is loose (tasamuh) because it defines induction in terms of its cause (the act of examination) rather than as a logical proof. He also notes that interpreting the definition as 'judgment upon the universal' is similarly imprecise. Al-Attar elaborates: induction is a type of evidence (dalil), i.e., a composite of premises that include the act of examination; it is not the examination itself. Therefore the proper definition should be 'a composite of propositions that include judgments on particulars in order to establish a judgment on the universal.' Finally, the example given—examining animals and concluding that all move their lower jaw when chewing—should specify 'most particulars' because induction is typically incomplete (istiqra' naqis). The discussion guards against the misunderstanding that induction is a simple observational process; rather, it is a structured argument based on examination.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
التصفح
الجزئيات
الحكم الكلي
تسامح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Attar criticize defining induction as 'examination of particulars'?
A) It is too vague about what particulars are.
B) It defines induction by its cause rather than its essence.
C) It requires the claim of complete enumeration.
D) It leads to certainty.

Key Takeaway
Induction is not simply looking at things; it is a structured argument that uses premises about particulars to support a universal conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Induction is the examination of particulars.' Why would al-Attar say this is a definition by the cause? Write a corrected definition in your own words.
?
Take the claim: 'I examined several animals and they all have a heart, so all animals have a heart.' Is this a valid induction according to the refined definition? What would the commentators say about the wording?

Tamthil: Definition and Certainty in Analogical Reasoning
Author defines tamthil and discusses whether it yields certainty or probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tamthil according to the text and distinguish between certain and probabilistic tamthil using examples from kalam.

Core Idea
Tamthil transfers a ruling via a common cause, but certainty hinges on the precision of that cause.

In this passage, the author defines tamthil (analogical reasoning) as a statement of participation between two particulars. The commentators refine this definition and then split tamthil into two epistemological categories: one yielding certainty and one yielding probability. This distinction is critical for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic — التمثيل (al-tamthīl). The author gives a concise definition: بيان مشاركة جزئي لآخر (a statement of the participation of a particular in another).
2
Unpack the definition through the commentators
Read al-Dusuqi's explanation: tamthil is likening one particular to another in a common meaning to transfer a ruling. Al-'Attar then criticizes the well-known definition and offers a refined definition: a composition of propositions stating participation in the cause of the ruling.
Notice the shift from defining by effect (leniency) to a more precise compositional definition.
3
Examine the kalam example
Analyze the example: 'The sky is created because it is like the house in composition, which is the cause of creation.' Identify the two particulars (sky and house) and the common cause (composition).
4
Distinguish certain vs. probabilistic tamthil
Using Rajab Effendi's division, note that certain tamthil (قطعي) yields certainty when the common cause is precisely defined (e.g., imkan for the world). Probabilistic tamthil (ظني) occurs when the common attribute is less precise.
5
Apply the division to a new example
Consider whether the example 'the sky is like the house in composition' is certain or probabilistic. According to the text, it is given as a general example; Rajab Effendi classifies the 'world like the house in imkan' as certain, implying the composition example may be probabilistic unless the cause is fully specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا التَّمْثِيلُ فَهُوَ: بَيَانُ مُشَارَكَةِ جُزْئِيٍّ لِآخَرَ.
قَالَ الدُّسُوقِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ: فَهُوَ بَيَانُ... إِلَخ. وَحَاصِلُهُ: تَشْبِيهُ جُزْئِيٍّ بِجُزْئِيٍّ فِي مَعْنَى مُشْتَرَكٍ بَيْنَهُمَا؛ لِيَثْبُتَ فِي الْمُشَبَّهِ الْحُكْمُ الثَّابِتُ فِي الْمُشَبَّهِ بِهِ الْمُعَلَّلُ بِذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى الْمُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهُمَا كَقَوْلِنَا: السَّمَاءُ حَادِثَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّهَا كَالْبَيْتِ فِي التَّأْلِيفِ الَّذِي هُوَ عِلَّةُ الْحُدُوثِ.
وَقَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: عَدَلَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَنِ التَّعْرِيفِ الْمَشْهُورِ، وَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ الْحُكْمِ فِي جُزْئِيٍّ؛ لِثُبُوتِهِ فِي جُزْئِيٍّ آخَرَ لِمَعْنَى مُشْتَرَكٍ بَيْنَهُمَا لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الْمُسَامَحَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ تَعْرِيفُ الشَّيْءِ بِأَثَرِهِ الْمُتَرَتِّبِ عَلَيْهِ. قَالَ: وَالظَّاهِرُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: هُوَ الْمُؤَلَّفُ مِنْ قَضَايَا تَشْتَمِلُ عَلَى بَيَانِ مُشَارَكَةِ جُزْئِيٍّ لِجُزْئِيٍّ آخَرَ فِي عِلَّةِ الْحُكْمِ لَهُ، يُثْبِتُ ذَلِكَ الْحُكْمَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْجُزْئِيِّ. قَالَ رَجَبٌ أَفَنْدِيُّ: وَقَدْ قَسَمَ الْقَوْمُ التَّمْثِيلَ إِلَى تَمْثِيلٍ قَطْعِيٍّ: يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ كَقَوْلِنَا: الْعَالَمُ كَالْبَيْتِ فِي الْإِمْكَانِ، وَهُوَ عِلَّةٌ لِلِاحْتِيَاجِ؛ أَيْ الْمُؤَثِّرِ، فَيَكُونُ الْعَالَمُ مُحْتَاجًا إِلَى الْمُؤَثِّرِ أَيْضًا، وَإِلَى غَيْرِ قَطْعِيٍّ: يُفِيدُ الظَّنَّ مِثْلَ: الْعَالَمُ كَالْبَيْتِ.
And as for tamthil, it is: a statement of the participation of a particular in another. Al-Dusuqi said: His statement: 'it is a statement... etc.' Its summary: likening a particular to a particular in a meaning common between them, in order to establish in the compared thing the ruling established in the thing compared to, which is explained by that common meaning between them, like our saying: The sky is created because it is like the house in composition, which is the cause of creation. Al-'Attar said: The author departed from the well-known definition, which is establishing a ruling in a particular due to its establishment in another particular for a meaning common between them, because it involves leniency; since it is a definition of a thing by its effect. He said: The apparent is that one should say: it is a composition of propositions that include a statement of a particular's participation with another particular in the cause of the ruling for it, establishing that ruling in that particular. Rajab Effendi said: The people divided tamthil into: certain tamthil: that yields certainty, like our saying: The world is like the house in possibility, and it is a cause of need; i.e., the effect, so the world also needs the effect; and non-certain tamthil: that yields probability, like: The world is like the house.
Source pages p. 862

Commentary
This passage defines tamthil (analogical reasoning) and discusses its nature and certainty. The author first gives a concise definition: tamthil is a statement of the participation of one particular in another. Al-Dusuqi's commentary explains that this means likening one particular to another in a common meaning, with the aim of transferring a ruling from the original (the thing compared to) to the new case (the thing compared). He provides an example from kalam: the sky is created because it resembles the house in composition, and composition is the cause of creation. This is a classic analogical argument used by theologians to prove that the world is created. Al-'Attar then notes that the author's definition differs from the well-known definition (which defines tamthil as 'establishing a ruling in a particular because it is established in another particular for a common meaning'). The author's definition is preferable because the well-known one defines tamthil by its outcome, which is a leniency. Al-'Attar offers a more precise definition: tamthil is a composition of propositions that includes a statement of a particular's participation with another in the cause of a ruling, leading to the establishment of that ruling in the first particular. Rajab Effendi further divides tamthil into two types: certain tamthil, which yields certainty (yaqin), and non-certain tamthil, which yields probability (zann). The certain example is from kalam: the world is like the house in possibility (imkan), which necessitates a creator (the effect). The non-certain example is simply the world is like the house, without specifying the common attribute precisely, leading to probability. This classification reflects the epistemological status of analogical reasoning in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
تمثيل
تشبيه
جزئي
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of certain tamthil (تمثيل قطعي) according to the passage?
A) The world is like the house in possibility, therefore it needs an effect.
B) The sky is like the house in composition, therefore it is created.
C) Wine is intoxicating, and nabidh resembles it, so nabidh is forbidden.
D) All men are mortal, Socrates is a man, so Socrates is mortal.

Key Takeaway
Tamthil yields certainty only when the common cause (illa) is precisely identified and leads to a necessary connection; otherwise it is probabilistic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the two particulars and the common cause in the example: 'The sky is created because it is like the house in composition.' Then state whether this tamthil is certain or probabilistic according to Rajab Effendi's division. Justify your answer.
?
Construct a tamthil of your own using a fiqh example: say 'Wine is forbidden because of intoxication; nabidh shares intoxication, so nabidh is forbidden.' Explain whether it is certain or probabilistic and why.

The Refined Definition of Tamthil (Analogy)
Critique and replacement of tamthil definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a correct definition of tamthil that avoids defining it by its effect, and distinguish it from the flawed common definition.

Core Idea
Tamthil is a composition of propositions showing participation in a cause, not merely a judgment transferred from one particular to another.

The common definition of tamthil is 'establishing a judgment in a particular because it is established in another particular due to a shared meaning.' The commentator finds this inaccurate because it defines the thing by its effect. The corrected definition focuses on the logical structure: a set of propositions that demonstrate participation in the cause of the judgment. Let's unpack why the old definition falls short and how the new one fixes it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common definition under critique
Find the phrase 'إثبات الحكم في جزئي لثبوته في جزئي آخر لمعنى مشترك بينهما' in the Arabic. This is the well-known definition that the author departed from.
This definition is considered musāmaha (inaccurate) because it defines tamthil by its result.
2
Understand the objection: defining a thing by its effect
The commentator says this definition defines tamthil by its effect (athar). An effect cannot define the thing itself because the effect comes after the thing. Tamthil is the reasoning process, not the resulting judgment.
This is a logical error in definition construction.
3
Extract the corrected definition from the commentary
Look for 'هو المؤلَّفُ مِن قضايا تشتمل على بيان مشاركة جزئي لجزئي آخَرَ في علّة الحكم له، يثبت ذلك الحكم في ذلك الجزئي'. This is the preferred definition: it highlights the composition of propositions and the sharing of a cause.
The phrase 'مشاركة في علّة الحكم' is key: the cause of judgment must be shared.
4
Compare the two definitions
Note that the corrected definition explicitly mentions 'علّة الحكم' (cause of judgment) and the composition of propositions (mu'allaf min qadaya). The old definition omitted the causal link and the propositional structure.
The corrected definition also avoids limiting to a single judgment transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الاسْتِقْرَاءِ وَالتَّمْثِيْلِ. فإذا ردَّ إلى صورة القياس؛ صارَ هكذا: السَّماءُ مؤلَّفٌ، وكلُّ مؤلّف حادث، فتطرُّقُ الخلل إن وجدَ؛ إِنَّما يكونُ في الكُبرى، بخلافِ الاستقراء؛ فإنَّه إِذا رُدَّ إلى القياس؛ فإنَّ تطرق الخلل فيه إنَّما هو بالنسبة إلى صُغراه؛ أعني: هذا الأمر الكُلّي منحصر في تلك الجزئيَّاتِ الَّتي وقع الاستقراء فيها، ويُسمَّى الجزئي الأوَّلُ. (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَمَّا التَّمْثِيلُ ... إِلَخ) عدل المصنِّفُ عن التّعريف المشهورِ، وَهُو إثباتُ الحكم في جزئي؛ لثبوتِهِ في جزئي آخر لمعنى مُشترك بينهما لاشتماله على المسامحة؛ لأنَّهُ تَعريفُ الشَّيء بأثره المترتب عليه، وقال في شرح الرسالة: الأصوبُ أنَّهُ نسبةُ جزئي لجزئي في معنى مُشترك بينهما، ونظر فيه بعضُ الشَّارحين بأن فيه مُسامحة أيضاً. قال والظَّاهِرُ أنْ يُقال: هو المؤلَّفُ مِن قضايا تشتمل على بيان مشاركة جزئي لجزئي آخَرَ في علّة الحكم له، يثبت ذلك الحكم في ذلك الجزئي، قال رجب أفندي: وَقَد قسم القومُ التَّمثيل إلى: تمثيل قطعي: يفيد اليقين كَقولِنَا: العالمُ كَالبيتِ في الإمكان، وهو علَّةٌ للاحتياج؛ أي: المؤثر، فيكونُ العالم محتاجاً إلى المؤثر أيضاً، وإلى غير قطعي: يفيدُ الظَّنَّ مثل: العالمُ كَالبيتِ في التأليف، وهو علَّهُ الحدوثِ، فيكون العالم حادثاً أيضاً، والظَّاهِرُ مِنَ التَّمثيل في مقابلة القياس هو الثَّاني؛ إذ الأوَّلُ يرجعُ إلى القياس قطعاً، فينبغي على هذا أن يُذكر في تعريفه قيد يخرج الأوّل لكون المشاركة المذكورة ظنّية. ا.هـ. والذي في الحواشي السَّيالكوتيَّةِ أنَّ التَّمثيل لا يكونُ مُفيداً لليقين إلا إذا ثبت عليه الجامع، وعدم كون خصوصيَّةِ الأصلِ شرطاً، أو خصوصيَّةِ الفرع مانعاً قطعاً، لكن تحصيل العلم بهذهِ الأمورِ صعب جدًّا؛ فَلِذَا لم يقسمُوا التَّمثيلَ إلى مَا يُفيدُ اليقين، وإلى مَا يُفيدُ الظَّنَّ كَمَا فَسَّمُوا الاستقراء. (قَوْلُهُ : لِجُزْئِيّ آخَرَ ... إِلَخ) ليس المراد بالجزئي؛ الجزئي الإضافي للمعنى المشترك، بل ما يشمله المعنى المشترك؛ سواء كانَ محمولاً عليه أَوْ لَا؛ قالَهُ عبدُ الحكيم. ا. هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فِي عِلَّةِ الْحُكُم والمراد بها العلَّةُ المستلزمةُ لأصل الحكم؛ سواء كانَتْ عِلَّةً تامَّةٌ أو ناقصةٌ لَا مَطلقُ العلَّةِ؛ ضَرورةً أنَّ اشتراك مطلقِ العَلَّةِ لَا تستلزم اشتراك المعلول. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ : النَّبِيْذُ مُشكِرٌ ... إلخ) لو قالَ: النَّبيذُ حرام؛ لأَنَّهُ كَالحَمرِ في الإسكارِ الَّذي هو عِلَّةُ الحُرمةِ؛ لكان أخصرَ وَأَوْلَى، فإذا ردَّ إلى صورة القياس؛ صار هكذا: النَّبيذُ مُسكِرٌ كَالخمر، وكُلُّ مُشكرٍ حرام، فالنبيذُ حرام، فالجزئي الأوَّلُ أصغرُ، والثَّاني شبيه، والحكم أكبرُ، والمعنى المشترك أوسط، والمتكلمونَ يُسمونَ التَّمثيل استدلالاً بالشَّاهِدِ على الغائب والأصغر غائباً، والشَّبية شاهداً، والفقهاء يسمونه قياساً؛ لما فيه من حذوِ جزئي بجزئي وإلحاقه بِهِ، يُقالُ: قاسَ الشَّيء.
A chapter on induction and analogy. When it is reduced to the form of the syllogism, it becomes thus: 'The heaven is composed, and every composed thing is originated.' So if there is a defect, it occurs only in the major premise, unlike induction; for when it is reduced to the syllogism, the defect relates only to its minor premise, i.e., that this universal matter is confined to those particulars in which induction occurred, and it is called the first particular. (His statement: 'As for analogy... etc.') The author departed from the well-known definition, which is establishing a judgment for a particular because it is established for another particular due to a shared meaning, because it contains inaccuracy; for it is defining a thing by its effect. He said in the commentary on the Risala: The more correct is that it is the relation of a particular to a particular in a shared meaning. Some commentators objected that it also contains inaccuracy. He said: The apparent is that it should be said: It is composed of propositions that include the demonstration of the participation of a particular with another particular in the cause of the judgment, in order to establish that judgment in that particular. Rajab Efendi said: The people divided analogy into: conclusive analogy, which yields certainty, like our saying: 'The world is like a house in possibility, which is a cause of need, i.e., the efficient cause, so the world also needs an efficient cause'; and non-conclusive analogy, which yields probability, like: 'The world is like a house in composition, which is the cause of temporality, so the world is also temporal.' The apparent meaning of analogy in contrast to syllogism is the second; for the first reduces to syllogism definitively. Therefore, it is appropriate to mention in its definition a condition that excludes the first, because the mentioned participation is probable. End quote. What is in the Siwalkuti glosses is that analogy is only productive of certainty if the commonality is established, and the absence of the specific characteristic of the original being a condition or the specific characteristic of the branch being a prevention is known with certainty. But acquiring knowledge of these matters is very difficult; therefore, they did not divide analogy into what yields certainty and what yields probability as they divided induction. (His statement: 'For another particular... etc.') What is meant by 'particular' is not the relative particular to the shared meaning, but rather what the shared meaning encompasses, whether it is predicated or not, as said by Abd al-Hakim. End quote. (His statement: 'In the cause of the judgment' and what is meant by it is the cause that necessitates the original judgment, whether it is a complete or incomplete cause, not any cause; necessarily, because sharing in any cause does not necessitate sharing in the effect. (His statement: 'As it is said: Nabidh is intoxicating... etc.') If he had said: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is like wine in intoxication, which is the cause of prohibition,' it would be more concise and better. When reduced to the form of the syllogism, it becomes: 'Nabidh is intoxicating like wine, and every intoxicant is forbidden, so nabidh is forbidden.' So the first particular is the minor, the second is the analogue, the judgment is the major, and the shared meaning is the middle. The mutakallimun call analogy 'inference from the witnessed to the absent,' where the minor is absent and the analogue is witnessed. The jurists call it qiyas because it involves a particular being modeled upon another and annexed to it. One says: 'He measured the thing.'
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition and classification of tamthil (analogy). It begins by contrasting tamthil with istiqra' (induction) in terms of where defects appear when reduced to qiyas (syllogism). Then it critiques the common definition of tamthil as 'establishing a judgment for a particular because it is established for another particular due to a shared meaning,' noting that this defines the thing by its effect. It offers a better definition: 'a composition of propositions that demonstrate the participation of a particular with another particular in the cause of the judgment, in order to establish that judgment in that particular.' This definition avoids the previous inaccuracy. Next, it presents a classification of tamthil into conclusive (قطعي) and non-conclusive (غير قطعي). Conclusive analogy yields certainty, like the argument that the world is like a house in having possibility (imkan), which is the cause of needing an efficient cause. Non-conclusive analogy yields only probability, like the argument that the world is like a house in composition (ta'lif), which is the cause of being originated. The text notes that the first type actually reduces to a regular syllogism with certainty, so it is not truly tamthil in the sense used in logic; tamthil proper is the probable form. The gloss from al-Siyalkuti adds that for tamthil to yield certainty, one must establish the common cause and the absence of any special condition or prevention, but this is very difficult, hence analogy is not divided into certain and probable as induction is. The passage then clarifies what is meant by 'particular' in the definition: it is any thing that falls under the shared meaning, not the relative particular. It also specifies that the 'cause' is the cause that necessitates the original judgment, whether complete or incomplete, not any cause. Finally, it analyzes the example of khamr and nabidh: nabidh is forbidden because it shares the cause of intoxication with wine. When reduced to syllogism, the minor is nabidh, the major is the judgment of prohibition, and the middle is intoxication, which is the shared meaning. The mutakallimun call this 'inference from the witnessed to the absent' (istidlal bi al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib), while the fuqaha call it qiyas because it involves annexing a branch to an original based on a common cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author depart from the common definition of tamthil?
A) Because it is too long
B) Because it defines tamthil by its effect
C) Because it does not mention the minor premise
D) Because it is used by fuqaha but not mutakallimun

Key Takeaway
A correct definition of tamthil avoids defining by effect and centers on the composition of propositions demonstrating participation in a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write your own definition of tamthil in Arabic following the refined model: 'هو المؤلَّفُ مِن قضايا تشتمل على بيان مشاركة جزئي لجزئي آخَرَ في علّة الحكم له، يثبت ذلك الحكم في ذلك الجزئي'. Then test it by identifying the genus and differentia.
?
Find one example from a usul al-fiqh textbook where a definition of qiyas is given. Compare it with the refined tamthil definition. Are they the same or different? Why?

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
7 min
Classification of Tamthil: Conclusive and Probable
Division of tamthil into certain and probable types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conclusive tamthil (yielding certainty) and non-conclusive tamthil (yielding probability), and explain why the former is not true tamthil.

Core Idea
True tamthil yields only probability; conclusive analogy reduces to a regular syllogism.

The passage divides tamthil into two types: conclusive (qat'i) and non-conclusive (ghayr qat'i). But it notes that the conclusive type actually reduces to a standard syllogism and is not genuine tamthil. Let's walk through the two examples given—the world as a house in possibility vs. in composition—to see why one is certain and the other probable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of tamthil
In the Arabic, find the phrase 'تمثيل قطعي' and 'تمثيل غير قطعي'. The first yields yaqin (certainty), the second yields zann (probability).
These are also called conclusive and non-conclusive.
2
Examine the conclusive example: world like house in imkan
The argument: 'العالم كالبيت في الإمكان، وهو علَّة للاحتياج؛ أي: المؤثر، فيكون العالم محتاجاً إلى المؤثر أيضاً.' The shared cause (imkan) necessitates the need for an efficient cause with certainty.
Imkan here means possibility of existence, which necessarily implies a need for a cause.
3
Examine the non-conclusive example: world like house in ta'lif
The argument: 'العالم كالبيت في التأليف، وهو علَّهُ الحدوثِ، فيكون العالم حادثاً أيضاً.' The shared cause (ta'lif/composition) is not necessarily the cause of huduth (origination); composition is a property of originated things but not every composed thing is originated in the same way.
This yields only probability because the causal link is not certain.
4
Understand why conclusive tamthil is not really tamthil
The text says: 'والظَّاهِرُ مِنَ التَّمثيل في مقابلة القياس هو الثَّاني؛ إذ الأوَّلُ يرجعُ إلى القياس قطعاً.' The first type reduces to syllogism definitively, so it is not an analogy in the logical sense (which is contrasted with qiyas).
True tamthil, in the context of logic, is the probable type.
5
Consider the difficulty of achieving certainty in tamthil
From the Siyalkuti gloss: for tamthil to yield certainty, one must establish the common cause and ensure no special condition or prevention exists. This is very hard, so tamthil is not divided into certain and probable like induction.
This explains why tamthil is generally treated as probable.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition and classification of tamthil (analogy). It begins by contrasting tamthil with istiqra' (induction) in terms of where defects appear when reduced to qiyas (syllogism). Then it critiques the common definition of tamthil as 'establishing a judgment for a particular because it is established for another particular due to a shared meaning,' noting that this defines the thing by its effect. It offers a better definition: 'a composition of propositions that demonstrate the participation of a particular with another particular in the cause of the judgment, in order to establish that judgment in that particular.' This definition avoids the previous inaccuracy. Next, it presents a classification of tamthil into conclusive (قطعي) and non-conclusive (غير قطعي). Conclusive analogy yields certainty, like the argument that the world is like a house in having possibility (imkan), which is the cause of needing an efficient cause. Non-conclusive analogy yields only probability, like the argument that the world is like a house in composition (ta'lif), which is the cause of being originated. The text notes that the first type actually reduces to a regular syllogism with certainty, so it is not truly tamthil in the sense used in logic; tamthil proper is the probable form. The gloss from al-Siyalkuti adds that for tamthil to yield certainty, one must establish the common cause and the absence of any special condition or prevention, but this is very difficult, hence analogy is not divided into certain and probable as induction is. The passage then clarifies what is meant by 'particular' in the definition: it is any thing that falls under the shared meaning, not the relative particular. It also specifies that the 'cause' is the cause that necessitates the original judgment, whether complete or incomplete, not any cause. Finally, it analyzes the example of khamr and nabidh: nabidh is forbidden because it shares the cause of intoxication with wine. When reduced to syllogism, the minor is nabidh, the major is the judgment of prohibition, and the middle is intoxication, which is the shared meaning. The mutakallimun call this 'inference from the witnessed to the absent' (istidlal bi al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib), while the fuqaha call it qiyas because it involves annexing a branch to an original based on a common cause.

Key Terms
تمثيل قطعي
تمثيل غير قطعي
اليقين
الظن
الجامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the conclusive tamthil not considered true tamthil?
A) Because it uses an incomplete cause
B) Because it reduces to a syllogism definitively
C) Because it does not have a shared meaning
D) Because it is used only by fuqaha

Key Takeaway
True tamthil is probabilistic; any analogy that yields certainty is actually a standard syllogism in disguise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conclusive tamthil argument using a fiqh example where the 'illa is certain, and a non-conclusive one where the 'illa is probable. Use the pattern: 'X is like Y in Z, which is the cause of judgment J, so X also has J.' Then evaluate which one yields certainty.
?

Reducing Tamthil to Syllogistic Form
Reduction of tamthil to categorical syllogism with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert an analogical argument (tamthil) into a categorical syllogism, identifying the minor term, major term, and middle term.

Core Idea
Every tamthil can be expressed as a categorical syllogism with the shared cause as the middle term.

The passage shows how the tamthil argument about nabidh (date wine) and khamr (grape wine) is reduced to a syllogism. The key is that the shared cause (intoxication) becomes the middle term. Let's walk through the reduction step by step, using the example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Indentify the original tamthil statement
In the Arabic, find the example: 'النَّبِيْذُ مُشكِرٌ كَالخَمر، وكل مُسكر حرام، فالنبيذ حرام.' This is the tamthil argument in reduced form.
The text suggests a more concise phrasing: 'النبيذ حرام لأنه كالخمر في الإسكار'.
2
Extract the three terms: minor, major, middle
From the syllogism: minor (asghar) = النبيذ (nabidh), major (akbar) = حرام (forbidden), middle (awsat) = مسكر (intoxicant). The shared cause (intoxication) becomes the middle term.
The text explicitly says: 'فالجزئي الأوَّلُ أصغرُ، والثَّاني شبيه، والحكم أكبرُ، والمعنى المشترك أوسط.'
3
Write the syllogism in standard categorical form
First premise: كل مسكر حرام (all intoxicants are forbidden). Second premise: النبيذ مسكر (nabidh is intoxicating). Conclusion: النبيذ حرام (nabidh is forbidden). This is a Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
Note that the second premise could also be expressed as 'النبيذ مسكر كالخمر' but 'كالخمر' is extraneous to the syllogistic form.
4
Relate to the mutakallimun and fuqaha terminology
Mutakallimun call this 'inference from the witnessed to the absent' (al-shahid to al-gha'ib): the minor (nabidh) is the absent (gha'ib), the analogue (khamr) is the witnessed (shahid). Fuqaha call it qiyas because it annexes a branch (far') to an original (asl) based on a common cause.
This shows how the same logical structure is labelled differently in different sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الاسْتِقْرَاءِ وَالتَّمْثِيْلِ. فإذا ردَّ إلى صورة القياس؛ صارَ هكذا: السَّماءُ مؤلَّفٌ، وكلُّ مؤلّف حادث، فتطرُّقُ الخلل إن وجدَ؛ إِنَّما يكونُ في الكُبرى، بخلافِ الاستقراء؛ فإنَّه إِذا رُدَّ إلى القياس؛ فإنَّ تطرق الخلل فيه إنَّما هو بالنسبة إلى صُغراه؛ أعني: هذا الأمر الكُلّي منحصر في تلك الجزئيَّاتِ الَّتي وقع الاستقراء فيها، ويُسمَّى الجزئي الأوَّلُ. (قَوْلُهُ : وَأَمَّا التَّمْثِيلُ ... إِلَخ) عدل المصنِّفُ عن التّعريف المشهورِ، وَهُو إثباتُ الحكم في جزئي؛ لثبوتِهِ في جزئي آخر لمعنى مُشترك بينهما لاشتماله على المسامحة؛ لأنَّهُ تَعريفُ الشَّيء بأثره المترتب عليه، وقال في شرح الرسالة: الأصوبُ أنَّهُ نسبةُ جزئي لجزئي في معنى مُشترك بينهما، ونظر فيه بعضُ الشَّارحين بأن فيه مُسامحة أيضاً. قال والظَّاهِرُ أنْ يُقال: هو المؤلَّفُ مِن قضايا تشتمل على بيان مشاركة جزئي لجزئي آخَرَ في علّة الحكم له، يثبت ذلك الحكم في ذلك الجزئي، قال رجب أفندي: وَقَد قسم القومُ التَّمثيل إلى: تمثيل قطعي: يفيد اليقين كَقولِنَا: العالمُ كَالبيتِ في الإمكان، وهو علَّةٌ للاحتياج؛ أي: المؤثر، فيكونُ العالم محتاجاً إلى المؤثر أيضاً، وإلى غير قطعي: يفيدُ الظَّنَّ مثل: العالمُ كَالبيتِ في التأليف، وهو علَّهُ الحدوثِ، فيكون العالم حادثاً أيضاً، والظَّاهِرُ مِنَ التَّمثيل في مقابلة القياس هو الثَّاني؛ إذ الأوَّلُ يرجعُ إلى القياس قطعاً، فينبغي على هذا أن يُذكر في تعريفه قيد يخرج الأوّل لكون المشاركة المذكورة ظنّية. ا.هـ. والذي في الحواشي السَّيالكوتيَّةِ أنَّ التَّمثيل لا يكونُ مُفيداً لليقين إلا إذا ثبت عليه الجامع، وعدم كون خصوصيَّةِ الأصلِ شرطاً، أو خصوصيَّةِ الفرع مانعاً قطعاً، لكن تحصيل العلم بهذهِ الأمورِ صعب جدًّا؛ فَلِذَا لم يقسمُوا التَّمثيلَ إلى مَا يُفيدُ اليقين، وإلى مَا يُفيدُ الظَّنَّ كَمَا فَسَّمُوا الاستقراء. (قَوْلُهُ : لِجُزْئِيّ آخَرَ ... إِلَخ) ليس المراد بالجزئي؛ الجزئي الإضافي للمعنى المشترك، بل ما يشمله المعنى المشترك؛ سواء كانَ محمولاً عليه أَوْ لَا؛ قالَهُ عبدُ الحكيم. ا. هـ. (قَوْلُهُ : فِي عِلَّةِ الْحُكُم والمراد بها العلَّةُ المستلزمةُ لأصل الحكم؛ سواء كانَتْ عِلَّةً تامَّةٌ أو ناقصةٌ لَا مَطلقُ العلَّةِ؛ ضَرورةً أنَّ اشتراك مطلقِ العَلَّةِ لَا تستلزم اشتراك المعلول. (قَوْلُهُ : كَمَا يُقَالُ : النَّبِيْذُ مُشكِرٌ ... إلخ) لو قالَ: النَّبيذُ حرام؛ لأَنَّهُ كَالحَمرِ في الإسكارِ الَّذي هو عِلَّةُ الحُرمةِ؛ لكان أخصرَ وَأَوْلَى، فإذا ردَّ إلى صورة القياس؛ صار هكذا: النَّبيذُ مُسكِرٌ كَالخمر، وكُلُّ مُشكرٍ حرام، فالنبيذُ حرام، فالجزئي الأوَّلُ أصغرُ، والثَّاني شبيه، والحكم أكبرُ، والمعنى المشترك أوسط، والمتكلمونَ يُسمونَ التَّمثيل استدلالاً بالشَّاهِدِ على الغائب والأصغر غائباً، والشَّبية شاهداً، والفقهاء يسمونه قياساً؛ لما فيه من حذوِ جزئي بجزئي وإلحاقه بِهِ، يُقالُ: قاسَ الشَّيء.
A chapter on induction and analogy. When it is reduced to the form of the syllogism, it becomes thus: 'The heaven is composed, and every composed thing is originated.' So if there is a defect, it occurs only in the major premise, unlike induction; for when it is reduced to the syllogism, the defect relates only to its minor premise, i.e., that this universal matter is confined to those particulars in which induction occurred, and it is called the first particular. (His statement: 'As for analogy... etc.') The author departed from the well-known definition, which is establishing a judgment for a particular because it is established for another particular due to a shared meaning, because it contains inaccuracy; for it is defining a thing by its effect. He said in the commentary on the Risala: The more correct is that it is the relation of a particular to a particular in a shared meaning. Some commentators objected that it also contains inaccuracy. He said: The apparent is that it should be said: It is composed of propositions that include the demonstration of the participation of a particular with another particular in the cause of the judgment, in order to establish that judgment in that particular. Rajab Efendi said: The people divided analogy into: conclusive analogy, which yields certainty, like our saying: 'The world is like a house in possibility, which is a cause of need, i.e., the efficient cause, so the world also needs an efficient cause'; and non-conclusive analogy, which yields probability, like: 'The world is like a house in composition, which is the cause of temporality, so the world is also temporal.' The apparent meaning of analogy in contrast to syllogism is the second; for the first reduces to syllogism definitively. Therefore, it is appropriate to mention in its definition a condition that excludes the first, because the mentioned participation is probable. End quote. What is in the Siwalkuti glosses is that analogy is only productive of certainty if the commonality is established, and the absence of the specific characteristic of the original being a condition or the specific characteristic of the branch being a prevention is known with certainty. But acquiring knowledge of these matters is very difficult; therefore, they did not divide analogy into what yields certainty and what yields probability as they divided induction. (His statement: 'For another particular... etc.') What is meant by 'particular' is not the relative particular to the shared meaning, but rather what the shared meaning encompasses, whether it is predicated or not, as said by Abd al-Hakim. End quote. (His statement: 'In the cause of the judgment' and what is meant by it is the cause that necessitates the original judgment, whether it is a complete or incomplete cause, not any cause; necessarily, because sharing in any cause does not necessitate sharing in the effect. (His statement: 'As it is said: Nabidh is intoxicating... etc.') If he had said: 'Nabidh is forbidden because it is like wine in intoxication, which is the cause of prohibition,' it would be more concise and better. When reduced to the form of the syllogism, it becomes: 'Nabidh is intoxicating like wine, and every intoxicant is forbidden, so nabidh is forbidden.' So the first particular is the minor, the second is the analogue, the judgment is the major, and the shared meaning is the middle. The mutakallimun call analogy 'inference from the witnessed to the absent,' where the minor is absent and the analogue is witnessed. The jurists call it qiyas because it involves a particular being modeled upon another and annexed to it. One says: 'He measured the thing.'
Source pages p. 863

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition and classification of tamthil (analogy). It begins by contrasting tamthil with istiqra' (induction) in terms of where defects appear when reduced to qiyas (syllogism). Then it critiques the common definition of tamthil as 'establishing a judgment for a particular because it is established for another particular due to a shared meaning,' noting that this defines the thing by its effect. It offers a better definition: 'a composition of propositions that demonstrate the participation of a particular with another particular in the cause of the judgment, in order to establish that judgment in that particular.' This definition avoids the previous inaccuracy. Next, it presents a classification of tamthil into conclusive (قطعي) and non-conclusive (غير قطعي). Conclusive analogy yields certainty, like the argument that the world is like a house in having possibility (imkan), which is the cause of needing an efficient cause. Non-conclusive analogy yields only probability, like the argument that the world is like a house in composition (ta'lif), which is the cause of being originated. The text notes that the first type actually reduces to a regular syllogism with certainty, so it is not truly tamthil in the sense used in logic; tamthil proper is the probable form. The gloss from al-Siyalkuti adds that for tamthil to yield certainty, one must establish the common cause and the absence of any special condition or prevention, but this is very difficult, hence analogy is not divided into certain and probable as induction is. The passage then clarifies what is meant by 'particular' in the definition: it is any thing that falls under the shared meaning, not the relative particular. It also specifies that the 'cause' is the cause that necessitates the original judgment, whether complete or incomplete, not any cause. Finally, it analyzes the example of khamr and nabidh: nabidh is forbidden because it shares the cause of intoxication with wine. When reduced to syllogism, the minor is nabidh, the major is the judgment of prohibition, and the middle is intoxication, which is the shared meaning. The mutakallimun call this 'inference from the witnessed to the absent' (istidlal bi al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib), while the fuqaha call it qiyas because it involves annexing a branch to an original based on a common cause.

Key Takeaway
Tamthil is structurally identical to a categorical syllogism; the shared cause serves as the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the tamthil argument: 'التراب ليس بنجس لأن الماء ليس بنجس في عدم الإحساس' (Soil is not impure because water is not impure in lacking sensation). Reduce it to a categorical syllogism, identifying the minor, major, and middle terms.
